Summary 0:
EU rules on marketing communications for collective investment undertakings (UCITS) and collective investment funds (AIFs)
EU policies on marketing Communications for UCITS and AIFs
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1286/2014 — marketing communications and notification procedures applicable to UCITS
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the marketing communications* and notifications* that collective investment firms* must send to investors.
It applies to:
alternative investment fund managers;
UCITS management companies, including any UCITS which has not designated a UCITS management company;
euVECA managers; and
EuSEF managers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to marketing communications:
to investors;
to the general public;
in the context of pre-marketing.
EU countries must:
ensure that marketing communications are not misleading;
notify the management company within 10 working days of receipt of marketing communications;
provide the marketing company with a copy of the relevant national law, regulations and administrative provisions.
The marketing communications must contain:
a description of the fund’s assets and liabilities;
a summary of the fees or charges that managers may charge to investors for the services they provide;
information on the risks and benefits of investing in the fund;
an estimate of the amount of money that investors should expect to invest.
In addition, the regulation requires:
the marketing communications to be presented in a manner that is fair, clear and not misleading, and
the information to be provided in a language that is customary in the sphere of international finance;
the communication to investors to be accompanied by a description of how the fund is managed and how it is managed.
If a marketing communication is not accompanied by an explanation of the risks or benefits that investors may expect, the marketing communication must be amended.
Before a marketing communications can be published, the manager of a qualifying venture capital fund must ensure that it is adequately documented and that it has been authorised by the competent authorities of the EU country in which it is published.
Any subscription by professional investors to units or shares of a qualified social entrepreneurship fund, or of an AIF established as a result of the marketing, must be considered to be the result of marketing and shall be subject to the applicable notification procedures referred to in Article 16.
Prior notification must be provided to the management companies of any request to amend its marketing communications.
Where a management company does not comply with this regulation, it must inform the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) of the reasons for its failure to comply with the regulation.
ESMA must develop draft implementing technical standards to determine the standard forms, templates and procedures for the publications and notifications under this regulation. It will submit those draft implementing standards to the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission by 2 February 2021.
Every second year, ESMA will submit a report to the EU countries on the implementation of this regulation and on the effects of national laws, regulations, administrative provisions governing marketing communications based also on the information received.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Investment Bank (EIB) is one of the largest investment firms in the world. It manages over €2 trillion in assets.
For more information, see:
‘UCITS marketing communications’ on the EIB's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1095/2010 of 27 October 2010 on marketing communication for collective investments in collective investment undertaking undertakings and UCITS (OJ L 183, 30.10.2010, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 1095 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 1:
EU rules on market surveillance of non-compliant and unsafe products
EU rules covering non-compliance with EU law
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 on the enforcement of EU rules on the application of Union law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for market surveillance* of products that are subject to EU law.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ market surveillance authorities are able to ensure the proper enforcement of these rules.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all products covered by the EU’s market surveillance rules. It does not apply to:
toys;
medical devices;
personal protective equipment;
electrical appliances;
telecommunications equipment.
EU countries must designate their own market surveillance authority and designate a single liaison office.
Market surveillance authorities must:
exercise their powers independently, impartially and without bias;
carry out their duties independently and impartially, without bias.
They must also cooperate with each other and cooperate with the European Commission.
The Commission must take appropriate enforcement measures when it becomes aware of a serious risk presented by a product that is imported from a non-EU country.
A European Union product compliance network (EUPCN) is set up to facilitate the exchange of information between EU countries on products subject to the regulation. It meets at regular intervals and, where necessary, at the reasoned request of the Commission or a Member State.
Cooperation between the Commission and the EU countries is encouraged.
Member States must draw up a national strategy for the implementation of the regulation by 16 July 2022. The strategy must include:
a description of the objectives of the surveillance authorities’ activities;
a list of the products that fall within the scope of this regulation;
the number of authorised representatives of the national surveillance authorities;
measures to be taken by the national authorities to ensure consistency in their activities.
In addition, the regulation sets up a European Union Product Compliance Network (UPCN), which meets regularly to exchange information on the compliance with the regulation and to assist in the cooperation between the authorities in different Member States.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
From 16 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the functioning of the market surveillance system, including:
the creation of a European Commission Directorate-General for the Protection of Consumers’ Rights and the Enforcement of Trade in Goods,
the establishment of the EUPCN, and
the setting-up of the European Electronic Trading System (ETS).
For more information, see:
EU market surveillance (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Market surveillance authority: a national authority responsible for monitoring the compliance of products with EU legislation.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 852/2018 of 16 June 2018 establishing the European Union Market Surveillance Authority (OJ L 183, 16.7.2018, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS

See consolidated version.

Summary 2:
Broadcasting organisations’ licensing of copyright and related rights for retransmission of their programmes in the EU (Directive 2018/13/EU on the licensing of the rights of broadcasting organisations for the transmission of television and radio programmes in other EU countries.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It aims to ensure that broadcasting organisations are able to transmit their programmes to the public and to make available to them their programmes when providing ancillary online services.
It sets out the rules for the licensing and authorisation of copyright, related rights and other rights in the works or other protected subject matter used in the programmes, for example the rights in phonograms or performances.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
the broadcasting organisations themselves;
collective management organisations (collective organisations) that manage the rights held by broadcasting organisations;
operators that distribute television or radio programmes to users;
the collective management organisations that manage retransmissions by cable of programmes from broadcasting organisations.
EU countries must ensure that:
all broadcasting organisations have the right to grant or refuse authorisation to a retransmitter of their programme to be transmitted in another EU country;
they have the obligation to exercise this right through a collective management organisation.
Collective management organisation must also ensure that the operator of the retransmitters’ licence fee is reasonable and adequate.
The operator of a retranmission service must also inform the European Commission of the terms and conditions under which it will apply for the authorisation.
Where the operator applies for authorisation, it must inform the Commission.
the Commission must assess the economic value of the licensing terms, including the value allocated to the means of transmission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLACE INTO FORCE?
From 7 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
Directive 2014/26/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 May 2014 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the broadcasting of audiovisual works.
For more information, see:
Radio and television licensing (European Commission).

Summary 3:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the.eu top-level domain (ccTLD) and the.EU Multistakeholder Advisory Group (MAAG)
Regulation(EU) 2018/852 on the establishment of a.eu Top Level Domain Registry
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down the rules for the establishment and operation of a registry for the.eU Top Level (Top Level Domain) in the European Union (EU).
It sets out the conditions for its implementation, including the designation and characteristics of the registry.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC) No 874/2004.
KEY POINTS
The.eu TLD is one of the largest country code TLDs in the world. It is complementary to existing ccTLDs and is open to the global registration of generic.eu domains.
The registry is a not-for-profit organisation. It has its registered office, central administration and principal place of business within the territory of the EU.
Eligible registrars
The Commission designates a registry that meets the following criteria:
must be based in an EU country;
be established in the EU;
have a registered office in the country in which it is located;
is established in a non-EU country; and
must have a minimum number of domain names in the top level.eu domain.
A.eu registrar may reserve or register domain names considered necessary for its operational functions under the contract referred to in Article 8(4) of the regulation.
Registration and revocation
The registrar is responsible for revoking domain names that have been used in bad faith.
Registry and registry disputes
The regulation sets out a dispute resolution procedure for disputes with regard to domain names.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation applies from 13 October 2022.

Summary 4:
The European Connectivity Fund (CEF) for the Digital Single Market
The European Cross-Border Connectivity Facility
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on the European Connecting Fund for the digital single market
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Commission’s Connecting Europe Facility (the CEF), a European Union (EU) instrument for the development of European digital connectivity.
The CEF aims to support actions in the field of digital connectivity to improve the free movement of persons, goods, capital and services between EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Eligible actions
The following actions are eligible:
legal entities established in:
a Member State, including joint ventures;
a third country associated to the CEF; or
an overseas country or territory;
international organisations, if provided for in the work programmes, international organisations.
Awards
The award criteria for the selection of actions are based on the following criteria:
cost-benefit analysis;
energy generation costs;
system integration costs; and
environmental and security costs.
Cooperation between the participating Member States
The work programmes are implemented through work programmes and calls for proposals. The CEF is funded by the general budget of the EU.
EU countries may also participate on the basis of other legal instruments.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2018.
It runs until 31 December 2023.
BACKGROUND
It is one of the main instruments of the European Union's (EU), the other being the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMF).
For more information, see:
European Connectivity fund (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Connected Europe Facility: an instrument designed to provide financial support to projects of common interest in the area of the trans-European digital connectivity (e.g. smart grids).
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 5:
Removal of terrorist content on the internet
Removalling terrorist content online
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 on the removal of terrorist material on the Internet
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that hosting service providers and users’ trust in the online environment are protected against the dissemination of harmful terrorist content.
It sets out rules to prevent and combat the misuse of hosting services by terrorist groups and their supporters to disseminate terrorist content in order to spread their message, to radicalise and recruit followers, and to facilitate and direct terrorist activity.
KEY POINTS
Hosting service providers must:
ensure that their services are not used to facilitate or direct terrorist activities;
take all necessary measures to prevent, detect and remove terrorist content from their services.
They must also ensure that their removal orders are effective, proportionate and non-discriminatory.
Removal orders are issued by the competent authority of the EU country in which the hosting service provider is located. They must be issued in the form of a removal order and must be executed within 3 months of receipt of the decision and on an annual basis thereafter.
A contact point must be set up to deal with any request for removal. This contact point should serve only for operational purposes.
The competent authority must also inform the host service provider of any interference with an investigative interest (deconfliction) or any reason for not complying with the removal order.
If the host provider cannot comply with the order on grounds of force majeure or de facto impossibility not attributable to the hosting services, it must, without undue delay, inform the authority that issued the order of those grounds, using the template set out in Annex III.
EU countries must ensure that information about the contact point is made publicly available.
In addition, the competent authorities must be able to take into account the number of users in the EU and the impact of its services in disseminating terrorist content, as well as the safeguards put in place to address the misuse.
Where a host provider fails to comply with an order, it may be subject to sanctions, such as:
a ban on hosting services;
a restriction of access to its services for the duration of the order;
suspension of its hosting services for a period of time;
restriction of its ability to host or host other services that facilitate or facilitate terrorist activity;
imposition of sanctions for infringements of the regulation’s specific provisions.
Exemptions
The regulation does not apply to the following services:
mailing services, including emails or private messaging services, as defined in point (5) of Article 2 of Directive (EEC) 2018/1972 of the European Parliament and of the Council (see summary);
internet hosting services (e.g. social media hosting services);
online storage services (i.e. hosting services that hold data in the memory of a physical or virtual server);
web hosting services which store and host user-generated content;
social networking services (including hosting services).
The European Commission is empowered to adopt delegated acts to supplement this regulation with technical requirements for the electronic means to be used by competent authorities for the transmission of removal orders.
Penalties
The Commission may:
impose specific penalties for hosting services providers that fail to implement this regulation;
apply sanctions to host service providers that do not comply with its specific provisions;
introduce specific measures to address infringements.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat the spread of harmful online content. These include:
Directive (EC) No 596/2004 on the fight against the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDs);
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 852/2004 and (EC), No 853/2004 (see consolidated version).
For more information, see:
‘The fight against terrorism’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027
Framework programme for research and innovation 2021-2020
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on the European Union’s framework programme for Research & Innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing the EU budget for Horizon Europe, the EU's main research and development (R&I) programme.
It aims to increase the EU’S investment in R&I through Horizon Europe.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The European Union (EU) is investing €8.5 billion in Horizon Europe for the period 2021–2027.
The programme is divided into 4 main objectives:
research and innovation (R & I):
science, technology, economic, environmental and societal impact (S&S);
environmental impact (e.g. climate-neutral and smart cities);
smart specialisation (i.e. smart specialisation).
Programme objectives
The main objectives of the programme are to:
promote research and technological innovation in the fields of science, technology and environmental impact (SSI);
contribute to the sustainable development of the EU economy and society;
improve the quality of life of people and the environment;
encourage the sharing of knowledge and skills between EU countries and regions;
support the development of innovative technologies and services.
Funding and transfers of resources
The EU countries’ contributions to Horizon Europe are covered by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF and the European Social Fund for Innovation (SIFIDF), the European Investment Bank (SIB) and other financial instruments. The European Commission is responsible for administering the programme and for its implementation.
Procurement
EU countries must provide the European Commission with the necessary financial resources to finance the programme. The Commission, in consultation with the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions, may also provide financial support to the European Research Council (JRC).
The Commission may also use the European Innovation Mechanism (EIM) to provide the EU countries with the financial resources they need to implement their programmes. The EIM contributes to the overall budget of Horizon Europe 2020–2025.
In addition, the Commission may use the EIM to support the activities of the Joint Research Centre for Innovation, which supports research, innovation and technological development in the areas of climate change, smart cities, energy efficiency, mobility and sustainable development.
Monitoring and reporting
The Programme is monitored and monitored by the Commission, which is assisted by independent observers in the evaluation process, where applicable. It also publishes the results of its evaluations and the conclusions of its observations.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The framework programme is part of the general EU budget.
For more information, see:
Horizon Europe (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
                  
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, of 18 July 2018, on the Framework Programme for research & innovation (OJ L 183, 1.8.2019, pp. 1–50)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom, Euratom) 2018–1046 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 7:
European Union satellite navigation and Earth observation programme (EU SST)
European Union satellites navigation and earth observation programme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1139 on the European Union Satellite Navigation and Earth Observation Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s European Union Space Agency (EUSATCOM).
It sets out the rules for the development and operation of the EU SST (European Union Navigation Satellite System).
KEY POINTS
The European Union SST is a multiannual programme designed to:
ensure that the EU has a leading position in the global satellite navigation market;
contribute to the development of the global Earth observation system of systems (GEO) and the Copernicus satellite navigation system (Copernicus);
provide a high level of interoperability, interoperability and standardisation in the areas of navigation, Earth observation and satellite communications;
ensuring the security of the systems and their services against cyber-attacks.
The EUSST is funded by the EU budget and is implemented by the European Commission. It is managed by the Agency for Space Technology (ASAT).
The Agency is responsible for:
developing and operating the EU-wide SST services;
providing technical assistance to the participating national authorities;
coordinating the development, operation and management of the SST.
It is assisted by the Security Accreditation Board, which is composed of experts from the European Space Agency and the European Centre for the Protection of Civil Aviation Safety (ECAS).
.
Its tasks include:
the design, development and implementation of the European satellite navigation systems (SST) and satellite communication services (GOVSAT) for the EU;
monitoring the performance of the system and its users;
designing and implementing the GOVATCOM Hub;
assisting the Commission in the implementation of GOVSatCOM activities;
advising the Commission and EU countries on the security and operational aspects of the programme.
In addition, the Agency:
provides the Commission with technical assistance in the field of SST;
covers the cost-reimbursement of contracts for the provision of services and technical assistance;
is responsible for the coordination of the activities of the contracting authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The SST programme was established under Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 and its subsequent amendments.
For more information, see:
EU Satellites (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1139/2018 of 7 January 2019 establishing the Union Satellits and Earth Observations Programme (OJ L 183, 8.2.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2018-1139 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 8:
EU programme for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027 on the development of high-performance computing and artificial intelligence (HPC) and cybersecurity and trust (AI) and advanced digital skills (MFF 2021-2027)
EU programme to develop high-performing computing and cybersecurity (AI and trust)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2020/2093 establishing the European Union (EU) programme for a period of 7 years from 1 July 2021 to 1 January 2027
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s High Performance Computing and Artificial Intelligence (AI & Trust) programme.
It sets out the objectives of the programme, which is funded by the multiannual financial framework laid down in Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, for its period from 2021 to 2027.
KEY POINTS
The programme has 5 interrelated specific objectives:
Specific Objective 1:
High Performance Computing
Artificial intelligence
Cybersecurity and Trust
advanced digital skills.
EU countries are encouraged to take the necessary steps to develop and implement innovative solutions to:
ensure the security and trust of the Union;
improve the quality of life of EU citizens;
enhance the competitiveness of the European digital economy;
develop innovative products and services.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It is assisted by the European Digital Innovation Hub (EDI Hub).
The EU countries are responsible for selecting an additional EDI Hub in each year. The programme also supports the establishment of a European digital innovation centre in each EU country.

Summary 9:
EU SMEs’s internal market governance programme (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/826 establishing the EU SMEs' Internal Market Governance Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU)-wide programme to improve the quality, visibility, transparency and reliability of EU statistics on the internal market.
KEY POINTS
SMEs are the engine of the EU economy. They contribute to growth, competitiveness and employment and it should continue benefitting all citizens and businesses equally.
The programme aims to:
improve the quality and transparency of EU internal market statistics;
promote fair competition and a level playing field for businesses, especially SMEs, and consumers;
ensure that EU countries’ official controls are being implemented, complied with and enforced, including in respect of imports;
support SMEs to team up with SMEs from non-EU countries;
facilitate the exchange of information and best practice between EU countries and the European Commission;
provide technical assistance and training to small and medium-sized enterprises (SME) in the field of trade and trade promotion;
contribute to the development of EU-wide information and communication technology (ICT) networks;
encourage SMEs and consumers to take advantage of the opportunities offered by the European standardisation of EU products and services;
increase the visibility and visibility of EU rules on trade in goods and services.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme include:
improving the quality of EU market surveillance authorities’ statistical systems;
establishing an EU-based system for trade surveillance authorities;
raising awareness of the importance of trade in agricultural products;
enhancing the role of SMEs in the EU market;
strengthening the capacity-building of European SMEs;
increasing the visibility, visibility and transparency in EU trade statistics.
Cooperation between the Commission and non-profit-making entities
EU countries must:
support the activities of the European SME Envoy Network;
cooperate with non-governmental organisations;
coordinate the actions of EU countries in the area of veterinary and phytosanitary emergency measures;
take the necessary measures to protect human health and the environment;
assist the Commission in implementing the programme.
Financing
The European Commission must adopt implementing acts establishing lower co-financing rates. The Commission may, if necessary, amend these to take account of technological developments in the fields of digitalisation, artificial intelligence, algorithms, big data, cybersecurity and forensic technology.
Eligible entities
The Commission may designate one or more entities as the sole beneficiaries. That entity may be:
a private body or a public body; or
a public body.
It may also designate two or more organisations as sole beneficiaries:
the European Commission or the European Parliament or the Council;
the EU countries or the Commission itself.
In addition, the Commission may grant grants to the following entities:
an EU country or the EU countries, the European Economic and Social Committee or the Committee of the Regions;
a non-government organisation;
an international organisation or the United Nations Economic Commission for Europe.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 31 August 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
 
The EU has a long-term strategy to modernise and modernise its internal market, which aims to improve its quality and reliability. It is based on a number of objectives, including:
promoting fair competition, including at global level, and encouraging businesses to reap the benefits of the internal markets;
reducing barriers to trade and improving the transparency and quality of the information available to consumers.
For more information, see:
EU SME Governance programme (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Official market surveillance: a system of official controls carried out by EU countries to verify and monitor that relevant EU requirements are being complied with.
* Trade promotion: the system of trade promotion, in which EU countries organise trade fairs and market surveillance activities.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 10:
EU rules on the acquisition, possession and transfer of firearms (codified version) (SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/65/EU on the licensing of firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It lays down rules on licensing and the transfer of arms and ammunition.
It aims to ensure that the acquisition and possession of firearms is governed by a single administrative procedure.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that firearms are only made available for civilian use and that they are accompanied by a licence;
issue a licence for each type of firearm and its essential components.
The directive applies to all types of firearms, including:
firearms intended for use by the armed forces;
weapons of war;
replicas of antique weapons;
recreational firearms;
military missiles and launchers;
firearm reproductions;
explosives and launchers.
Licences must be issued by the competent national authority of each EU country.
National authorities must ensure that:
the holder of a licence bears the unique marking, as provided for under paragraph 1, permitting identification of the transferring entity;
the transfer of a firearm or its essential component from government stocks to permanent civilian use is carried out in accordance with the law of the EU country in which it is being transferred;
transfer of firearms from one EU country to another is authorised by the authority of that country without the prior authorisation of the country in question.
In addition, EU countries may grant dealers the right to transfer firearms from their territory to a dealer established in another EU country without prior authorisations.
Dealers must inform each other and the European Commission of the national authorities responsible for transmitting and receiving information and for complying with the obligations set out in Article 16(4) of the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Union (EU) has adopted a number of measures to protect the security of its citizens. These include:
rules on the registration of firearms and their essential components;
measures to prevent the illegal trade in firearms.
For more information, see:
Firearms (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Firearm: a firearm designed and manufactured to fire a shot, bullet or projectile by the action of a combustible propellant.
* Ammunition: a substance that can be fired by means of a fire extinguisher.

Summary 11:
Maritime transport: EU-wide reporting of customs and transit obligations
Maritime Transport: EU–wide reporting on customs and transport obligations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 on the EU’s maritime transport data set (EMSWe)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the efficiency of the maritime transport sector and to reduce administrative burdens.
It sets up a single EU data set, known as the Maritime Information System for Trade and Transport Locations (EMW), to facilitate the exchange of information between EU countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must establish a maritime single window with one or more other EU countries to report on:
customs status of goods;
departure formalities;
electronic transport documents used for transit;
exit notification;
ex-export notification.
Each EU country must set up a maritime national single window for each of the following categories of reporting obligations:
exports;
transport formalities.
The European Commission must establish an electronic system for each category of reporting obligation. The electronic system must:
identify those data elements that are necessary for the application of those reporting obligations;
provide a common user registry and access management system;
ensure that each EU country has a single user registration by means of an existing Union registry with EU level recognition, federated user management and Union level user monitoring.
EU Member States must also establish a national single portal for each type of vessel.
National single windows must have a uniform internet address.
Member States must ensure that the national single windows are available to the public and that they wish to use for the reporting of their maritime transport obligations.
Reporting obligations must be reported to the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) and the European Commission. Member States may also request the Commission to amend the data set on the basis of the reporting obligations contained in their national legislation and requirements. The Commission must justify its decision in writing to the Member States.
In addition, Member States are required to:
report to the Commission on the number and type of vessels that call at their ports;
report on the type and number of persons on board their ships;
assist the Commission with the collection and processing of data;
set up and maintain a database of all the information that ships must report to the EMSWe data set.
Data protection and confidentiality
The Commission must ensure the protection of personal data and the confidentiality of the information it collects. The data collected must be stored in a secure database and must not be used for any purpose other than the purpose for which it was originally intended.
Implementing acts
Each Member State must adopt an implementing act to implement the regulation by 15 August 2021. This implementing act must include:
the name and contact details of the port where the vessel is registered;
a description of the vessel and its port of registration;
the number of vessels registered in that port and the port of registry;
details of the number of passengers and crew;
information on the port and port facilities used.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation applies from 15 August 2019. It repeals Directive 2010/65/EU.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of implementing acts to modernise and streamline the EU maritime transport system, including:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1725 on the harmonisation of the EU Maritime Single Window;
Directive 2014/40/EU on the interoperability of the electronic systems for the transmission of maritime information between Member States (see summary).
The main objectives of these implementing acts are:
to:
improve the efficiency and effectiveness of maritime transport;
reduce the administrative burden on Member States and the Commission;
to improve the quality of the data they collect; and
to reduce the administrative burdens on the Commission.
For more information, see:
EU maritime transport (European Commission).

Summary 12:
Electronic road toll systems (ETS) — rules and obligations of toll chargers and EETS providers (REFERENCES
Directive (EU) 2019/849 on the interoperability of electronic road tolls (ETES)
DirectIVE (EU, Euratom) No 552/2013 on the rules and responsibilities of toll-charging authorities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s electronic toll system (ETS) is interoperable.
It recasts Directive 2004/52/EC, which had to become law in the EU countries by 19 October 2011.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all vehicles registered in EU countries. It does not apply to scrap vehicles, stolen vehicles or number plates, or outdated vehicle registration.
EETS providers must:
ensure that they have an EETS domain on their territory;
provide toll-chargers with a statement setting out the general conditions for accessing their EETS domains, in accordance with the implementing acts referred to in paragraph 9 of the directive;
establish and maintain a contact office in each EU country.
Each EU country must ensure that any toll charger responsible for a toll charger domain on its territory develops and maintains a statement on how it intends to use EETS.
Toll chargers are responsible for:
establishing, maintaining and maintaining a register of EETS-registered vehicles;
ensuring that the EETS service providers have access to their databases;
registering and managing EETS services.
providing information to the toll charger on the failure to pay a road fee.
collecting information on the non-payment of a road toll.
checking whether a vehicle is registered in an EU country has been detected for non-compliance with the road toll and sending an information letter to the responsible body.
Information letter
The information letter must contain the following information:
the name and address of the contact office;
the vehicle registration number and make and model;
a description of the vehicle and its registration certificate.
The contact office may be a natural person or a public or private body. It must be located in the territory of the EU country in which the vehicle with registration was detected.
EU countries must ensure the following:
that the information letter contains:
a list of the toll-paying bodies responsible for EETS;
information on the number of vehicles with registration in the country in whose territory the vehicle was detected;
details of the number and model of the vehicles detected by the responsible bodies.
In addition, the directive sets out the responsibilities of the national authorities responsible for the registration of vehicles.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 19 October 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Directive is one of a series of measures designed to improve interoperability between EU countries’ EETS systems. It complements Regulation (EC) No 182/2011 on the setting up of the Electronic Toll Committee.
For more information, see:
‘EETS interoperability’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Electronic road toll system: a system that allows users to pay tolls for the use of a single payment method, such as a toll road.
ACT
Council Directive (EU), Euratom of 19 September 2019 on interoperable electronic toll systems in the European Union (OJ L 183, 19.10.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 13:
European network of immigration liaison officers
European network on migration
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — European network of liaison officers for the implementation of the Schengen acquis
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU), the Commission, the European Border and Coast Guard Agency, Europol and the European Agency for Fundamental Rights (EASO) can more systematically coordinate the tasks and roles of their liaison officers deployed in third countries.
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 377/2004.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, the Commission and EU agencies. It does not apply to non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Norway, Liechtenstein or Switzerland.
EU countries may decide to deploy their own liaison officers to third countries on the basis of their competence and training. They may also agree to share certain tasks among each other, depending on the competence of their officers.
Joint deployment of officers by the Commission
The Commission may deploy liaison officers from its headquarters in the EU to countries other than those of the EU. These officers are responsible for:
facilitating and supporting the networks provided for in the regulation’s annexes;
cooperating with local or regional cooperation networks and cooperating, where and when appropriate, with liaison officers of countries other countries other that are not EU countries;
supporting the work of the Steering Board of the European Asylum and Migration Agency (EMA).
The Steering board is composed of one representative from each EU country and one representative of the Commission. It is assisted by a secretariat.
The European Commission is represented by a representative from the EASO.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 20 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the integration of migrants into its internal market. These include:
the creation of a European Migration Agency;
the introduction of a common approach to migration policy.
In addition to this, the regulation establishes a European Network of Immigration Liaison Officers (ENOLA) which aims to:
support the development, implementation and maximise the impact of EU action on migration;
facilitate and support the networks set up by EU countries and the Commission;
contribute to the fight against smuggling of migrants and trafficking in human beings.
For more information, see:
European Network of Migrants (European Commission).

Summary 14:
Security standards for identity cards and residence cards
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 on the security standards applicable to identity cards issued by EU countries to their nationals and to residence documents issued by Member States to EU citizens and their family members when exercising their right to free movement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes minimum security and format standards for the security of identity cards* and residence documents* for EU citizens.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
identity cards are issued in a secure and interoperable format;
they contain a facial image of the holder of the card and two fingerprints in interoperable digital formats;
the biometric identifiers are collected solely by qualified and duly authorised staff designated by the authorities responsible for issuing identity cards or residence cards, for the purpose of being integrated into the highly secure storage medium provided for in Article 3(5) of the regulation and in Article 7(1) for residence cards.
National identity cards must have a minimum validity of 5 years and a maximum period of validity of 10 years. The card must contain the two-letter country code of the EU country issuing the card, printed in negative in a blue rectangle and encircled by 12 yellow stars.
The holder of an identity card must provide a fingerprint and facial image for verification purposes. Children under the age of 12 years may be exempt from the requirement to give fingerprints.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that the identity cardholders’ fingerprints are stored in a high-security storage medium and that they are erased or destroyed at the end of their 10-year validity;
notify the competent national authority if they believe that their fingerprints have been falsified;
use only authorised staff and only when the document is required to be produced by law;
verify the authenticity of the document by means of a facial or fingerprint image or a facial photograph.
Collection and storage of biometric data must be carried out only by authorised staff.
Where necessary, national authorities may cooperate with an external service provider. However, they should exercise particular caution when cooperating with the external service providers.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The regulation applies from 3 August 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has introduced a number of measures to protect the right of EU citizens to move and reside freely within the EU. These include:
the introduction of minimum security standards for national identity cards;
introduction of a European Union-wide system for the authentication of residence documents.
For more information, see:
EU identity cards (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* National identity card: a card issued by an EU country to its nationals or to its family members.
* Residence document: a document issued by a non-EU country to the family members of an EU citizen when exercising its right to live and work in the territory of that country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 849/2019 of 5 September 2019 establishing minimum security requirements for the identity cards, residence documents and registration certificates to be issued in the Member States pursuant to Directive 2004/38/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 29 April 2004 (OJ L 183, 10.10.2019, pp. 1-16)

Summary 15:
Interoperability between EU information systems for security, border and migration management (VIS) and the Schengen Information System (VIS), and the common identity repository (CIR)
Interoperation between EU Information Systems for Security, Border and Migration Management (VIS, CIR, ESP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1860 on interoperability between the information systems of the European Union concerning the Visa Information System and the exchange of data between Member States on short-stay visas
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the interoperability of the EU’s internal information systems (VIS and CIR) for the purposes of border and immigration management.
It sets out rules for the sharing of biometric and travel document data between EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the following:
a common EU information system (VIS);
a single identity repository for identification;
a shared European Union information system for security and border management (ESP);
an interoperable European Union Information System for security (eu-LISA).
The European Commission is responsible for developing the shared BMS and for its technical management. It is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of the Member States.
The CIR contains the data relating to persons or travel documents held in Europol data or in the Interpol databases. The European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Europol) has access to the data stored in the CIR for the purpose of combating identity fraud.
Each Member State must keep logs of queries that its duly authorised staff make. The CIR must reply in such a way that the security of the data is not compromised.
Where a query results in a red link in accordance with Article 32 of the regulation, the authorities referred to in Article 26(2) must have access, solely for the reasons of combating fraud, to the information stored in CIR.
A common European Union identity database (EUR-ID) is set up. It contains the following data:
travel documents;
travel document images;
fingerprints;
biometric data;
visas;
passports;
vehicle registration numbers.
In addition, the European Commission must develop a practical handbook to provide technical and operational guidelines, recommendations and best practices for the use of the interoperable components of the BMS. The Commission must adopt this handbook within 30 days of adoption of the implementing act.
Eur-ID is a centralised database for the identification of persons and travel documents. It can be accessed by Europol, Interpol, Eurojust and the European Border Guard Agency. It also contains the information on the number of persons who have entered the EU and their travel documents, as well as their travel dates and travel destinations.
An interoperability component of the ESP contains the biometric templates, which it must obtain from the following biometric data. These are:
fingerprint images of the holder of the travel document;
card data of the bearer of a travel document.
When a user enters the ESP, a link indicating that it is the same person should be established. If, however, this link is not established, the user must provide a description of the identity of the person who entered the ESP and the reason for the query. This information is then used to identify the person concerned.
If the user does not provide the information required for identification, the ESP will only be able to perform a query using the information contained in the passport or travel document, which is stored in a separate database (e.g. Europol or Interpol).
In order to prevent identity fraud, the EU countries must ensure that the authorities have access to a common identity database for identification. This database will contain the following information:
the number of travel documents and travel dates;
the date of entry into the EU, the date of departure from the Member State and the country where the person entered the Union and the date when the person left the Union.
Security incidents must be managed so as to ensure a quick, effective and proper response.
Non-discrimination and fundamental rights
Processing of personal data must not result in discrimination against persons on any grounds such as gender, race, colour, ethnic or social origin, genetic features, language, religion or belief, political or any other opinion, membership of a national minority, property, birth, disability, age or sexual orientation.
Member States must ensure the protection of the rights of children, the elderly, persons with a disability and persons in need of international protection.
National authorities must apply appropriate rules of professional secrecy or other equivalent duties of confidentiality to their staff required to work with data stored on the interoperality components.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 11 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been implementing a series of measures to improve interoperability in the area of visa and travel



Summary 16:
Greenhouse gas emissions from renewable energy
Greenhouse gaseous fuels and biofuels and bioliquids and biomass fuels — greening the EU’s energy market
Greenhouses gas emissions — greenening the EU's energy markets
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/865 on the greening of the energy market (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from the production, transport, storage and conversion of renewable energy.
It recasts and repeals Directive 2009/28/EC.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that renewable energy is available to the public and the private sector at affordable prices;
promote the use of renewable heating and cooling systems and equipment that achieve a significant reduction in energy consumption;
encourage cooperation between renewable energy producers and consumers.
The directive applies to all sectors of the economy, including transport, energy production, storage, distribution and distribution. It does not apply to:
biofuels;
biodiesel;
biomass fuels;
energy storage and distribution;
electrical equipment;
gas.
In addition, the directive sets out rules on renewable energy in the transport sector, including:
energy efficiency;
the use of energy from renewable sources;
carbon capture and storage (e.g. photovoltaics, geothermal energy, heat pumps enabling the generation of electricity, heat and cooling, and heat pumps;
electricity generated from geothermal and thermal energy;
renewable energy from biomass fuels; and
energy from wind turbines.
EU Member States must ensure that:
there is a clear distinction between individual renewables self-consumers and jointly acting renewables consumers;
there are separate rules for wind, solar and geothermal installations.
Member States must also ensure that the renewable energy communities can participate in available support schemes on an equal footing with large participants.
There are also rules on:
participation of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in renewable energy markets;
participating in the EU-wide renewable energy market;
cooperation between SMEs and the public authorities;
sharing of information on the availability of renewable resources;
support for the development and operation of renewable power generation facilities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 July 2019. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2020.
BACKGROUND
The EU is experiencing a significant increase in demand for energy. Part of that increase is likely to be met through an increase in the amount of land devoted to agriculture.
For more information, see:
‘Green energy’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 17:
Radio spectrum policy: interoperability and interoperability between the EU’s public safety answering points and emergency services (PSAPs)
Radio Spectrum Policy
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/852 — interoperability of the radio spectrum
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve interoperability in the radio communications sector.
It recasts and repeals Directives 2014/30/EU, 2014/35/EU and 2014/53/EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
radio spectrum (i.e. the frequencies between 3.5 GHz and 66 GHz) for the transmission and reception of radio signals;
radio-frequency identification (RFID) technologies and services (e.g. mobile phones, internet-connected cars, smart phones, etc.).
It does not apply to the use of radio spectrum for other purposes, such as:
electronic communications;
information and communication technologies;
telecommunications and information services;
mobile phones;
vehicle safety;
emergency services.
EU countries must ensure that:
Radio spectrum is used only when general interest objectives are at stake and should be clearly justified and subject to regular review.
The European Commission must adopt multiannual radio spectrum policy programmes, where appropriate.
National regulatory authorities must cooperate with each other where the relevant market is found to be transnational.
Regulation (EU countries have to ensure that the European Commission publishes a report on the implementation of the directive every 2 years. The report every 5 years on the progress made in implementing the directive. The Commission must also publish a report every 3 years on how the directive has been implemented.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The radio spectrum is one of the most important elements of the European Union (EU). It is used to transmit and receive radio signals and is essential for the communication of information and communication services. It is also used for emergency services and for navigation.
For more information, see:
EU radio spectrum (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Radio frequency identification (RADIO: the ability of a radio system to identify a person, object or object in danger.

Summary 18:
Monitoring and reporting of CO2 emissions and fuel consumption values for new heavy-duty vehicles
Monitoring, reporting and collection of data on greenhouse gas emissions and fuels
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/849 on the monitoring and reporting by manufacturers of heavy duty vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the EU’s system for measuring and reporting greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions, fuel consumption and energy efficiency values for each new light-duty vehicle.
It aims to reduce GHG emissions and energy consumption by 40 % and 30 % respectively by 2030 compared to 2005.
KEY POINTS
The regulation requires manufacturers to:
monitor and report to the European Commission the CO2 (carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions* and the fuel consumption (Emissions) values determined for new light duty vehicles;
report these data to the Commission in a publicly accessible manner and free of charge;
ensure the public has access to the information they need to make informed decisions on the uptake of more energy-efficient vehicles.
The Commission may impose an administrative fine where it finds that the data reported by the manufacturer deviates from the data recorded in the framework of Regulation (EC) No 595/2009 and, in particular, in accordance with Directive 2009/125/EC (see summary).
The European Environment Agency (EEA) is responsible for managing the EU ETS Register of Heavy-Duty Vehicles on behalf of the Commission. The Register is publicly available with the exception of data entry (a) specified in Part A of Annex I and data entry, and data entries 1, 24, 25, 32, 33, 39 and 40 specified in point 2 of Part B of the Annex I.
EU Member States must notify the Commission of any changes in the serial arrangement and serial management arrangements for the reporting of the data, and notify the European Parliament and the Council of the EU Member States of any such changes.
Reporting
The data reported must be made available to the public in an easily accessible manner. The Commission may, by means of implementing acts, determine the verification and correction measures referred to in paragraphs 2 and 3 of this regulation.
Where a manufacturer fails to report the required data within the applicable deadline, it may impose administrative fines. The amounts of the administrative fines will be considered as revenue for the general budget of the Union.
In addition, the Commission may:
impose penalties on the manufacturer if the data it receives from the manufacturer do not conform with the data set out in the regulation;
require the manufacturer to provide the Commission with a report on the number of vehicles registered in each Member State and the total number of units registered in that Member State;
implement a system for the collection and transmission of data for each type of vehicle. The data must be publicly available in a range format.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation applies from 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to tackle climate change, including:
Implementing Decision (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 on the setting up of a European Union Emissions Trading System (EU ETS) for light commercial vehicles and repealing Decision No 280/2004/EC;
Delegated Regulation (Euratom) 2017/2400 on the establishment of the European Union Electronic Vehicle Register (EVR);
Commission Implementing Decision No 582/2011 on the harmonisation of technical specifications for electronic transmission systems for heavy duty trucks and buses;
Directive 2009/126/EC on the registration of light commercial buses and light commercial vans;
Commission Regulation (EU), Euratom and EEA Implementing Decisions No 596/2009, No 597/2009/EC and No 598/2009 on the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
For more information, see:
European Union ETS (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* CO2 emission: the total amount of greenhouse gases emitted by a vehicle in a given year.
*Fuel consumption: the amount of energy used to transport a given volume of goods or passengers.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (UE) No 749/2019 of 21 December 2019 establishing a framework for the monitoring, reporting, collection and collation of greenhouse gas emission values and energy-efficiency values for the new light and medium duty vehicles (OJ L 127, 1.1.2019, pp. 1-8)

Summary 19:
Security of gas supply: EU countries’ national emergency plans and emergency plans (Regulation (EU) No 994/2010 — security of the EU’s natural gas supply (SGE)
Security of the European Union natural gas market: regulation (EU).
SGE
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It lays down rules on the security of EU gas supply.
It aims to ensure that the EU gas market is secure.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the responsibilities of national authorities and the European Commission.
National authorities must:
conduct a comprehensive national risk assessment and assess natural, technological, commercial, financial, social, political and market-related risks, andany other relevant ones;
identify the relevant risk factors for each EU country;
prepare a national emergency plan and an emergency response plan;
set up a risk management system;
ensure that the national emergency response plans and the emergency plans are updated regularly and published.
The European Commission adopts a risk assessment report every 2 years.
Each EU country must notify the Commission of any national emergency.
EU countries must also notify the European Gas Guarantee Group (EGG) and the Gas Coordination Group (GCG). The GCG adopts its rules of procedure.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
the competent authorities, in particular through their national authorities, conduct risk assessments and assess the relevant risks;
the European Commission, through its technical experts, adopts risk assessment reports and risk management plans;
adopts risk assessments;
lists the risk factors that are likely to lead to a disruption of the supply of gas;
coordinates the measures to be taken in the event of a disruption;
inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the measures taken in case of a crisis;
draw up and implement emergency plans to deal with any disruption of gas supplies;
provide the Commission with a list of the gas supply risk groups.
A national emergency notification must be sent to the Commission within 4 months of receiving a notification. It must include:
a description of the emergency situation;
a list of gas delivery information, such as minimum and maximum gas volumes, delivery points or conditions for the suspension of gas deliveries;
an estimate of the amount of gas needed to meet the demand;
details of the number of gas storage and distribution points in the EU.
If the Commission considers that an emergency is likely to occur, it must take the necessary measures to ensure the safety of the internal gas market, including:
implementing the emergency response measures;
establishing a network of emergency response points;
developing and implementing an emergency management system.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The SGE has been amended several times, most recently in 2018.
For more information, see:
EU gas security (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Natural gas: natural gas supplied by a natural gas company.
* Gas security: the ability of a gas company to provide uninterrupted gas supply throughout the EU without interruption of its supply. It is the responsibility of the company to ensure its security of supply and to provide the EU with reliable, uninterrupted and safe gas.
ACT
 
  барганска грабанда правлана: гарка (гродна) (OJ L 127, 1.4.2017, pp. 1-2)

Summary 20:
Energy labelling of electrical lamps and luminaires
Energy labels and information sheets
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on energy labelling
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the labelling and product information sheets of energy-related products.
It aims to promote energy efficiency, climate mitigation and environmental protection.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
each unit of a product is accompanied by a label in paper form complying with the relevant delegated act;
each product information sheet is supplemented by a product informationheet.
The regulation applies to:
electrical lamps and light bulbs;
luminaries;
electromagnetic lamps;
light bulbs.
Labels must comply with:
the relevant delegated acts;
the European Commission’s delegated acts adopted in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU);
the rules laid down in Directive 2010/30/EU and Directive 96/60/EC on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the sale and putting into service of electrical appliances.
Where a supplier places a product on the market or puts into service, it must provide the dealer with a label complying with these delegated acts. The dealer must not display the rescaled labels before that date.
If a dealer does not have a label, it may request one from the supplier in order to check that the product complies with the delegated act.
the dealer must provide a product-specific label and information sheet.
In addition, the dealer must keep a record of changes to the label and the information sheet for market surveillance purposes, keeping track of the dates of any editing.
National authorities must take the necessary measures to deal with products presenting a risk.
Penalties for infringements of the provisions of the regulation are effective, proportionate and dissuasive. The Commission may also adopt delegated acts to impose penalties on suppliers and manufacturers.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.

Summary 21:
Protecting the rights of natural persons in the processing of personal data
Protection of the right to personal data in the European Union
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/865 on the protection of the rights and obligations of the data subject
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the rights, obligations and responsibilities of the natural persons concerned are respected when processing their personal data.
It lays down rules on the processing, storage and use of these personal data by companies and public authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
personal data collected by the EU’s national authorities, controllers, processors or other recipients in the EU;
data collected by EU countries and international organisations;
the processing and storage of such data by the European Commission;
processing and storage by the Commission.
Rights of the individual concerned
The data subject has the following rights:
the right to be informed and to have their rights respected;
to have their data processed only for the purpose for which they have given their consent; and
to be able to withdraw their consent as soon as possible.
Right to erasure (‘right to be forgotten’)
The rights of data subjects must be respected in all cases, including:
when processing personal data;
when the processing is carried out in a non-EU country;
in cases where personal data are transferred from the EU to a third country or international organisation;
where they are used for the purposes of a specific to a specific purpose.
in relation to an EU country or to which they belong.
In addition, data subjects have the right of access to the information they have been provided.
Consultation and consent
Before processing a personal data, the user must be given the opportunity to give their consent. This must be done in writing, or by other means, including, where appropriate, by electronic means. It must be as easy as possible to withdraw as to give consent.
If the user does not give his or her consent, the processing must be terminated.
The user must also be informed of the reasons for the decision-making process and the consequences of the decision.
When processing, the controller must:
ensure that only personal data which are necessary for each specific purpose of the processing are processed;
provide the user with the necessary technical and organisational measures for ensuring that, by default, only the personal data that are necessary to meet the specific needs of that specific purpose are processed.
Disclosure of processing activities
The controller must inform the user of all processing activities, including the names and contact details of the person responsible for processing the data. The user must have access to all the information necessary for the processing to be carried out. The data subject must be informed in writing of the information that is necessary to carry out the processing. The information must be provided in writing or in electronic form, including by means of a pseudonymisation, in accordance with EU or EU country law.
EU countries must ensure that:
data subjects receive full and effective compensation for the damage they have suffered.
Data subjects who have been the subject of a breach of their rights must be compensated for any damage caused by their own staff. The EU country in whose territory the damage was caused must make good such damage under the conditions applicable to damage caused in its own territory.
Where a data subject is the object of an infringement of their right to privacy, the EU country concerned must compensate them for the loss of their privacy.
Commission Implementing Regulation (EC) No 865/2016
The Commission has adopted implementing rules for micro, small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) which have a turnover of less than €500,000. The regulation sets out the rules for the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 14 April 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 14 April 2017.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Personal data: a set of data on a person, including their name, address, gender, age, nationality, place of residence, gender identity, gender expression, sexual orientation, age of birth, ethnic origin, gender of the user, and any other characteristic of that person.
* Processing: the process of collecting, storing, processing, storing and storing personal data for a particular purpose, such as to identify, analyse, analyse and analyse the results of, or to make decisions on, the use of that data, in order to make a product or service available to the consumer.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1888/2008 of 14 April 2008 on the Protection of the Rights and Responsibilities of the Data Subject (Codified version) (OJ L 127, 15.5.2008, pp. 1

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

exchange standards and revisions.

special indices
treatment of products in specific areas.
Deadlines, exchange standards, revisions and revisions

Summary 23:
Anti-dumping investigations in the EU: rules for establishing and pursuing an investigation
Anti-Dumping investigations: rules governing the establishment and pursuing investigations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1225/2009 — rules for initiating and pursuing a European Union (EU) investigation into dumping
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down detailed rules for the investigation into the alleged dumping of goods by non-EU countries.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 652/2009.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down the procedures for initiating an investigation into allegations of dumping. It covers:
the establishment and treatment of the facts;
the imposition of provisional measures, the imposition and collection of anti-duty duties, the duration and review of measures and the public disclosure of information relating to investigations;
provisional and definitive duties.
Provisional measures
The European Commission may impose provisional or definitive duties on imports of a product from a market-economy third country if it considers that there is an injury to the EU’s economic interests caused by the import of the product from that country.
The Commission may also impose a duty on imports from a non-market economy third country. The duty must not exceed the margin of dumping established by the Commission.
Dumping must be calculated using a weighted average of all relevant and duly documented evidence.
Importers must register with the Commission for a period of 9 months. Imports must not be made subject to registration for more than 9 months and must be subject to a duty of no more than 0.5% of the value of the goods they export.
If the Commission considers that the goods concerned are subject to dumping, it may decide to impose a provisional duty on the imports. The amount of the provisional duty must be less than the margin established but it should not be greater than the lesser duty if such a lesser duty would be adequate to remove the injury to EU industry. The Commission may, however, impose a definitive duty of up to 1 % of the amount of duty imposed on imports.
In the event that the Commission concludes that the imports are not subject to the duty, it can impose a final duty of at least 1 %. The final duty may be higher or lower than the initial duty, depending on the level of the damage caused.
Where a duty is imposed, the Commission must notify the EU countries and the European Economic and Social Committee of the reasons for the decision.
When an EU country withdraws an undertaking from an investigation, it must inform the Commission of the reason and inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Court of Auditors and the Court of Justice of the European Union of the case.
Public disclosure
In order to determine whether two parties are associated, account may be taken of the definition of related parties set out in Article 127 of Commission Implementing Regulation (EU), as well as of the time limits for the disclosure of the information to be made.
Information must be made available to the Commission, the EU country concerned and to the public. The information must be used only for the purpose for which it was requested.
EU countries must provide the Commission with all relevant information, including:
information relating to the origin of the imports;
information on the number of companies involved in the investigation;
details of the number and type of companies that have been involved; and
the number of firms involved in each investigation.
Expiry of the investigation
The investigation is terminated if the Commission decides that the investigation is no longer necessary.
Duration, reviews and refunds
The duration of an investigation is determined by a Commission decision. The decision must be published in the Official Journal no later than 1 month after the Commission has received all the relevant evidence. It must be reviewed at least every 3 years.
A Commission decision may also be made to suspend an investigation if the information it receives is insufficient or if the investigation has not been completed within the deadline specified in the regulation.
An adjustment may be made for differences in commissions paid in respect of the sales under consideration.
Reviews
A decision may be adopted to suspend the investigation or to grant a refund to a company that has not complied with the conditions of the regulation or to withdraw its undertaking from the investigation. It may also grant the Commission the right to review the decision and to grant refunds to the company that withdrew its undertaking.
References
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Commission Implementing Regulations (EU, Euratom) 2015/2447
-
OJ L 127 of 26.4.2015
See consolidated version.

Summary 24:
Protecting the EU’s economic interests against subsidised imports from non-EU countries: EU anti-subsidies and countervailing measures (codified version)
Protecting EU‘s economic interest against subsidies and other forms of unfair trade in goods: codified version
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the protection of the European Union against subsidisation and anti-infringement measures
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Council Regulation (EC) No 597/2009 on protection against subsidies.
It aims to ensure that EU countries and exporters do not benefit from unfair trade practices.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
all imports into the EU from countries or exporters not covered by the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade 1994 (GATT) and to imports from countries covered by Annex I of the Agreement on Agriculture (Agreement on Agriculture);
all goods imported for free circulation from countries outside the EU that are subject to a specific type of subsidisation or other type of anti-competitive measures.
Importers and importers must notify the European Commission of any imports they consider to be subject to such a type of subsidy or to an anti-defrauding measure.
The Commission may launch an investigation to determine whether a country or an exporter has benefited from such a subsidy or a similar type of trade practice. The investigation must cover both subsidies and any injury caused by such a practice. It must be launched within 60 days of receiving a formal complaint from an interested party. The Commission request for information.
Provisional duties may be imposed on imports that are not subject to the investigation. The provisional duties must be imposed no earlier than 60 days from the initiation of the proceedings but no later than 9 months from the beginning of the investigations. In cases of breach or withdrawal of undertakings, definitive duties may also be levied on goods entered for the free circulation no more than 90 days before the application of provisional measures, provided that the imports have been registered in accordance with Article 24(5) and that any such retroactive assessment does not apply to imports entered before the breach or withdrawing of the undertaking.
Where a provisional duty has not been imposed, the interested party must be provided with an opportunity to request final disclosure within time limits set by the Commission.
Duties may not hinder the procedures of customs clearance.
If the Commission concludes that a country has not complied with its obligations under the regulation, it may impose a definitive duty on the goods it considers to have benefited from the subsidy or the anti-fraud measure. It may also impose a provisional or final duty on goods that have been imported for the purpose of preventing or limiting the use of domestic over imported goods.
In addition, the Commission may request the assistance of the competent authorities of the Member States in the monitoring of the undertakings. They must send to the Commission the information requested together with the results of all inspections, checks or investigations carried out.
Disclosure rights of interested parties
All interested parties must be given the opportunity to present their case within the time limits laid down in the regulation. If they refuse to do so, they may be subject, for example, to a preliminary investigation or to a final decision.
Any interested party that does not provide the Commission with all the information it needs may be excluded from the investigation or from the final decision, unless it can prove that the claim is justified. In addition, any interested party may be refused access to, or otherwise prevented from providing necessary information within the limits set for in this regulation, or significantly impedes the investigation, or if it is found that the Commission has not made an affirmative or negative decision on the facts available.
Rules on the conduct of investigations and the review of investigations
The European Commission may review the regulation in the light of new information it receives. The regulation sets out detailed rules on how to do this.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Subsidies: any form of economic or financial support to a producer or exporter that is intended to encourage or facilitate the production of a particular product.
* Countervailing measure: any trade measure that is designed to prevent or restrict the supply of a product or to restrict the export of that product to a non-member of the EU.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 of 21 December 2011 on the Protection of the Union against Subsidisation and Anti-Infringement Measures (Codified Version) (OJ L 183, 28.12.2011, pp. 1-36)
