Summary 25:
EU financial support for EU countries affected by the COVID-19 crisis (2020-2021)
EU financial aid for countries hit by the crisis
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2094 on the European Union financial assistance for countries in the event of a crisis
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a financial instrument, known as the EU financial assistance facility (EUFIF), to help EU countries recover and recover from the impact of the crisis.
The EUFIF is the largest financial instrument in the EU’s economic and monetary union (EMU). It provides up to €360 million in loan support to EU countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must submit to the European Commission a recovery and resilience plan that is duly reasoned and substantiated. The plan must include:
a comprehensive economic and financial recovery plan for each EU country;
recovery and resilience plans for each of the EU countries covered by the facility;
a medium-to-low-impact assessment of the economic impact of measures taken;
measures to improve the resilience of the economy and the competitiveness of the labour market;
investments in digital technologies to support the digitalisation of SMEs and the growth of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs);
measures in the energy, transport and green sectors to improve energy efficiency;
the implementation of measures to reduce greenhouse gas emissions.
Each EU country may also request technical support under the Technical Support Instrument (TSI).
. The total amount of support for each country is up to 6.8 % of its gross national income (GNI) in current prices.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULAWARD APPLY?
The regulation applies from 1 February 2020.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for administering the facility. It is assisted by the European Stability Mechanism (ESM).
For more information, see:
EU Financial Aid Facility (European Commission).

Summary 26:
The COVID-19 instrument for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027 (European Semester 2020-2027)
The EU’s economic and social resilience instrument (EUESI)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/241 establishing the EUESI
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Economic and Social Instrument (EESI).
It aims to support national authorities in improving their capacity to:
design, develop and implement reforms;
prepare, amend, implement and revise recovery and resilience plans.
It also aims to encourage investment, to increase competitiveness and to achieve sustainable economic convergence, resilience and recovery.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The instrument is designed to support EU countries’ efforts to improve their capacity:
to:
develop and implement sustainable economic policies and strategies;
to prepare, amend and revise their economic and financial recovery plans; and
to promote the sustainable development of the European Union (EU).
Objectives
The objective of the instrument is to assist EU countries in:
improving their capacity in developing, developing and implementing reforms and to prepare and revising their economic recovery plans pursuant to the European Semester (1 January 2021-31 December 2020);
to encourage investment and to promote the green and digital transitions.
Specific objectives
To achieve the general objective, the instrument has the specific objectives of:
supporting the development of sustainable economic and economic policies that take into account guidelines for employment;
encouraging investment in the green, digital and sustainable growth;
promoting the sustainable growth and jobs;
fostering innovation and competitiveness;
raising awareness of the impact of climate change and climate change on the economy;
providing technical support for the development and implementation of economic recovery strategies.
Cooperation and support plans
EU countries are required to develop their own national multiannual investment strategies in support of those priorities in the context of the EU Semester.
The European Commission is responsible for coordinating and coordinating the instrument. It is assisted by the European Research and Innovation Fund (ERIF) and the European Investment Bank (EIBF).
The EESI has a budget of €864 million.
Eligible actions
The Commission may grant grants up to 100% of the eligible costs. The Commission may also request technical support from EU countries.
In addition, the Commission may make available final studies or reports produced as part of eligible actions.
Transparency
The regulation establishes a single online public repository through which it may, subject to applicable rules and on the basis of consultation with the EU countries concerned, make available the results of studies, reports and analyses it has carried out, as well as any final studies and reports produced by EU countries, that it considers to be eligible for EU funding.
Monitoring and evaluation
To ensure the proper implementation of this instrument, EU countries must:
report to the Commission on the progress they have made in implementing the instrument;
ensure that the measures they have taken are transparent;
monitor the effectiveness of the instruments and the results they have achieved.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The European economic resilience instrument’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Economic and social instrument: an instrument designed to help EU countries improve their capacities to develop, develop, implement, prepare and revise economic, social and recovery plans in order to promote sustainable economic growth and to encourage the growth of the Union's economy.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 552/2020 of 27 June 2020 establishing the European economic and Social instrument (OJ L 183, 1.7.2020, pp. 1-8)

Summary 27:
Compensation for passengers in the event of damage to railway rolling stock and carriage facilities (Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007)
Compensation to passengers in case of damage or damage to rail rolling stock or carriage facilities
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — Compensation for passengers injured or seriously injured on the railway system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on compensation for passengers who are injured or severely injured on railway services.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (EC, No 1370/2007.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the carriage of passengers on rail services;
traffic management of trains;
conditions of carriage;
the maintenance and repair of railway infrastructure;
services provided under public service contracts.
Railways must:
ensure that passengers are provided with adequate number of places for bicycles;
provide passengers with the means to travel without delay and without undue delay.
Train companies must ensure that:
there are sufficient places for passengers with disabilities and persons with reduced mobility to enable them to be independently mobile;
there is sufficient space for bicycles in the carriage and in the cabins;
they must provide adequate numbers of places to accommodate persons with disabilities, unless the acquisition or upgrade concerns restaurant cars, sleeping cars or couchette cars.
Passengers are entitled to compensation for:
delays of up to 60 minutes;
luggage loss;
partial loss of luggage
total loss of carriage.
total compensation for partial loss of baggage
damage
damage to railway infrastructure
loss of carriage facilities.
EU countries must take all necessary measures to ensure that compensation is paid out in a non-discriminatory manner.
In addition, the European Commission must adopt an implementing act establishing a common form for compensation requests under this regulation by 7 June 2023.
The Commission must publish a report on the implementation of the regulation every 2 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 7 June 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Parliament and the Council adopted the regulation on behalf of the European Union (now the EU).
For more information, see:
Railway compensation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Limitation of liability: the amount of compensation to be paid for the loss or damage caused by the carriage, carriage or equipment of a train.
* Liability: the liability of the railway company for any damage or loss resulting from its negligence or intentional omission.

Summary 28:
Pre-accession assistance for the period 2021-2027: instrument for preaccession financial assistance (IPA III) for the EU’s candidate countries
Post-Accession assistance: Instrument for Preaccession Financial Aid for the European Union (IPP III)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 establishing a framework for planning and delivering external assistance to the countries seeking to join the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a multiannual financial framework (MFF) to support the countries applying for the status of candidate country in the EU.
It sets out the priorities for providing assistance to these countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes the instrument for Pre-accessional Financial Assistance (‘IAPA III’).
The aim of the MFF is to provide financial support for the countries that are applying for EU membership.
The beneficiaries are Albania, Bosnia and Herzegovina (Bulgaria, Croatia, Cyprus, Estonia, Greece, Hungary, Latvia, Lithuania, Montenegro, Poland, Romania, Serbia, Slovenia, Turkey, Ukraine, Ukraine and the United Kingdom (1).
They are listed in Annex I of the regulation.
Each beneficiary is assessed on its own merits according to its commitment to and progress in implementing reforms, in particular in the field of the rule of law and fundamental rights, democratic institutions and public administration reform, economic development and competitiveness.
In addition, the beneficiaries are assessed according to their capacity to implement reforms.
the European Commission adopts a strategic plan for each year. The strategic board is composed of representatives of the European Commission, all EU countries and the European Investment Bank (EIB).
Strategic planning and general principles
The European Commission is responsible for the strategic planning and implementation of the assistance. It adopts the strategic plans for each of the beneficiaries. The plan must be adopted for a period of up to 7 years. It must include a description of the priority areas for which the assistance will be provided and the measures to be taken to achieve these priorities.
EU countries are assessed on the basis of the progress they have made in implementing their reform objectives. The Commission is assisted by a committee for the instrument.
Monitoring, reporting and evaluation
The Commission monitors, audits and evaluates the progress made by the beneficiaries, including at local level. It also monitors, auditors and evaluates its financial interests.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
   
It applies from 1 January 2021.

Summary 29:
External Action Guarantee for the implementation of the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development (SDGs) and the EU’s common foreign and security policy (EASDP)
External Action guarantee for the execution of the SDGs and the European Common Foreign and Security Policy (ECSD)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — the European External Action Guarantees
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union (EU)-wide external action guarantee*.
It aims to ensure consistency between the different areas of its external action and between these and its other policies, as well as to work for a high degree of cooperation in all fields of international relations.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries and territories in the European Neighbourhood Area (EEA). It covers the period from 1 January 2021 until the launch of that evaluation.
The EASDP is funded by the EU budget. It is implemented through a number of instruments, such as the European Instrument for International Nuclear Safety Cooperation (EIFIC), the European Fund for Strategic Development (EFSD+), the EU External Action Service (EFSD) and other instruments.
It covers the following areas of cooperation:
human rights and democracy;
civil society and civil society organisations;
peace, stability and conflict prevention;
global challenges;
development and security for development;
international cooperation;
economic and social development.
In addition, the EEFSDGs are supported by:
the European Union External Action Fund (EAFD);
;
the EFSD, which is the main instrument for the financing of multilateral and bilateral and multilateral programmes.
EU support for multilateral projects
The European Union also provides financial support for the development and security of EU countries in the areas covered by the EAFD, including:
research and development programmes;
cooperation with non-EU countries and international organisations in the fields of human rights, democracy, civil society, peace, stability, conflict prevention, global challenges, economic development and social inclusion;
exchange of information and communication technologies;
support for research and development (R & D&D) programmes and research and innovation (R&I) activities;
training and mentoring programmes for young people;
capacity building of military actors for development, in accordance with Article 9 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU).
The EU also provides support for:
economic development, including through:
investment in infrastructure and energy;
research, development and innovation;
education, training and awareness-raising;
environmental protection;
health and safety;
transport;
science and technology;
information and culture;
energy;
justice and security;
regional cooperation.
Implementation
EU countries’ national authorities are responsible for implementing the programme. They must:
ensure that the programme is implemented in line with the objectives of the multilateral instruments;
take the necessary measures to implement the programme;
prepare action plans for the duration of the programme and ensure that they are transparent and timely;
coordinate their actions with the relevant international organisations and bodies;
provide the European Commission with information on the progress made and the results achieved.
A strategic board is responsible for the organisation and functioning of EFSD+. It meets at least twice a year and, when possible, adopts opinions by consensus.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It applies from 31 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
The UEADG is one of the main instruments of the Union's external action policy. The other instruments are the EIFIC and the EFSDP.
For more information, see:
External action guarantee (European Commission).

Summary 30:
Whistleblower protection: protecting the public interest in the fight against breaches of EU law (Directive 2018/2021/EU)
Whistlebleaker protection: safeguarding the public’s interest in protecting the EU’S environmental protection
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/1921 on the protection of persons reporting breaches of Union law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out rules to protect persons who report breaches of the EU environmental acquis (EU law on environmental protection).
It aims to improve the enforcement of EU environmental protection.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to persons who:
report breaches to EU institutions, bodies, offices or agencies;
make a public disclosure of a breach;
have the right to be compensated for any damage caused by the breach.
It does not apply to:
the protection of classified information;
protection of legal and medical professional privilege;
the secrecy of judicial deliberations;
rules on criminal procedure.
EU institutions and bodies have the obligation to establish internal and external reporting channels and to follow up on reports.
Reporting must be carried out by:
a person who is well placed to report a breach directly to the company head;
a company officer well placed in a position to report to the organisational head, such as a chief compliance or human resources officer, an integrity officer, a legal or privacy officer or a chief financial officer;
an auditor, a chief audit executive or a member of the board;
third parties such as external counsel, auditors, trade union representatives or employees’ representatives.
An internal reporting channel may also be set up by an external reporting platform provider or by a third party.
In addition, the reporting person must be encouraged to report breaches to other sources of information such as the public or the media.
The reporting person may not be penalised for reporting a breach if the breach remains unaddressed, for instance in cases where the breach was not appropriately assessed or investigated, or no appropriate remedial action was taken.
Persons who are not sure about how to report or whether they will be protected in the end may be discouraged from reporting. However, this should not discourage them from doing so.
Exchange of information between reporting persons
Reporting persons must be able to share information with each other and with third parties. This information may include:
information on the nature and scope of the breach,
the reasons for the breach and the nature of the action taken to address it;
any other information that may be relevant to the investigation, investigation or remedial actions to be taken;
details of the person who made the disclosure, including the nature, nature and content of the information provided.
External reporting channels must also be established to enable reporting by means of physical meetings, within a reasonable timeframe. These channels should also be open to third parties, including:
consultants,
auditors,
legal and medical professionals,
employee representatives,
public service employees,
other persons working in the public sector.
This directive does not affect the application of EU or national law relating to any of the following:
privacy of confidential information,
access to personal data,
protecting legal and professional privilege,
rules of judicial deliberation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
   Â  
The European Commission has adopted a communication on the implementation of the directive.
For more information, see:
EU whistleblower protection (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* External reporting channel: a channel where a person reports a breach to another person or organisation.
* Internal reporting: an internal channel in which a person, or a company officer, or an employee, reports to the organisation head. In smaller entities, this function could be a dual function held by a company head, a vice-president, a compliance officer or an auditor or a board member.

Summary 31:
European integrated border management (EIBM)
European integration of borders
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 establishing a European integrated Border Management Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) Agency for the coordination, monitoring and protection of fundamental rights.
It sets up the European Border and Coast Guard Agency.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the tasks of the agency:
coordinating and monitoring the EU’s external borders;
monitoring and protecting the fundamental rights of EU citizens;
ensuring that EU countries’ border guards and return-related personnel are properly trained and equipped;
providing the European Commission with information and expertise on border management.
The agency is based in Warsaw, Poland. It is managed by an executive director and a management board. The executive director is appointed by the Commission. It may not delegate to the executive board tasks related to decisions that must be passed by a majority of two thirds of the management board and must be confirmed by the European Parliament and the Council of the EU.
Agency liaison officers are based in each EU country. They are responsible for the following tasks:
assisting the national coordination centre in the coordination and monitoring of EU border control operations;
collecting, analysing and analysing information on EU borders and border control systems;
operating the European situational picture of EU countries;
carrying out joint operations for the purposes of the European border control system;
reporting on the capabilities and capabilities of the Agency;
the coordination of information exchange between national border control authorities and EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that their national coordination centres are equipped with:
a national situational picture;
a database of national border security information;
training of border guard and return staff;
an annual report on the Agency’S capabilities.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of implementing acts on the European integration of border management, including:
Implementing act (EC) No 552/2014 on the establishment of the EIBM;
Implementation act (EEC) 2018/853 on the creation of the Executive Board of the Commission;
Regulating (EU/EU) 2019-2024 on the setting up of a European Integrated Border Control Agency (EICB) (see summary).
KEY TERMS
* European border management: a system of border controls that aims to ensure that the EU has effective border management at all points of entry, exit and transit, i.e. the broadest possible geographical basis.
* Schengen: the area of the external borders of all EU countries where there is a free movement of persons, goods and services between them.

Summary 32:
European Network of Information Systems Security Assurance Assurance (ENISA)

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on the European network for cybersecurity
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European network of information security assurance bodies, known as ENISA.
It aims to ensure that cybersecurity policies are based on well-developed risk assessment methods, in both the public and private sectors.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up the European Network for Innovation and Innovation Assurance for Cybersecurity, which aims to:
ensure a high level of cybersecurity in the EU’s information and communications technology (ICT) sectors;
ensures that EU countries’ cybersecurity policies take account of the risks inherent in ICT systems;
provide for the exchange of information and best practice on cybersecurity between EU countries;
encourage the development of national cybersecurity certification systems.
ENISA is responsible for the implementation of the European cybersecurity certification system (ECSIS) and the certification of cybersecurity products and services. It is also responsible for ensuring that the EU cybersecurity certification framework is applied at EU level.
EnISA has a management board and an executive director. The Executive Director is appointed by the Management Board and is assisted by an Executive Director.
The Executive Director and the Executive Director are assisted by a National Liaison Officers Network.
A European Cybersecurity Certification Coordination Group (ECCG) is set up to ensure the consistent application of the ESS certification system. The ECCG is composed of representatives of national certification authorities or other relevant national authorities.
An advisory group is also established to advise the ENISA Advisory Board on cybersecurity issues.

Summary 33:
EU countries’ gross national income (GNI) at market prices (ESA 2010)
EU countries' gross national incomes (ESA2010)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 549/2013 on the harmonisation of Gross National Income at market price
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the methodology for the calculation and transmission of Gross national income*.
It aims to improve the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of the European Union’s (EU, Euratom) Gross Gross Gross Domestic Product (GDP) data.
It repeals Directive 89/130/EEC and Regulation (EC) No 1287/2003.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies from 18 April 2019.
Before 1 October each year, EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with figures for GNI aggregates and their components, in accordance with the definitions referred to in Article 1.
The data must be transmitted for the preceding year and any changes made to the data for previous years must be communicated at the same time. The data must comply with the relevant definitions and accounting rules of ESA 2010 and with the accounting rules set out in Annex A to the regulation.
Eurostat is responsible for transmitting the data to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC).
The Commission may, in the context of national accounting procedures, carry out statistical visits to EU countries to verify that the data comply with ESA 2010 accounting rules. In exercising its right to carry out such visits, the Commission must respect the rules on statistical confidentiality laid down in Article V of the EU Statutes.
In addition to these rules, Eurostat must ensure that:
the data are reliable, exhaustive and comparable;
the inventory and overloading of GNI data are consistent with the ESA 2010 rules;
there is no duplication or overloading in the GNI inventory and the data are up-to-date;
data are comparable across EU countries and that there is compliance with the applicable definitions.
Delegated acts
The European Parliament and the Council have delegated the power to adopt delegated acts to the Commission for a period of 5 years. The delegated acts enter into force only if no objection has been expressed either by the EU Parliament or by the Council within 2 months of notification of that act to them and if, before the expiry of that period, they have both informed the Commission that they will not object.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It applies from 1 October 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national accounting rules by 1 October 2020.
BACKGROUND
GDP is the sum of gross value added of the various institutional sectors or the various industries plus taxes and less subsidies on products (which are not allocated to sectors and industries).
GNI is the average annual Gross Gross Income at Market Price at Gross Domestic Income at Gross Prices. It can be defined in three ways:
production approach: GDP minus primary income payable by resident institutional units to non-resident institutional units plus primary income receivable by resident institutions from the rest of the world;
income approach: Gross Gross Incomes at Gross Income (GGNI)* minus gross domestic product (GDI)* minus primary incomes receivable from non-residents.
For more information, see:
‘GDP at Market Prices’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Gross Gross Indicators: a measure of the gross value-added (GVA) of the economy of an EU country. It is calculated by taking into account:
Gross national income at gross prices;
gross domestic product: the total value of goods and services produced and traded in the economy;
primary income: the gross income of residents of a country.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval of vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units from the United Kingdom
EU type–type–approval requirements for vehicles and systems*, components, separate technical unit
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — EU type–approvals of vehicles and components from outside the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the granting of EU type‑approval* of vehicles*, systems, parts, components or separate technical* units of types not approved under the UK’s EU type‐approval.
It aims to ensure that EU type-approved vehicles and parts from the UK can continue to be sold in the EU market.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles, systems and components of types approved by the European Union type‐certification authority (EU type‐authorisation authority) in accordance with Directive 2007/46/EC (see summary);
separate technical units (e.g. engines, brakes, brakes and separate electrical units);
systems and components (including separate electronic control units).
Vehicles and components covered by the regulation must bear the type‐approved and marked as ‘EU type‑certified’ or ‘UK type‑approved’ markings on the vehicle, system or component.
Vehicle manufacturers must provide the European Commission with the following documentation and information:
the original UK type‐applicable vehicle, including all amendments, the information folder and the test reports;
the vehicle type‐the type‐number of the vehicle and its components, including the number of axles, wheels, tyres and brakes;
a certificate of conformity assessment and conformity assessment.
The technical service must be designated by the authority granting the type‑authorisation and notified by the EU Member State to the Commission.
If a vehicle is not covered by this regulation, the manufacturer must notify the competent authority of the Member State of the EU country in which it is registered and provide the technical service with all the necessary information.
A vehicle or system, system, component or separate electrical unit must be accompanied by a type‐recognition certificate. The certificate must contain the mention ‘Previously type‐ approved and marked‐as’ and refer to the type–approved mark received following the UK type–certification.
Where the manufacturer has not provided the technical services with the required information, it must inform the competent authorities in the Member States of its intention to do so.
In addition, the regulation sets out the conditions under which the EU type•approval authority may grant a Union type* approval to a vehicle or a system, systems or separate unit that does not comply with this regulation. The regulation does not apply to vehicles that have been placed on the market before 1 January 2008 or that have not been placed into service since 1 January 2013.
This regulation applies from 30 March 2019, unless the European Council, in agreement with the EU countries, unanimously decides to extend that period.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 February 2019.
BACKGROUND
The United Kingdom withdraws from the EU and becomes a third country (non-EU country) as of 1 January 2020.
For more information, see:
EU vehicle type–applicability (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Vehicle: any vehicle or component of a vehicle that has been approved by a European type‐control authority.
* System: any separate electrical or electronic control unit or separate electronic unit that has not been approved under this regulation and which has not yet entered into service.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барскагандарка гранка (OJ L 94, 30.2.2019, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019–852 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 35:
European Police Office (Europol) — rules on cooperation between EU countries and Europol (Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 883/2013 on the European Police Office
European Police Organisation (Euratom)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2009/371/JHA on the establishment of a European police office
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules governing cooperation between European Union (EU) countries’ law enforcement authorities and the European Union’s police office, Europol.
It replaces the Convention based on Article K.3 of the Treaty on European Union, on establishing a European Union police office.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1).
Each EU country must:
supply Europol with the information necessary for it to fulfil its objectives, including information relating to forms of crime the prevention or combating of which is considered a priority by the EU;
ensure effective communication and cooperation of all relevant competent authorities with Europol;
raise awareness of Europol's activities;
in accordance with national law, ensure compliance with the law when supplying information to Europol when supplying it.
EU countries may also participate in joint investigation teams.
In addition, EU countries may allow direct contacts between their national police units and the Europol, subject to prior involvement of the national unit.
Europol may directly exchange information, with the exception of personal data, with entities referred to in paragraph 1 of this regulation, in so far as such an exchange is relevant for the performance of its tasks.
The management board is composed of one representative from each EU country and one representative of the European Commission. It meets on the initiative of its Chairperson and is chaired by the Deputy Chairperson. The Executive Director is responsible for the day-to-day running of the management board and for the overall direction of the organisation.
Management Board
The Management Board:
is composed of representatives of the Member States;
is chaired by a representative of each Member State;
meets at least twice a year;
has the power to adopt opinions, guidelines, recommendations, recommendations and best practices.
Executive Director
The executive director is appointed by the European Parliament and the Council and is assisted by a Deputy Director. The executive director submits a report to the Management Board every 2 years on the organisation’S performance and its budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
 Â  Â
  барсканскиг   гарка  харанка (Europolice) is the European police organisation responsible for preventing and combating organised crime. It was set up in 1993.
For more information, see:
Europolice (European External Action Service).

Summary 36:
EU financial assistance for countries affected by the COVID-19 outbreak (2020-2021) (SARS-CoV-2 and SARS coronavirus-2) (Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046)
European Union (EU) financial assistance to countries in difficulties or seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond its control (SARV-2020) (Council Regulation (EU).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 1046/2020 on the European Union’s financial assistance instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union financial instrument (EUFinance Instrument) to provide assistance to EU countries in the event of an exceptional occurrence beyond their control.
It allows the EU to provide financial assistance under exceptional circumstances.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except the United Kingdom (1), which is not eligible for financial assistance because of its withdrawal from the EU.
EU countries may request EU financial assistance where:
their actual and possibly also planned public expenditure has suddenly and severely increased as of 1 February 2020 due to national measures directly related to short‐time work schemes and similar measures to address the socio-economic effects of the exceptional occurrence caused by the outbreak of the coronaviruses;
their gross national income (GNI) has fallen below €100 000 000 000 for the period from 1 January 2020 to 30 March 2020;
the number of deaths from the outbreak in the EU has exceeded 22,000;
there has been a significant increase in the number of cases of the severe acute respiratory syndrome coronaviral diseases.
The European Commission may call on calls on guarantees from EU countries to provide the EU with financial assistance. The Commission must inform the EU countries about the extent to which the margin has been drawn.
Beneficiaries may use financial assistance primarily in support of their national short-time work programmes or similar measures, and, where applicable, in the support of relevant health-related measures.
Contributions to the Instrument are provided in the form of irrevocable, unconditional and on-demand guarantees.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 February 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
The COVID outbreak was declared a pandemic by the World Health Organization (WHO) on 8 May 2020.
For more information, see:
‘CoVID-2020’ on the EU’�s website.

Council Regulation of 18 July 2018 on the Instrument for European Union Financial Assistance to Countries in Difficulty or is seriously Threatened with Severe Difficulty caused by an Exceptional Occurrence beyond its Control (COVID-2019) (OJ L 183, 30.7.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 37:
EU financial markets: clearing of financial derivatives and clearing of credit default swaps (CDSs)
EU Financial Markets: Clearing of Financial Derivatives and Credit Default Swaps (CCDS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/23 on the clearing and settlement of credit derivatives and credit default swap transactions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the establishment and functioning of the European financial markets.
It aims to ensure that the EU’s financial markets remain stable and orderly.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all financial derivatives* and credit derivatives* traded in the EU. It covers:
listed and over-the-counter (OTC) financial and commodity derivatives, cash equities, bonds and other products such as repos;
cash equities* and repurchase agreements*;
counterparties* and investors* with exposure to these products.
Suspension of the trading obligation
EU countries may suspend the trading obligations of a CCP if it is necessary to preserve financial stability and market confidence and to prevent counterparties and investors having high and uncertain risk exposures to a CCP. The suspension should be of a temporary nature with a possibility of extension.
Implementing act
The implementing act sets out a procedure for the suspension of trading obligations. It applies from 12 August 2022 with the exception of:
the clearing obligation which shall apply from 4 July 2020;
the suspension of the OTC derivatives trading obligation from 11 February 2021.
Resolution authorities
the resolution authorities, resolution colleges and the resolution authorities.
the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA).
The resolution authorities are responsible for the resolution of CCPs that fail to comply with the requirements of the regulation.
The European Banking Authority (EBA) and the European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority are also involved in the resolution process.
Responsibilities of resolution authorities
The competent authorities and resolution authorities must:
ensure that CCPs comply with all relevant EU rules;
prepare and implement recovery and resolution plans;
coordinate their activities with the relevant national authorities;
cooperate with the European Banking Supervisory College and the competent national authorities in the event of a failure of the CCP;
take all necessary measures to prevent or address a serious threat to financial stability or the orderly functioning of financial markets in the European Union (EU).
EU Member States must also ensure that appropriate resources are allocated to those resolution authorities and to the resolution colleges.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 12 August 2023.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a number of cross-border financial services providers. They provide services across national borders to a broad range of financial and other institutions across the EU, including banks, insurance companies, investment firms and other financial institutions.
In addition, the regulation sets out rules on:
clearing and settlement obligations for CCPs;
recovery and resolution planning;
preparation and resolution procedures.
For more information, see:
‘Clearance and settlement procedures for credit derivatives’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 38:
European Year of Rail (EU) 2021
European Year 2021
SUMMARY OF:
Decision 2021/852/EU — European Year of rail
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
   
 
It establishes the European Year 2021 as the European Union’s (EU’S) ‘European year of rail’.
It aims to promote rail transport in line with the objectives set out in the communication on the European Green Deal, including with regard to sustainable and smart mobility.
KEY POINTS
The year 2021 will be important for the Union rail policy, since it will represent the first full year where the rules agreed under the Fourth Railway Package will be implemented throughout the Union, namely on the opening of the market of domestic passenger services, on reducing costs and administrative burden for railway undertakings operating across the Union and on providing the European European Union Agency for Railways (ERA) with additional tasks that aim to lower technical barriers.
The European Year will also be the first year in which the European Commission will establish a European label to promote goods and products transported by rail so as to encourage businesses to switch their transport to rail.
In addition, the Commission will set up key performance indicators to monitor the implementation of this decision. The Commission will inform the European Parliament and the Council of its plans by 31 March 2021. Representatives of the EU countries may participate in those meetings as observers.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
The decision applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a climate-neutral society. It aims to transform the EU into a fair and prosperous society with a modern, resource-efficient and competitive economy where there are no net emissions of greenhouse gases in 2050 and where economic growth is decoupled from resource use.
For more information, see:
European Green Deal (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Passenger: a person travelling by rail to, from, or from, a place of residence or business.
* Rail: a means of transport used for the transport of people and essential goods.

Summary 39:
European Security and Defence College (ESDC) (CFSPD)
European security and defence college
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFPS) 2016/2382 establishing a European Security and defence College
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a European security and defense college.
It repeals and repeals Joint Action 2008/550/CFSP.
KEY POINTS
The ESDC is the European Union’s (EU) training and education centre for law enforcement and defence. It is responsible for providing training and training-related activities in the field of law enforcement, defence and security.
The decision establishes the ESDC with the aim of:
establishing close links with the EU institutions and relevant EU agencies, in particular with, but not limited to:
the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (FRONTEX) and the European Defence Agency (EDA) and with the European Satellite Centre (EU SatCen);
supporting the European Alumni Association (EUISS);
cooperating with and drawing on the expertise of international organisations and other relevant actors, such as the European Training and Education institutes of third countries;
drawing on the experience and expertise of national training centres of non-EU countries.
Organisation
The Council appoints a steering committee, chaired by a representative of the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (HR). It is composed of representatives of the EU countries and chaired by the European Parliament and the Council. The head is appointed by the Council and is assisted by the Executive Academic Board. The ESDC has a secretariat. The Secretariat provides support to the Steering Committee, to the Board including its configurations and to institutes for the management, coordination and organisation of ESDC. The Head of the Executive Board is appointed on the basis of a pre-selection process. The High Representative and the Vice-President of the Council are responsible for appointing the head of the Board and the Secretariat.
A steering committee is set up by the Head and comprises representatives of EU countries. It prepares a draft budget for the following financial year and submits it to the Council by 31 July. The Council adopts the draft budget by 31 December. The budget is then approved by the Standing Committee.
Cooperation
The European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Training (CEPOL) and Europol provide technical assistance and technical support for ESDC activities. The EUISS provides financial support for research and other projects in the area of CFSP. In addition, the EUISS supports ESDC Alumni Associations. The European Union Institute for Defence Education and Training (EUIDET) provides technical support to ESDC in the areas of training, education and research.

It has applied since 22 April 2016.

Summary 40:
Reused water: water reuse
Reclaimed water: Water reuse
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2020 on the use of reclaimed water for agricultural irrigation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the reuse of water from urban and agricultural sources.
It aims to protect the environment and human and animal health by ensuring that water is reused for agricultural and urban purposes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
reclaimed water* used for agricultural purposes;
reused water used for urban and rural drinking water;
water used for drinking, bathing, bathing and sanitary purposes; and
water reused for drinking and bathing.
EU countries must ensure that:
the water reuse risk management plan is established by the reclamation facility operator;
the competent authority in charge of the water reuse plan is responsible for drawing up and implementing the plan.
The plan must be updated every 2 years. It must include:
a description of the risks associated with water reuse;
a list of the types of water used;
measures to be taken to reduce the risk of contamination;
details of the methods and methods of water reuse.
In addition, the plan must include a description of how the plan is to be implemented.
Risk management measures must be in place to prevent, reduce and minimise the impact of the reuse on the environment, including:
pollutants, e.g. micro-organisms, eutrophilic bacteria, echinoderms, and e.
the impact of water pollution on human health.
a risk assessment must be carried out every 5 years.
A risk assessment plan must also be established for each type of water use.
Annex I to the regulation sets out the minimum requirements for water quality and the key elements of risk management.
If a water reuse system is used for agriculture, the authority responsible for it must:
ensure that the water is treated and reused at very low rates (2–20 litres/hour) from a system of small-diameter plastic pipes with outlets called emitters or drippers;
assess the water’s quality, including the presence of microorganisms;
establish a monitoring system to assess the performance of the system;
monitor the water quality class with the most stringent requirements, Class A, to assess whether the performance targets (log10 reduction) are complied with;
set performance targets for Class B and Class C, based on the results of a risk assessment carried out by the competent authority.
Where water is used to irrigate crops, it must be treated, treated, reused and used in accordance with the rules set out in Annex II.
To ensure that water reuse is not adversely affected by pollution, EU countries must take measures to prevent and reduce the spread of micro-plastics and other substances of emerging concern. These measures must include measures to: ensure that the plan for water reuse and the monitoring system are in place;ensure water quality monitoring is carried out;establish a risk management system for the water used in agricultural irrigation;establish monitoring systems to monitor the water treatment and reuse of agricultural water.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation applies from 13 May 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 13 May 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulation.
For more information, see:

Summary 41:
EU blue card for highly qualified third-country workers — rules for their mobility within the EU (2018-2023)
European blue card: rules for the mobility of highly qualified workers
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/2023 on the right of citizens of the European Union and their family members to move and reside freely within the Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that highly qualified non-EU citizens and their families have the right to live and work freely in the EU.
It sets out the rules for granting and renewing an EU blue card.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all EU citizens, their families and their associates who have been granted an EU Blue Card. It does not apply to:
non-EU nationals who are already resident in another EU country;
EU citizens who have already been granted a blue card and who wish to remain in that EU country.
EU Blue Card holders must have a valid EU card valid for at least 24 months. It is valid for a maximum of 2 years. It may be renewed for an additional period of up to 5 years, depending on the number of years of continuous employment.
The holder must be able to work in the territory of the EU country in which they are admitted and have the necessary documents and information to prove that they meet the admission criteria.
They must be admitted to the labour market of the country of origin and be accompanied by their employer.
Employment
The employer must provide the holder with:
a complete application for a job,
a statement of the employer’s identity,
the number of employees
a description of the company and the occupation
a list of the sectors, occupations and regions in which the holder is allowed to engage.
If an employer does not provide the necessary information, the holder may be refused admission.
When an employer fails to provide the required information, it may be considered a threat to public health or to public order.
An employer may be subject to sanctions for failing to comply with this directive.
In addition, EU countries may apply a simplified recognition procedure for employers.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLYEARS OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 18 November 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 November 2023.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive. It found that the directive has been largely successful in improving the mobility and integration of highly skilled workers.
For more information, see:
‘EU blue cards’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* EU Blue card: a permanent residence permit issued by an EU country to a highly skilled worker who has been admitted to its labour market.
ACT
Council Directive (EEC) No 549/2013 of 18 November 2013 establishing a European Union blue card (OJ L 75, 10.3.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 43:
Paternity and carers’ leave: equal treatment between men and women in the workplace (Directive 2019/24/EU on paternity and carer’s leave and parental leave for workers)
Paternity (and carers' leave)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It sets out minimum rules for the provision of paternity and childcare leave in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the balance between working and family life.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
paternity leave is taken around the time of the birth of the child and should be clearly linked to the birth for the purposes of providing care;
each worker has the right to 5 working days of paternity leave per year.
Each worker is entitled to a maximum of 4 months of parental leave. This is divided into 10 working days, expressed in working weeks or other time units, taking into account that ten working days correspond to two calendar weeks.
The duration of this leave is calculated according to the number of consecutive fixed-term contracts that a worker has had with the same employer.
Employers must:
accept or refuse a request for parental leave in ways other than on a full-time basis;
ensure that workers who exercise their right to parental leave receive a payment or an allowance for the duration of the leave;
provide flexible working arrangements to allow them to remain in the workforce;
allow one parent to transfer to the other parent more than two months out of the four months of the parental leave provided for in this directive.
In addition, EU countries may determine additional details regarding the scope and conditions of carers leave in accordance with national law or practice.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The directive entered into force on 2 August 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 August 2022.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive. It found that the directive has not yet been fully implemented in all EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘Parental leave’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Paternity leave: a period of unpaid leave taken by a man or woman for the purpose of providing for the care of a child.
ACT
Directive (EU) 2019/23/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 4 April 2019 on the right of the Member States to provide for the equitable treatment of men (Paternity Directive) and of women (Caring Leave Directive).

Summary 44:
Fraud and other criminal offences affecting the EU’s financial interests
Fraud
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/849 on the protection of the European Communities’ financial interests (Financial Instruments Directive)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It establishes minimum rules relating to the definition of criminal offences and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the Union's financial interests.
It aims to prevent and combat fraud and other forms of illegal activities at the expense of the EU's financial interest.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must take the necessary measures to enable the investigation, prosecution, trial and judicial decision of offences that are punishable by a maximum sanction of at least 4 years of imprisonment for at least 5 years from the time when the offence was committed. These sanctions must be effective, proportionate, dissuasive and dissuasive.
The directive applies to:
fraud affecting the financial interests of EU institutions, bodies, offices or agencies;
any expenditure in connection with the public contracts determined by Article 101(1) of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012;
public procurement-related expenditure;
all revenue arising from VAT own resources.
Exemptions
The Directive does not apply to the following criminal offences:
any offence that is committed for the benefit of a legal person established in the territory of an EU country, or for the purpose of the exercise of its official duty.
any other criminal offence which does not require intention.
the offender is a habitual resident in the EU country and is not covered by this directive.
Any offence which is committed by a natural person or by a person acting in his or her official duty is covered by the directive. However, EU countries may decide to extend its jurisdiction to criminal offences which have been committed outside its territory in any of the following situations:
when the offender is an individual or a legal entity established in its territory;
when an offence is committed on behalf of an official of a national authority;
where the offender acts in the official capacity of a public authority.
Sanctions
The EU countries must ensure that the maximum sanction for the offences referred to in this directive is at least four years’ imprisonment. The maximum sanction may be reduced to a maximum of 3 years if the offence has been committed for a period of more than 5 years.
Where a country does not provide for an explicit threshold for considerable damage or advantage as a basis for a maximum penalty, it must take into account that the amount of damage and advantage is taken into account by its courts in the determination of sanctions for fraud.
Cooperation between the EU countries and the European Commission (OLAF) and other EU institutions and bodies
The Commission must cooperate with the national authorities of EU countries in order to identify, investigate, prosecute and bring to justice those who commit, commit or attempt to commit, or are involved in committing, offences affecting EU funds.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The European Parliament and the Council adopted it on 6 July 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 6 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The Financial Instruments Directive (FID) is one of a series of legal instruments designed to protect the European Union (EU). The other instruments are Regulation (EC) No 2988/95 (see summary) and Directive 2009/13/EC.
For more information, see:
‘Fraud’ and ‘Other Criminal Offences’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
Act
Entry into force
Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal: 6.7.2017
OJ L 183, 6.8.2017, pp. 1-5
RELATED ACTS

Summary 45:
EU rules on cross-border card-based payment transactions (EU-wide rules on merchant fees) (EU countries’ national rules on card fees and charges)
EU countries' rules on transnational card-by-card payments (EU country rules on user charges and fees)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/865 on the harmonisation of EU rules on the charging of merchant fees and fees for card based payment transactions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise the charges charged by merchants and consumers for the payment instruments they accept.
It sets out the rules that must be applied to all card-related transactions, including:
transactions with commercial cards;
cash withdrawals at automatic teller machines or at a payment service provider’s counter;
cross-border transactions with payment cards issued by three party payment card schemes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following types of transactions: transactions with commercial and payment card issuers and payment service providers, including cash withdrawals, cash withdrawals and cash withdrawals at automated tellers or at the counter of a payment services provider;
payment card transactions issued by a payment card scheme.
The legislation does not apply to:
payments made by a merchant to a third party (e.g. a bank) or to a non-governmental organisation; or
payee payments made by an individual.
In addition, the regulation does not cover the following:
co-badging and choice of payment brand or payment application;
of payment application.
Co-related fees
Payment service providers must charge merchants for the right to accept a payment instrument issued by the same issuer.
Payments must be made in the currency of the EU country in which the payment instrument is issued. The fees charged must be the same as those charged for the corresponding payment within the country where the payment was made. The regulation also sets out a limit on the amount of fees that can be charged for each type of payment instrument. The limit is set by the European Commission. The reference period for the first calculation of such value is 15 calendar months before the date of application of paragraphs 2 and 3 and shall end 3 calendar months after that date.
EU country authorities must ensure that the fees charged by the merchant are calculated in accordance with the EU’S national rules.
Where a payment is made in another EU country, the payment provider must charge the same amount for the same type of transaction. The European Commission must publish a report every 3 years on the implementation of the regulation.
Consumers must be informed of the fees paid by merchants for each payment instrument they use. This information must be displayed prominently at the entrance of the shop and at the till. The Commission must collect the information required to establish this report and cooperate closely with the authorities for the collection of data. EU countries must also ensure that payment services users have access to out-of-court complaint and redress procedures or take equivalent measures in the event of disputes between payment service users and payment services providers.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The EU country implementing the regulation has to incorporate it into national law by 9 June 2015. It applies from 8 June 2015, with the exception of Articles 3, 4, 6 and 12, which shall apply from 9 December 2015, and of Articles 7, 8, 9 and 10, which will apply from9 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to improve the functioning of the internal market for electronic payments. These include:
Delegated Regulation (EC) No 924/2009 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 25 October 2011 establishing the principle that charges paid by users for a payment in euro are the same for the equivalent payment within a Member State including cross-Border payments;
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 552/2013 and (EC), No 553/2013 establishing the European Union rules on consumer charges and charges for the use of electronic payment cards and on fees charged for cross-EU payment transactions;
Directive 2011/83/EU on the establishment of a single European Union payment card system (see summary).
The new regulation is part of the effort to modernise the EU payment system and to establish a single market for all types of payment instruments.
For more information, see:
European Union rules for the electronic payment system (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Commercial card: a card that is issued by one or more issuers.
* Payment service provider: a provider of payment services.

Summary 46:
Europass — validation of non-formal and informal learning
Europass – validation of skills and qualifications
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/852 on the European Union’s validation of qualifications and skills
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It aims to facilitate the validation of the knowledge, skills and competences of individuals who have been acquired through learning, validated, and to obtain a full qualification, or, where applicable, partial qualification.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out the criteria and procedures for the validation and assessment of qualifications.
It applies to all EU citizens who wish to move to another EU country and to employers wishing to recruit workers from other EU countries.
The validation of these qualifications is based on the Qualification Evaluation Framework (EQF), which is a standardised set of assessment and validation methods used by EU countries to assess and validate qualifications. It also covers skills obtained through education and training, youth work and volunteering.
EU countries are responsible for the implementation of this decision at national level through the relevant national services and without prejudice to national arrangements in terms of implementation and organisation.
In addition, the decision establishes a European network of employment services (EURES), which provides free assistance to jobseekers, employers and jobseekers’ access to mobility services, and assists employers in recruiting workers from non-EU countries. The Europass portal also provides information on qualifications and training systems in EU countries, as well as on job vacancies.
EURES portals provide users with a wide range of information, such as job vacancies, job vacancies and training opportunities, and qualifications. The portal also allows jobseekers to complete their CVs in standardised formats.
Users have the option to store their personal data on the Europass online platform. This information can be used to:
assess and validate their qualifications;
provide employers with information on the qualifications they have acquired;
ensure that employers have access to the information they need to make a decision on a job applicant;
make it easier for jobseekers and employers to find suitable candidates.
A Europass portfolio comprises five document templates:
the Europass Curriculum Vitae (CV);
the Qualifications template;
the qualifications template; and
the qualification template.
Each EU country is responsible for implementing this decision through its national services.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
From 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of initiatives aimed at improving the quality of education, training and vocational education in the EU. These include the creation of Europass, which is an online information system designed to assist jobseekers in their search for a job.
For more information, see:
‘Qualifications and skills’ on Europass’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* Qualifications: skills acquired through education or training, training or volunteering, or through youth work.

Summary 47:
Statistics concerning the transport of goods by inland waterways (codified version)
Statistics concerning:
Transport of goods through inland waterways
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1365/2006 on the collection and transmission of inland waterways statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EU) No 223/2009 on inland waterways.
It aims to improve the quality and comparability of EU-wide transport statistics.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must send the European Commission (Eurostat) a report containing such information and data as it may request in order to verify the quality of the data transmitted.
Eurostat must transmit the data to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions every 5 years.
The Commission has the power to:
delegate the statistical powers delegated to it by EU countries for the collection, transmission and analysis of inland waterway transport statistics;
use these powers to monitor and develop the common transport policy, as well as the transport elements of policies on the regions and on trans-European networks.
Delegated acts are adopted in accordance with the implementing acts contained in the annexes to the regulation. The Commission must justify the statistical actions for which those delegated acts provide, using, where appropriate, a cost-effectiveness analysis, including an assessment of the burden on respondents and of the production costs as referred to in Article 14(3)(c) of the regulation (Article 14(2) of Regulation (EC).
The European Commission must draw up a report in respect of the delegation of power not later than 9 months before the end of the 5-year period.
Each year, the Commission must submit a report to Eurostat with a summary of the information it has received from the EU countries. The report must include:
the number of vessel traffic (annual data);
the type of transport (international (except transit) and number of movements of loaded vessels and empty vessels;
the total number of vessels (Vessel-km, empty vessels) and vessels-km (vessel-km).
In addition to these data, Eurostat must also provide the Commission with:
a report on the number and type of vessels and the number of empty vessels used;
a description of the types of goods transported;
an estimate of the economic and social impact of the EU’s transport policy on the inland waterways sector.
In the event of a change in the number or type of vessel or vessel-kilometres used, EU countries must provide a report on that change, including:
number of vessels used, and the type of vehicle and vessel-km used, in the transport sector;
total number of vehicles and vessels used in the transportation sector; and
the volume of goods moved in the course of household and office removals; baggage transported separately from passengers; motor vehicles being moved for repair; other non-market goods moving for repair.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
Inland waterways are the main means of transport in the European Union (EU). They constitute a significant part of the transport system and constitute a major source of revenue for the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Inland waterway statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Inland waterways: the term ‘inland waterways’ is used to describe the means by which goods are transported across the EU by sea, air, rail, road, inland waterways and other modes of transport. It includes:
vehicle traffic (traffic in the form of: vessels, aircraft, trains, barges and other means of transportation;
transit (transit of goods between EU countries);
total movements of goods (vehicle movements and movements of empty vehicles);
movements of vehicles (vehicles, trains and other forms of transport).
* Vessel traffic: the total number and types of vehicles, including aircraft, ships and other types of transport, that are used to transport goods by water.

Summary 48:
Statistics at EU level on rail transport (Regulation (EU) 2017/852)
Statistics at Union level on railway transport (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation No 852/2017/EU on the transmission of EU-wide railway transport statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection, transmission and analysis of statistics on rail traffic in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all railways in the EU. It does not apply to the carriage of goods by rail.
Each EU country must report statistics which relate to rail transport on its national territory. These include:
total passenger-kilometre movements;
total train-km;
transit between two places (a place of loading/embarkation and a place of unloading/disembarking) outside the reporting country;
international transport;
traffic between a place (of loading or unloading) or international transport (between two places) in the reporting EU country and another place in another EU country.
EU countries must transmit the statistics referred to in the regulation’s annexes to Eurostat every year.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of the regulation every 3 years. This report must include information on the quality of the data transmitted, the data collection methods used and information on potential improvements and on users’ needs.
In addition, the Commission must publish a report every 2 years on the results of the statistical analyses carried out by the EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a single rail transport market. It is the largest single market in the world, with a total rail transport volume of over 100 million passenger-km.
For more information, see:
‘Rail transport statistics’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Passenger: the number of passengers transported by rail over a distance of one kilometre.
* Train: the means of transport by rail of one or more persons.

Summary 49:
Exemption from visa requirement for non-EU nationals of non-European Union (EU) countries: Schengen rules on non-visas (codified version) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 539/2001 on the application of the principle of reciprocity in the field of visa requirements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for the granting of an exemption from the visa requirement to nationals of one or more EU countries who are not nationals of an EU country.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
non-EU countries whose nationals are subject to a visa requirement when crossing the external borders of EU countries for more than 90 days;
third countries which are exempt from the requirement to be in possession of a visa when crossing their external borders.
It does not apply to the following countries:
the United Kingdom (1);
the Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (2);
Ireland (3);
Sweden (4);
Iceland (5);
Norway (6);
Germany (7);
Austria (8);
France (9);
Spain (10);
Italy (11);
Belgium (12); and
Switzerland (13).
Exemptions from visa requirements:
EU countries’ nationals of Hong Kong, Hong Kong and Taiwan (1),
Hong Kong (2),
Seychelles (3) and
the Republic of Ireland (4),
the People’s Republic of Moldova (5) and the Kingdom of Norway (6) (7).
The European Commission adopts the implementing act within 1 month of receiving the notification from a simple majority of the EU countries of the existence of circumstances relating to the non-compliance with certain requirements.
The implementing act must be amended by the Commission within 30 days of receiving a report from the European Parliament, the Council or the European Economic and Social Committee of the European Union (EEU) on the implementation of the regulation. The Commission then adopts a delegated act to suspend the exemption for 90 days, taking into account the available resources in the consulates of the Member States and to amend Annex II accordingly.
If the Commission considers that the situation has changed as regards those criteria, it adopts an implementing act to extend the duration of the suspension for a period of 3 years.
In addition, the Commission must publish a report every 3 years on the status of the third countries which it considers, based on concrete and reliable information, to be no longer complying with the requirements. The report must focus on the countries which have been derogating from the obligation to grant a visa.
A delegated act may be adopted to suspend an exemption for up to 3 years if:
there is a substantial increase in the number of nationals of a third country who do not comply with the visa requirements;
the number of visa applications has increased by more than 50%.
The Commission must consult experts designated by each Member State in accordance with the principles laid down in the Interinstitutional Agreement of 13 April 2016 on Better Law-Making.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a visa-free travel policy for its nationals. It applies to all EU citizens who are:
traveling to, from, or staying in the territory of another Member State;
visa holders of a valid residence permit;
travelers who have a valid passport.
For more information, see:
Visa requirements (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Non-EU country: any country which does not grant a valid visa to its nationals when crossing its external borders, unless it does so on the basis of a request from the EU country concerned.
* Exempted country: a country which has granted a visa to a non-member of its territory for a specific period of time.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 50:
European Union Agency for Criminal Justice Cooperation (EUROJHA) — rules of procedure, structure and operation of Eurojust (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1271/2013 establishing Eurojust
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the legal framework for Eurojust, a European Union agency for criminal justice cooperation (Eurojust).
It sets out Eurojust’s organisational structure, its tasks and its budget.
KEY POINTS
Scope
Eurojust is responsible for investigating, prosecuting, investigating and prosecuting serious criminal offences in the European Union (EU). It has a number of tasks, including:
collecting, analysing and prosecuting criminal offences;
exchanging information;
providing technical assistance;
assisting the European Parliament, the Council, the European Commission and the European Police Organisation (EPPO) in the field of criminal justice;
contributing to the prevention, investigation and prosecution of serious crimes in the EU.
The regulation sets out the tasks and objectives of the agency. It also lays down the rules for its work programme.
It covers criminal offences related to the serious crimes listed in Annex I of the regulation. The regulation does not apply to:
reciprocal cooperation between EU countries’ national authorities;
cooperation between Eurojust and Interpol;
joint investigation teams;
the exchange of information between the national authorities of EU countries and between national authorities and Eurojust.
Euronavisional work programme
The European Union countries are responsible for drawing up and implementing Eurojust's resource programming. The resource programming must be sent to the Commission by no later than 31 January each year. It must clearly indicate which tasks have been added, changed or deleted in comparison with the previous financial year. The European Parliament and the Council have the power to adopt the programming document. The College of Justice of the EU adopts the programming documents. It is assisted by an Executive Board and an Executive Director. The Executive Board adopts implementing rules concerning the Data Protection Officer.
EU countries must cooperate with Eurojust in order to prevent, investigate and prosecute criminal offences. They must:
cooperate with each other and cooperate with Interpol and other international organisations;
ensure that Eurojust carries out its tasks in accordance with the rules of the European Criminal Police Organisation;
participate in the work of the College and the Executive Board;
share information on Eurojust with the Commission and other relevant national authorities.
In addition, Eurojust:
is responsible for:
ensuring the effective implementation of the multiannual work programme for the period 2014-2020;
conducts joint investigations with other EU countries;
facilitates cooperation between national police and judicial authorities in the fight against organised crime;
coordinates with the United Nations Office for the Prohibition of Transnational Organized Crime (OPOC);
provides technical assistance to the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (OLAC) and the Eurojust Directorate-General for Law Enforcement Cooperation;
is involved in the coordination of the joint investigation teams set up by Eurojust;
has a budget of €1.5 billion for the current financial year, which includes:
a budget for the operational budget;
an annual work programme of €500 million for the next financial year;
a work programme to be adopted by the College by 31 January every year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
 
For more information, see:
‘Eurojust and the EU’ on the European External Action Service's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision 2014/852/JHA of 21 December 2014 establishing the legal basis for the establishment of the Agency for Community Justice Cooperation known as Eurojust [See amending act(s)].
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) No 652/2014 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
