Summary 51:
Criminal law to combat money laundering: preventing, investigating and prosecuting money laundering (Directive (EU) 2017/1371 on combating money laundering by means of criminal law (SUMMARY OF:
Directive on the prevention, investigation and prosecution of money laundering in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to prevent, investigate and prosecute money laundering.
It lays down minimum rules on the freezing and confiscation of the instrumentalities and proceeds of crime in criminal matters.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
money laundering involving property derived from criminal offences affecting the EU’s financial interests;
the transfer, conversion, concealment or disguise of property and results in further damage than that already caused by the criminal activity, for instance by putting the property into circulation and, by doing so, concealing its unlawful origin.
EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure that the offences are punishable by effective, proportionate and dissuasive criminal penalties.
Penalties for natural persons
The legislation does not preclude criminal proceedings from being brought against natural persons who are perpetrators, inciters or accessories in any of the offences referred to in Article 3(1) and (5) and Article 4. However, they must be brought against individuals who have committed the offences in the exercise of their professional activities and who are an obliged entity within the meaning of Article 2(s) of the Directive (i.e. for example, an individual who is an agent of a criminal organisation or a legal person) and who has committed the offence in order to carry out professional activities.
In addition, the following aggravating circumstances are to be regarded as aggravating:
the offence was committed within the framework of an organised criminal organisation,
the offender is an obliging entity, or has committed it in the practice of their profession.
The offender has the right to appeal against the decision to initiate or conclude criminal proceedings, including in cases where the offender has died.
National authorities must assist each other in the widest possible way and ensure that information is exchanged in an effective and timely manner in accordance with national law and the existing EU legal framework. They must also ensure that sufficient personnel and targeted training, resources and up-to-date technological capacity are available.
Proportionate penalties
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and to the Council by 3 December 2022 assessing the extent to which the EU countries have complied with this directive. It must also inform the Commission of the number of cases where it is not possible to initiate, conclude or prosecute criminal proceedings against the offender.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The law entered into force on 3 December 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 3 November 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU is a signatory to the Convention on Laundering, Search, Seizure and Confiscation of Proceeds from Crime and on the Financing of Terrorism.
For more information, see:
‘Fighting money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Proceeds of crime: instrumentalities or proceeds of criminal activity that are used for the purpose of laundering or concealing their unlawful origin and that are the result of the transfer or conversion of goods, services, money or other property, which are the property of a person, organisation or entity that is involved in criminal activity.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EC) No 552/2014/EU of 3 November 2014 on the prohibition, investigation, prosecution and investigation of the money laundering of persons, companies, undertakings and undertakings of the Member States and repealing Council Directive 2014/42/EU (OJ L 127, 29.4.2014, pp. 39-43)
Successive amendments to Directive (Euratom) No 529/2014 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 52:
Fishing of bluefin tuna in the eastern Atlantic and the Mediterranean: rules for the conservation of Atlantic Tunas (European Union rules)
Fishing on bluefin fish: rules governing the conservation and management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/852 — rules for bluefin fishing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for fishing of large-scale pelagic longline tuna (Thunnus thynnus) in the Eastern Atlantic and Mediterranean.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the fishing of:
bluefin tuna caught by vessels over 24 metres, with the exception of the area delimited by west of 10° W and north of 42° N as well as of the Norwegian exclusive economic zone, where such fishing is permitted from 1 August to 31 January.
Large-scale longline fishing by vessels with a maximum length of 24 metres is permitted in the Atlantic Ocean and in the Mediterranean from 1 January to 31 May.
JFOs with other CPCs are not permitted.
EU countries must send a list of designated ports to the European Commission, which transmits that information to the ICCAT Secretariat.
Vessels must keep a daily logbook containing the details of all transfers carried out during the fishing season.
Catching vessels and traps must be accompanied by a written declaration with clearly written name and an ICCAT number.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a party to the International Convention for the Conservation of the Marine Environment (ICCAT) and the Convention on the Law of the Sea.
For more information, see:
Bluefin tuna fishing (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bluefin tuna: a species of fish that is the subject of a conservation convention.
* Longline fishing: fishing in which a longline line is attached to a boat.

Summary 53:
Injurious pricing: EU rules on the sale of ships below their normal value (IPI Code) (codified version)
Injunctive pricing: European system of injurious pricing for ships
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2016/849 on the enforcement of the EU’s rules on selling ships below the normal value.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) No 385/96.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ customs authorities do not discriminate against ships which are sold below their market value for commercial reasons.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
ships sold below the market price for commercial purposes (i.e. the price paid or payable for the vessel under consideration);
ships which have been delivered after the original date of sale;
ships that have been extended by more than a year.
EU countries must ensure that:
shipbuilding contracts are not terminated where the margin of the injurious price is de minimis;
contracts are terminated where there is no longer a legitimate commercial interest in the vessel.
Shipbuilding contracts may not be terminated where:
the price of the vessel is below its normal value;
the shipbuilder has not been able to meet its contractual obligations; or has not fulfilled its obligations under the contract; or
the contract has been terminated.
The European Commission may launch an investigation or proceedings against a shipbuilder if it considers that the contract is not being fulfilled.
Investigations or proceedings may be launched if:
a shipbuilding contracts have not been properly implemented;
a contract has not complied with the contract terms and conditions of the contract.
If an investigation is launched, the Commission must:
ensure the proper disclosure of the essential facts and considerations to parties which qualify for such treatment;
make available to interested parties the information they need to defend their interests;
establish a procedure for the proper allocation of costs, even in start-up situations.
Where the Commission considers that a shipbuilding contract does not comply with the conditions laid down in the IPI Code, it may initiate an investigation.
An investigation may also be opened against a vessel that has been extended beyond its normal delivery date.
Injurisdiction of the Commission
The Commission may decide to open an investigation against a contracting party if it is satisfied that the shipbuilder is not complying with the rules of the regulation. It must state the periods within which interested parties may make themselves known, present their views in writing and submit information if such views and information are to be taken into account during the investigation. The Commission may also decide to impose sanctions on the shipbuilding company if it finds that it has not applied the rules correctly.
Imposing sanctions must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. The imposition of sanctions must not be disproportionate to the amount of damage caused by the infringement of the rights of the contracting party.
Exchange rate fluctuations may lead to a change in the price of a vessel. In such a case, appropriate adjustments must be made to take into account any unreasonable effect on the injitable pricing margin due solely to exchange rate fluctuations between the original price of sale and the date of the change.
A vessel may be subject to an export price if it has been sold for less than the price it was originally paid for. The export price must be the price actually paid, payable or payable at the time of the sale.
When a vessel is sold below its market price, the customs authorities must not allow it to load or unload if it:
has not been unloaded or unloaded within the EU;
has been delayed;
was not loaded or unloading before the date on which it was sold;
had not been loaded or unloaded within the European Economic Area (EEA).
The customs authorities may also refuse to grant loading and unloading rights to vessels subject to the denial of loading and loading rights.
This regulation does not apply to vessels that have not yet been delivered.
Any shipbuilder that has extended the delivery date of a ship for commercial or technical reasons may not apply this regulation. However, if the extended delivery date was for normal commercial reasons and not to avoid the application of this regulation, it is subject to this regulation unless the vessel has been delivered within the scope of the ICIP Code.
To ensure that the Commission may impose sanctions, it must take the necessary measures, including:
imposing penalties on the vessel builder;
taking measures to impose penalties on any shipbuilder which has been found to have infringed the ICI Code;
prohibiting the export of vessels which are being sold for commercial and technical reasons;
exchanging information between the Commission and EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Regulation (EU)* No 849 has applied since 1 January 2016.
Amendments to the regulation have been incorporated into the original text



Summary 54:
Cableway installations — conformity assessment and conformity assessment requirements
Cableways — conformity assessments
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1025/2012 on the conformity assessment of cableway installations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the essential requirements for the design, manufacture, installation and testing of cableways in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that cableways are safe and that they comply with the essential safety requirements laid down in Directive 2000/9/EC on the harmonisation of technical harmonisation and standards for the carriage of passengers and goods on cableways.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
cableways designed and built for use on public roads, railways and other modes of transport, such as tramways, motorways, roads, bridges, bridges and tunnels, as well as to cableways used by the European Commission.
It does not apply to: installations which are not covered by the EU’s general safety legislation (Directive 2009/23/EC);
installations which have their vehicles running on a fixed track (such as funicular vehicles and multi-rope cable cars) which are equipped with an automatic braking device on the track, when the possibility of traction cable breaking cannot reasonably be excluded;
cables or their attachments breaking or falling;
installments which have been designed and constructed for use by the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council or the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC).
EU countries must notify the Commission and other competent bodies that carry out third-party conformity assessment tasks.
These bodies must ensure that:
the design and construction of a cableway installation meets the essential essential requirements set out in the regulation;
the installation as a whole meets the necessary essential requirements.
Manufacturers and importers must:
ensure that their products comply with all essential requirements;
provide the necessary technical documentation for the subsystem or the safety component according to Annex VIII;
make available to the Commission a single point at which the manufacturer can be contacted.
The Commission must also:
assess the safety of the subsystem and safety components of existing cableways;
conduct a safety analysis of the installation and its subsystem or safety components;
take the necessary measures to ensure the installation complies with its essential requirements and with the relevant EU safety legislation.
Notification of conformity assessment bodies
The European Commission must notify EU countries of the bodies authorised to carry out the third party conformity assessment* tasks under this regulation. These bodies must not engage in any activity that may conflict with their independence of judgement or integrity in relation to conformity assessment activities for which they are notified.
In addition, the Commission must inform EU countries and the other EU countries that the Commission has carried out a safety investigation into the installations covered by this regulation and that it has received a report on the results of the investigation.
Penalties for infringements
The penalties provided for in this regulation are effective, proportionate and dissuasive. They may include criminal penalties for serious infringements.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for persons with disabilities
Assistance and mobility for people with disabilities: EU accessibility rules
Assistive devices designed and manufactured for the use of persons who have disabilities must comply with the requirements of the European Union (EU) Accessibility Directive.
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2016/65/EU on the accessibility of products for the user of assistive devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the level of accessibility in the EU’s internal market for goods and services intended for use by persons who are blind, partially sighted or partially hearing.
It sets out minimum requirements for the design and production of products, as well as for the provision of services.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
personal computers, including desktops, notebooks, smartphones and tablets;
computer hardware systems;
mobile phones;
accessibility-related products;
assistance devices;
personal mobility devices.
Products must meet the following requirements:
the product must be designed, manufactured and marketed in such a way as to allow users to use it in a way that it is easily understood and understood by those with disabilities;
it must be compatible with assistive technologies;
all products must be able to operate in an accessible mode of communication.
The product must bear the CE conformity marking. The CE marking must be affixed visibly, legibly and indelibly to the product or to its data plate. It must indicate a single point at which the manufacturer can be contacted.
the manufacturer must ensure that the product complies with the relevant functional performance criteria.
EU countries must:
ensure that the CE marking is affixed on the product;
provide technical specifications for products that comply with this directive;
make the product available for sale on the market in a language easily understood by persons with a disability;
inform the competent authorities of the accessibility requirements and free movement of the product and of the information it contains.
Where a product does not comply with these requirements, it must be withdrawn from the market or withdrawn from circulation.
In addition to the above requirements, the directive sets out a number of specific requirements for:
products that are designed or manufactured with a view to being used by people who are partly sighted and partially hearing;
devices that are intended to be used for the purpose of mobility;
adviseive devices that are specifically designed to be adapted to the needs of people who have a disability.
Accessibility requirements
Products and services must be accessible by complying with the following functional performance requirements. These are:
functional performance criteria: the product must meet at least one or more of the following functions of the design, production and provision of products or services: the user interface, the display of information, the sound, visual and tactile relief format or sound in addition to a text warning so that blind persons can perceive it;
the user interface and display of product information;
features: the device must be easily understood, accessible and user-friendly.
Technical specifications: the technical specifications must be available in an easily understood language and must provide the user with the information they need to make the product accessible.
Support services: where available, support services (help desks, call centres, technical support, relay services and training services) must provide information on accessibility of a product and its compatibility with the product.
Consultation
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the directive. The Commission is assisted by a committee composed of representatives of the EU countries and the European Parliament and the Council.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 28 June 2016. It applies from 28 June 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a series of laws on accessibility, including:
Regulation (EC) No 261/2004 on the universal design of products (see summary);
Regulations (EC, EWG) No 1107/2006 and (EC), No 1025/2012 on the harmonisation of standards for products, and
Directives (EC and EWG), No 1177/2010 and 2011/61/EU of the Council on the free movement and marketing of goods, and repealing Directives 2002/92/EC and Directive 2011/63/EU.
For more information, see:
Accessible products (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Accessibility: the ability to use a product or a service in a particular way.
* Assistive device: a device designed and made to be put into use by a person who has a disability and which is intended for that person.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2016/35/EU
28.6.2016 on the approximation of the laws and regulations of the Member States relating to the accessibility and mobility of persons having a disability (OJ L 127, 28.5.2016, pp. 1-8)



Summary 56:
EU system for supply chain due diligence
EU system of supply-chain due-dealing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — Union system for Supply Chain Due-Dealing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a Union system to combat the supply chain link between conflict and illegal exploitation of minerals.
It aims to prevent the supply of metals to armed groups and security forces in the Democratic Republic of the Congo (DRC).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the trade in tin, tantalum and tungsten, their ores, and gold. It does not apply to recycled metals, which have undergone even further steps in the transformation process.
EU importers of metals must:
ensure that they carry out a risk assessment to assess the impact of their supply chain on the environment and human health and safety;
identify and report the results of their own supply chain checks to the competent national authorities;
report to the European Commission every 3 years on the findings of the risk assessment and the measures they have taken to prevent or mitigate adverse impacts;
assess the risks associated with their imports of metals and their response strategies.
In addition, they must ensure that they:
have a risk management system in place to identify and respond to any adverse impacts resulting from their imports;
have an independent third-party audit to check that they are carrying out their own due diligence;
carry out audits to ensure they are performing their own checks.
The European Union system also applies to:
supply chain audits of EU importers* of metals;
monitor the implementation of the EU system of due diligence.
the European Commission must keep a register of EU-wide responsible smelters and refiners.
Importers of metals are required to report to the Commission on the results they have received from their own audits and the actions taken to respond to adverse impacts.
National authorities must designate one or more competent authorities responsible for the application of the regulation. The Commission may adopt a delegated act to allow for compliance with this regulation by individual economic operators that are members of the schemes and to avoid double auditing.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULTRY TO APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat conflict and the trafficking in minerals. These include:
Directive 2014/33/EU on the prevention and control of trafficking in arms and related materiel;
Council Decision 2014/34/EU establishing a system for the control of the export of weapons and ammunition and repealing Decision 2004/8/EC (see summary).
In 2013, the Commission adopted a resolution calling on the EU to take action against the trafficking of arms and ammunition.
For more information, see:
Supply Chain Due Diligence (European Commission).

Summary 57:
Customs debt: rules for the collection and disposal of goods by the customs authorities (recast)
Customs Debt: Rules for the Collection and Disposal of Goods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 450/2008 on the collection of customs debts and the disposal of customs goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules governing the collection, disposal and disposal by customs authorities of customs debt.
It recasts and repeals the previous regulation, Regulation (EC), which had to become law in the European Union (EU) countries by 1 June 2008.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
customs debt on import and export duties;
non-originating goods;
unauthorised economic operators for customs simplifications;
the release for free circulation and temporary admission of goods.
The rules apply to all imports and exports of goods from non-EU countries to the EU, except for:
goods originating in the EU and those originating in an EU country other than the EU;
exports of goods originating in a EU country that are not subject to a customs duty.
Importers must lodge a customs declaration with the customs authority. The declaration must contain:
the goods’ name, address, date of arrival and destination;
a description of the origin and destination of the goods; and
the customs declaration’s content.
EU countries must ensure that the customs declaration is valid for at least 5 years.
Custom-related information must be provided by:
a customs declaration on the origin of the imported goods and
a summary of the customs duties and obligations;
an exit summary declaration.
In addition to the list of goods covered by the regulation, the regulation also sets out the rules on:
exchange of information between customs authorities and economic operators
the exchange of customs declarations
the payment of a customs debt
the destruction or disposal otherwise of goods
the right of economic operators to appeal against customs debts.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It applies from 1 June 2016.

Summary 58:
EU rules on intellectual property infringement
EU rules against infringement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1383/2003 on the enforcement of intellectual property rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules to combat the illegal trade in goods infringing intellectual property (IP) rights.
It aims to prevent acts in breach of the EU’s intellectual property laws.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that the competent customs department receives applications and detentions relating to infringements of IP rights;
take all necessary measures to prevent, detect, investigate and prosecute infringements;
cooperate with each other and with non-EU countries in order to prevent and combat infringements.
Implementing acts
The European Union (EU) has adopted implementing acts. These aim to:
facilitate the exchange of information between the customs authorities of EU countries;
improve cooperation between customs authorities and the European Commission;
promote the sharing of information on infringements, including information on the types of goods and persons involved.
The regulation applies to the import, export and transit of goods subject to customs supervision or customs control. It does not apply to the following:
goods that are subject to import controls or to customs control;
items that are intended for export to, or intended for, a third country;
materials that have been imported into, exported from, the EU.
In addition, it applies to goods intended for use in the manufacture, distribution or use of, or use in, or for the manufacture of, intellectual property-related products.
Customs authorities are responsible for:
monitoring the movement of goods;
detaining, seizing, seizing or confiscating the goods.
notifying the customs department of infringements
Requests for information
The competent customs authorities must notify the applicant within 10 working days of receiving a request for information. The applicant must provide the following information:
the name and address of the holder of the intellectual property right;
the date of entry into force of the decision to take action;
a description of the goods and the reasons for the decision.
If the applicant does not provide the information required, the customs authority may suspend the release of or detaining the goods, or ask the holder to provide them with any relevant information with respect to the goods before it takes any action.
Where the applicant fails to provide the required information within 10 days, it may request the applicant to supply the missing information within 3 working days.
When a customs department considers that the applicant has not supplied the information it needs, it must inform the applicant of its decision to suspend or confiscate the goods or to take any other action it considers necessary. The customs department may also suspend or detain the goods if it considers that they pose a risk to public health or safety.
Exchange of information
In order to facilitate the exchange and exchange of relevant information, EU countries must establish a centralised database of information relating to applications and detention. This database must be operational as soon as possible and not later than 1 January 2015. It must contain the information referred to in points (c), (e), (g), (h) and (i) of Article 6(3) of the regulation. The database must also contain information on:
customs controls and measures taken by customs authorities;
information on the number of persons involved in the import and export of goods, including:
name of vessel or registration of means of transport;
number of voyage/journey;
place;
freight bill or other transport document.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING ACT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of implementing acts to combat intellectual property infringements in the past. These include:
Directive 2013/13/EU on the prevention and enforcement of infringement of copyright and related rights (see summary);
Implementation of Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 596/2013 on the protection of intellectual properties (See summary).
For more information, see:
Trade in Intellectual Property Rights (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  The European Parliament and the Council of 3 July 2012 establishing a European Union system of laws and regulations relating to intellectual property.
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, EC and Euratom have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
Commission Implementing Act (Euratom) 2015/852 of 27 November 2015 establishing a system of rules and regulations concerning the enforcement and monitoring of infringments of copyright, related rights and related matters (OJ L 347, 28.11.2015, pp. 1-2)

Summary 59:
EU customs programme for cooperation in the field of customs (2020-2027)
EU customs program for cooperation on customs
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 establishing a customs programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) Customs Programme for cooperation for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation establishes the customs programme (known as the ‘Customs programme’), which aims to promote cooperation between EU countries’ customs authorities.
The programme is implemented by the European Commission and is funded by the general budget of the EU. It covers the period 2021–2027. The budget for the programme is €950 million.
Objectives
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote cooperation between customs authorities in the area of customs;
provide technical assistance for customs control equipment;
support the development and operation of a European electronic system;
encourage the exchange of information and best practice on customs control procedures and procedures;
improve the efficiency of customs control systems;
contribute to the harmonisation of customs procedures.
Cooperation actions
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It is assisted by a number of external experts, such as:
the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCA) and the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA);
the United Nations Office for Customs Cooperation (UNICEFICA);
a number of experts from non-EU countries.
Eligible actions
EU funding
The EU countries are eligible for funding under the programme, provided that they are:
financed by the EU budget;
cooperate with each other and cooperate with the Commission;
participate in multiannual work programmes for a maximum of 3 years.
Implementation and forms of EU funding
Implementing measures
The Commission may grant grants, prizes, procurement, indirect management, financial instruments, budgetary guarantees, financial assistance and the reimbursement of the external experts. It may also use other forms of Union funding, including:
grants and prizes for the purchase, maintenance and upgrade of equipment,
budget guarantees for the procurement of equipment and training;
financial assistance for the development, operation and maintenance of an IT capacity-building action.
Exemptions
The following actions are excluded from the scope of this regulation:
cooperation actions for the assessment of equipment needs or, where appropriate, training in relation to the equipment purchased;
the procurement of training for customs authorities;
exchange of information on customs procedures, procedures and best practices;
training of customs authorities for the implementation of the work programme;
research and development actions.
In addition, the programme does not apply to the following actions:
organisation, monitoring, evaluation, verification, evaluation and control;
promotion of, monitoring and evaluation of customs controls;
monitoring, assessment, verification and evaluation;
implementation of work programmes.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 11 March 2021.

Summary 60:
ESF+ (European Social Fund)
ESFocusing on social inclusion (ESF+)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/817 on the European Social Fund’s social inclusion strand
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It sets out the general rules for the European social fund (ESSF+) and the specific objectives of the ESF+ strand under shared management.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must allocate at least 25 % of their resources to the specific objective for the social inclusion policy area set out in Article 4(1), points (h) to (l), including the promotion of the socio-economic integration of third-country nationals.
The ESF is the main instrument of the European Pillar of Social Rights (ESR), which aims to promote equal opportunities and access to the labour market; fair working conditions; and social protection and inclusion. It is also the main source of financial support for the implementation of the Erasmus+ programme.
In order to achieve its objectives, EU countries must:
ensure equal opportunities for workers on a non-discriminatory basis by ensuring close cooperation of the public employment services of EU countries, the Commission and the social partners;
promote the integration of disadvantaged learners in learning mobility;
encourage the participation of women and minorities in learning and training;
support the social integration of non-EU nationals;
provide support for addressing material deprivation.
Funding and methods of implementation
The European Union funding is based on the following sources:
grants* from the general budget of the EU budget;
cofinancing from beneficiaries’ own resources, income generated by the project or financial or in-kind contributions from third parties.
co-financing for grants may be provided from beneficiaries' own resources;
other common output indicators for common result indicators* (e.g. gross domestic product (GDP) and gross national income (GNI).
The EU budget is financed by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE?

BACKGROUND

For more information, see:

KEY TERMS

Summary 61:
European Union solidarity programme for young people (EU 2020/2021)
European Union (EU2020/2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/817 on the European Union’s Youth Solidarity Programme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND THE PROGRAMME?
The regulation establishes the EU 2020/2020 programme for the participation of young people in solidarity activities.
The programme aims to encourage young people to express their solidarity in practice by participating in humanitarian aid-related activities. It also aims to promote the application of Articles 21 and 23 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights.
KEY POINTS
Participation of young Europeans in solidarity actions
The EU2020 programme sets up two strands of actions:
the ‘participation’ strand of solidarity activities, which aims to provide young people with a wide range of opportunities to participate in humanitarian-related solidarity activities and the ‘European Voluntary Humanitarian Aid Corps’, which provides volunteers with the opportunity to help with the implementation of these actions.
Activities conducted under the programme are based on the following principles:
mutual respect for human rights and the right to equality between men and women;
the right to non-discrimination based on sex, racial or ethnic origin, religion or belief, disability, age or sexual orientation, socioeconomic background and socioeconomic background;
respect for the principles of human dignity and respect for the dignity of all persons;
participation in the European Voluntary humanitarian aid corps.
the participation of individuals in the programme is open to young people of any age, regardless of their nationality, gender, nationality, nationality or place of residence.
Organisation of the programme
EU countries must designate a national agency to carry out the actions and activities. The national agencies must inform relevant target groups about the activities and activities undertaken in their respective countries.
National agencies must ensure that the actions are coordinated and coordinated in line with the objectives of the EU2020/2022 programme.
Participants must be free of any form of discrimination based on their gender, religion, belief, age, disability or socioeconomic background. They must also be free from any forms of discrimination, such as age discrimination, sexual discrimination, ageism, racial discrimination or gender-based discrimination.
In addition, EU countries must:
ensure that participants are covered by insurance coverage and, where appropriate, to the relevant clearance requirements, in accordance with applicable national law;
cooperate with each other and with non-EU countries in order to exchange experience and best practice;
provide training and mentoring to participants;
encourage participation in the activities of NGOs and other non-governmental organisations (NGOs).
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the programme. It adopts the work programmes and the calls for proposals published by the Commission. The Commission may adopt delegated acts for the duration of the Programme.
Financing
The Regulation sets out rules on grants, prizes, procurement, indirect management, financial instruments, budgetary guarantees, financial assistance and reimbursement of external experts. The programme is financed by the EU budget. The cumulative financing must not exceed the total eligible costs of the action.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The United Kingdom withdraws from the EU and becomes a third country (non-EU country) as of 1 January 2022.
For more information, see:
United Kingdom withdrawal from the Union (European External Action Service).
KEY TERMS
* Humanitarian aid: a humanitarian aid activity that aims to help people in need by providing them with information, advice and assistance.
* Volunteer: a person who is actively involved in the organisation of, or participating in, a voluntary humanitarian aid organisation.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/818 of 21 January 2019 establishing the European United Nations Programme for the Participation of Young People in Solidarity Activities (OJ L 131, 1.2.2019, pp. 1-5)

Summary 62:
Emergency Fund for the Development of Small and Medium-sized Enterprises (EGSME)
Emergency fund for the development of small and medium-sized enterprises (EGSM)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 establishing the European Fund for Employment and Social Innovation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an emergency fund to provide financial support to displaced workers and self-employed persons whose activity has ceased.
It aims to eliminate inequalities and promote equality between men and women as well as to combat discrimination based on sex, racial or ethnic origin, religion or belief, disability, age or sexual orientation.
KEY POINTS
The EGF is a European Union (EU) emergency fund that operates reactively. It is funded by the EU’s general budget.
Eligible beneficiaries are:
displaced workers* (i.e. those who have left their job or have left the labour market);
employed persons (i.) who have ceased their activity;
unemployed persons.
Beneficiaries may include:
employed workers whose activities have ceased, within the reference periods provided for in Article 4(1) to (4) of the regulation;
employed people whose activity ceased, outside the reference period.
The European Commission decides on the technical terms of the financing for the EGF.
EU countries must ensure that beneficiaries are covered by the following rules:
the EGF does not replace support measures which are available for beneficiaries within the Union funds or other EU policies or programmes, but should, where possible, complement them;
the financial contributions from the EU funds or programmes are not to be used to supplement other EU programmes or policies.
the Commission and the EU countries must take all appropriate steps to prevent any discrimination against persons based on gender, gender identity, racial, ethnic origin or religion.
In addition, the Commission and EU countries are required to ensure that:
there is no discrimination against people based on their age or disability;
there are no restrictions on access to EGF funding;
all beneficiaries are treated equally;
any irregularity in the payment of EGF funds is rectified by the European Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat inequalities and to promote equal opportunities for women and men. These include the creation of the European Employment Fund (EGF), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
For more information, see:
Equal Opportunities for Women and Men (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Displaced worker: a person who has left his or her job or has left a job because of economic or social difficulties.
* Self-employed person: an individual who is not a worker, but who has a business or has a legitimate interest in it.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 63:
Transparency register for organisations and self-employed individuals engaged in EU policy-making and policy implementation (2014-2020)
Transparency platform for organisations engaged in European Union (EU) policies
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the European Parliament and the European Commission on the transparency register
Decision 2014/852/EU on the signing, on behalf of the EU, of the agreement between the EU Parliament and of the Council on the transposition of information on the activities of organisations and individuals involved in EU policies and in the implementation of EU policies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND THE DECISION?
The agreement establishes a transposition on the EU’s transparency register for individuals and organisations that are involved in the EU's policies and policies.
The aim is to ensure that the register is up-to-date and that the information it contains is available to the public.
KEY POINTS
The register is open to all individuals, organisations and entities that have a legitimate interest in the development of EU policy. It is designed to facilitate the exchange of information between the signatory institutions and the public on EU policies, policies and programmes.
It covers all activities covered by the EU budget, such as:
public procurement,
research and development,
information exchange,
transport and dissemination,
economic policy,
financial information,
environmental policy, and
public policy.
Applicants are eligible to be entered in the register if they carry out covered activities and observe the code of conduct set out in Annex I (‘code of conduct).
).
They must submit a complete application to the register.
Annex II sets out the information that must be entered into the register, including:
the number of persons and entities involved in covered activities;
the amount and source of any EU grants contributing to their operating costs.
Intermediaries must declare the estimated total annual revenue generated that is attributable to covered activities according to the grid below.
Organisations, including intermediaries, must also declare the amount of money they have received from the EU for their activities and the sources of funding.
Registrants must also provide the following information:
their name, address and contact details;
their contact details and email address;
any other information that may be considered sensitive.
If a registrant fails to comply with these requirements, the registrant may be subject to a formal investigation.
A management board is set up to manage the register and to ensure its proper functioning. The management board meets at least annually at the initiative of its Chair and is chaired by a European Parliament or Council representative. The coordinator is responsible for the coordination of the Secretariat. The Secretariat is assisted by a representative of the Commission.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
   
The decision and the agreement entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Parliament, the Council and the Commission are the signatories to the agreement.
For more information, see:
The EU transparency register (European Parliament).
KEY TERMS
* Transparency register: a register of information that is available in the public domain.
* Intermediaries: intermediaries that provide the public with information on EU policy decisions, policies, programmes and initiatives.
MAIN DOCUMENTS

Summary 65:
European Union Recovery Instrument
European Union recovery Instrument
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 establishing the European Union recovery instrument
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s economic recovery instrument (the ‘EU Recovery Instrument’).
It aims to support the economic recovery in the aftermath of the COVID-19 crisis.
KEY POINTS
The European Union (EU) is a multiannual financial instrument designed to support economic recovery throughout the EU. It is financed by the EU budget and the EU countries’ national budgets.
The objective of the EU Recovery Instrument is to:
ensure that economic recovery is sustainable and resilient;
provide technical and administrative assistance for the implementation of the measures;
facilitate the exchange of information and expertise between EU countries;
contribute to the economic and financial recovery of EU countries.
In order to achieve these objectives, the EU provides financial support through loans to EU countries, non-repayable support, support through financial instruments or provisioning for budgetary guarantees and related expenditure by the Union.
Financing of the Instrument and allocation of funds
The EU is responsible for financing the EU programmes under the multiannually-funded EU programmes. The amount of funds to be provided under the Instrument is calculated on the basis of a fixed deflator of 2 % per year.
EU countries are responsible for the financing of the specific EU programmes that they participate in. The European Commission must prepare a report every 3 years on how the programme is being implemented. The report must also include a breakdown of the financial resources allocated to each EU country.
Non-related expenditure
The amount of € 384 400 million in 2018 prices, of the amount referred to in Article 2(1) of this regulation, will constitute external assigned revenue to the Union programmes, while € 5 600 million of that amount will constitute the amount to be used for non-recoverable support.
Financial instruments
The Instrument is financed through:
unspent EU budget allocations;
reciprocal budgetary support from the European Stability Mechanism (ESM);
non-reciprocity appropriations from the general budget of the Union;
the provision of budgetary guarantees for budgetary expenditure.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The COVID 19 crisis caused significant disturbances to economic activity which are reflected in a steep decline in gross domestic product and a significant impact on employment, social conditions, poverty and inequalities.
For more information, see:
European Commission: COVID19 recovery instrument — European Union financial instruments (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* EU budget allocation: the amount of money that the EU allocates to each country under the EU programme.
* External assigned revenue: the revenue that the European Commission receives from the Member States’ governments for the purpose of paying for the expenditure of the programmes under its budget.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) No 1046/2018 of 18 July 2018 establishing the Economic and Social Instrument (EUR-2020) (OJ L 283, 31.12.2018, pp. 1-6)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU Euratom, Euratom) No 1080/2018 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

  The European Union Recovery Fund (EU-2020-2022) of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 July 2020 establishing the EU-2020 programme for the European Regional Development Fund and repealing Regulation (EC) No 1296/2013, (EU/2014/1EU) No 1301/2013 and repealing Decision No 516/2013 of the Court of Auditors and the European Central Bank.

Summary 66:
Sanctions for budgetary surveillance in the euro area (2011-2013)
Sanctions on budgetary surveillance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2011 on sanctions for budgetary control in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out sanctions for countries that fail to comply with the budgetary framework of the European Economic and Monetary Union (EMU).
It aims to improve the enforcement of the EU’s budgetary surveillance.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro. It does not apply to:
non-euro-area countries;
euro area countries with a deficit below 3 % of gross domestic product (GDP);
euro zone countries whose deficit is above 3% of GDP; and
euro countries whose GDP is below 0.2 % of GDP.
The European Commission is responsible for carrying out surveillance missions to assess the compliance of EU countries with budgetary rules. It also carries out economic dialogue with the European Parliament, the Council, the Commission, the European Council and, where appropriate, the President of the Council or the president of the Eurogroup to discuss the implementation of the regulation.
EU countries that do not comply with budgetary surveillance must be subject to sanctions. The Commission may, on grounds of exceptional economic circumstances or following a reasoned request by the EU country concerned, recommend that the Council reduce the amount of the non-interest-bearing deposit or cancel it. It may also annul, reduce or increase the fine.
In order to ensure a fair graduation of sanctions in the preventive and corrective parts of the SGPGDP surveillance, the regulation sets out detailed rules on:
the amount of interest-bearing deposits;
the procedure to be followed by the Commission for the investigation of such actions;
detailed rules of procedure aimed at guaranteeing the rights of the defence, access to the file, legal representation, confidentiality and provisions as to timing and the collection of the fines.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 November 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU budget is governed by the Stability and Growth Pact (SGP). The SGP is designed to ensure the proper functioning of the economic and monetary union. It is based on the medium-term budgetary objective and the appropriate adjustment path towards that objective.
For more information, see:
EU budgetary surveillance (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Sanctions: sanctions imposed on EU countries when they do not respect the budgetary rules laid down in the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) which go beyond the provisions applicable to all Member States.
* Monetary surveillance: the surveillance of the economy of an EU country to determine whether it is in a position to meet its budgetary objectives and to take appropriate corrective measures.

Summary 67:
Population and housing censuses
Statistics and housing surveys
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2003 on the transmission of data subject to statistical confidentiality to the European Commission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) collects reliable, comparable and comparable statistics on the population and housing populations of the EU countries.
It sets out the essential features of the statistical data to be collected and the methodology to be used.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
total population,
locality,
household status*,
family status,
type of family nucleus,
number of family members,
size of private household,
private household size.
Conventional and sample surveys
EU countries may base the statistics on different data sources, in particular on:
conventional censuses;
register-based censuses,
sample surveys,
a combination of conventional censuses and sampling surveys;
combined surveys with rotating samples (rolling censuses).
appropriate surveys with rotating samples (rollings).
The European Commission (Eurostat) is assisted by the Statistical Programme Committee.
Programme
The Commission adopts the programme of statistical data and the metadata, as well as the technical specifications for the collection and transmission of the data.
The programme must meet, to the highest possible extent, the essential requirements of the regulation. It must include:
the establishment of the reference years (reference years;
the adoption of the programme,
the selection of data sources and the selection of the methodology;
a description of the methods used to satisfy the requirements of this regulation.
National authorities must:
ensure that the data they collect and transmit meet the essential characteristics of the relevant statistical data;
make continuous efforts to enhance compliance with those essential features.
In the event of a revision or correction in accordance with Article 4(3), EU countries must transmit the modified data to Eurostat no later than on the date of release of the revised data. They must also provide the Commission with a report on the quality of these data transmitted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 20 June 2003.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s statistical system is governed by Regulation (Euratom) No 322/97.
For more information, see:
‘Housing and population’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Conventional population: the population of a geographical area in which there is a population of at least 1,000 people.
** Sample surveys: surveys carried out on the basis of a combination of national and regional surveys.

Council Regulation (EC, EC) No 550/2003 of 20 May 2003 on the submission of data to the Commission of the European Communities of the Community of the Member States of the one part and the European Economic Area of the other part (OJ L 284, 31.10.2003, pp. 1-8)

Summary 68:
Rules of Procedure for the 9th parliamentary term
Rules of Procedures for the ninth parliamentary term (2019-2022)
SUMMARY OF:
RULES OF PROCEDURE
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATIONS?
The rules of procedure set out by the European Parliament and of the Council of the European Union (EU) are designed to ensure the smooth functioning of the EU’s legislative and decision-making bodies.
They aim to:
ensure the proper functioning of Parliament and its committees and delegations;
facilitate the exchange of information and experience between the different committees, delegations and bodies involved in the legislative process;
promote the effective functioning of European political parties and European political foundations.
KEY POINTS
The Rules of Procedure apply to the appointment of:
the President, Vice-Presidents and Quaestors of Parliament;
the Chair, the Vice-Chair and the full-time members of the Single Resolution Board of the single resolution mechanism (SRSM);
the Chairs and Executive Directors of European Supervisory Authority (European Banking Authority, European Securities and Markets Authority and European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority) and the Managing Director and Deputy Managing Director of European Fund for Strategic Investments.
Political diversity
The Conference of Presidents and the European Political Parties and European Political FOUNDATIONS (EPFOUNDATIONS) are responsible for ensuring that the political diversity of Parliament is reflected in the committees and the delegations, as well as in the decision making bodies. The EPFAs and EPFOUNDFAs have the following responsibilities:
representing the EU countries and regions;
advising the European Commission on the implementation of EU policies and programmes;
contributing to the development of the common foreign and security policy area;
providing advice to the Commission and the Council.
Diversity
EU countries and EPFs must ensure that their political diversity is represented in Parliament.
The EPFs and EPAs must:
respect the principles of gender equality and diversity;
take the necessary measures to promote the participation of women and minorities in Parliament and in decision making;
support the work of the High Level Group on Gender Equality and Diversity on 11 April 2018.
Procedure in committee
If a proposal for a legally binding act does not secure a majority of the votes cast in committee, then the committee proposes to Parliament that the proposal be rejected.
When a proposal is adopted by a committee, it must be submitted to the full Parliament for a vote. The vote must be oral and must be expressed by saying the words ‘Yes’, ‘No’ or ‘I abstain’.
Voting procedure
The following voting procedure applies to texts submitted to Parliament:
first, where applicable, voting any amendment to the proposal for legally binding acts;
second, voting on that proposal as a whole, amended or otherwise;
third, if necessary, voting to approve the proposal as an amendment;
fourth, if the proposal is amended or repealed.
In the case of a draft proposal, the committee may propose to the whole of the Parliament that it be adopted as a proposal.
If the proposal does not receive a majority, the proposal must be rejected by a simple majority of votes.
Transparency of Parliament's activities
The EU Parliament is required to publish its annual report on its activities. The report must include:
a motion for a resolution;
a draft proposal;
an explanatory statement including, where appropriate, a financial statement.
It must also include the names of its members and bureau members.
FROM WHEN DO THE RULES APPLY?
They apply from 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Parliament is governed by the rules laid down in Article 302 of the Treaty on European Union.
For more information, see:
European Parliament (European External Action Service).
MAIN DOCUMENTS

Council Regulation (EEC) No 552/2014 of 22.11.2014 laying down the rules of procedures for the Ninth Parliamentary term (OJ L 302, 22.12.2014, pp. 1-5)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 529/2014 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 70:
EU’s own resources — financing the COVID-19 crisis and COVID 2020-2027 (OJ L 129, 1.7.2020, pp. 1-11)
EU‘s own resource system
EU's own resources system
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU, Euratom) No 609/2014 establishing a system of EU own resources
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) system of own resources to cover annual appropriations for payments.
It sets out the rules for the collection and management of EU countries’ own resources.
KEY POINTS
The decision sets out:
a maximum amount that the European Commission may annually call from a Member State to cover its own resources;
a minimum amount of €1.4 billion that the Commission may call from each Member State.
The Commission may also call on the Member States to provisionally provide the difference between the overall assets and the cash resource requirements, in proportion (‘pro rata’) to the estimated budget revenue of each of them.
A Member State which fails to honour a call must remain liable to honour it.
In addition, the Commission is empowered to borrow funds on capital markets on behalf of the EU for the sole and exclusive purpose of financing measures to address the consequences of COVID–19 crisis.
EU countries are free to take the most suitable measures to achieve those goals, in line with the principle of subsidiarity.
Each year, EU countries must:
collect their own resources and make them available to the Commission;
provide the Commission with a balance between the total amount of revenue and the amount of cash they have available.
Collecting own resources:
the Commission must draw up detailed rules for calculating and budgeting the balance of the balance, as well as the arrangements and arrangements necessary for controlling and supervising the collection.
Borrowing: the Commission can borrow up to €2.5 billion from the European Central Bank (ECB) or from the national central banks of Member States. The amount is calculated as a percentage of the sum of the gross national income (GNI) of all Member States, divided by the number of inhabitants of each.
Collection: the EU country must provide the balance between its total revenue and its cash resources. The Commission must calculate the balance in accordance with the rules laid down in the regulation.
Carry-over of surplus: any surplus of the Union's revenue over total actual expenditure during a financial year is carried over to the following financial year.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
From 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The COVID‑19 crisis led to the creation of the European Union's own resource structure.
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Decision (EC) No 608/2014 of 21 December 2014 establishing a System of EU Member States’ Own Resources
1.6.2014
2.1.2016
OJ C 130 of 1 January 2014
See consolidated version.

Summary 71:
EU rules on the labelling of tyres for heavy-duty vehicle tyres
EU rules governing the EU’s road haulage sector
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/858 — labelling requirements for tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on how tyres should be labelled to reduce the external rolling noise (external rolling noise) emitted by heavy‐duty vehicles.
It aims to encourage end-users to purchase tyres with lower external rolling noise by providing them with harmonised information on the tyre class and measurement values.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
re-treaded tyres;
traffic noise;
vehicle tyres; and
other essential parameters such as fuel efficiency and other essential parameters.
EU countries must ensure that:
the tyre label of a tyre type is clearly visible on the vehicle.
The tyre label must contain the following information:
type of tyre;
class;
number of classes;
internal rolling noise measured value;
wet grip index;
the number of tyres tested.
If a tyre does not meet the requirements, it must be replaced by a new type that meets the requirements.
In addition, the regulation sets out a verification procedure to check that the tyre label complies with the regulation. The test results must be sent to the relevant national authority. The authority must verify that the tyres comply with this regulation. If the measured values meet the declared classes or the declared internal rolling noise value within the verification tolerances referred to in Annex II of the regulation, it may issue a non-binding conformity assessment number (NOMA).
The European Commission may adopt delegated acts to amend the regulation in the light of technical progress. The regulation repeals Regulation (EC) No 1222/2009 with effect from 1 May 2021.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
From 1 May 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce traffic noise in the EU. These include:
Directive 2019/18/EU on the harmonisation of the rules on road haulier tyres and their trailers (see summary);
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/857 on the conformity assessment of road hauliers’ tyres and on their trailers;
Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 552/2009 and (EU), Euratom and Euratom of the European Parliament and of the Council of 25 November 2009 on the classification of tyres with respect to fuel efficiency, and on the testing of tyre types.
For more information, see:
Road haulier tyre labelling (European Commission).

Summary 72:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2019/1371 on the European Union Customs Union Instrument
Im implementing Regulation (EUR) 2020/1370 on the implementation of Regulation (EC) No 952/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council on the Union Customs Code
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets up the EU Customs Code (EU Customs Code) Instrument.
It aims to improve the effectiveness and efficiency of customs controls at EU borders.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all goods entering and exiting the EU’s external borders. It covers:
goods entering the customs territory of the EU;
customs controls with equivalent results;
equipment used to carry out customs controls.
The instrument is implemented through work programmes as referred to in Article 110(2) of the Financial Regulation. It is funded by the EU budget.
Implementation
The Commission implements the instrument under direct management in accordance with the Financial Regulations. The work programmes are designed to:
ensure the interoperability of the customs control equipment purchased with the support of EU programmes and instruments, and therefore its efficient use;
contribute to adequate and equivalent results of customs checks;
provide the Commission with information and communication actions relating to the instrument, to actions taken pursuant to the Instrument and to the results obtained.
Funding
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the work programmes. The Commission is assisted by the European External Action Service (EEAS) and the European Public Prosecutor's Office (EPPO).
the Commission sets up a coordination mechanism to ensure that the equipment purchased under the instrument is interoperable and that it is used effectively.
EU funding
The work programme covers more than one budgetary year, but not more than three. The total cost of the work programme must not exceed the total eligible costs of the action.
Equal treatment and transparency
The EU budget is protected by the principle of non-discrimination. In addition, the Commission must regularly provide information to the public and the public bodies, such as the public authorities, on the instrument and its actions and results.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The customs union is one of the cornerstones of the Union, which is the largest trading blocks in the world. However, there is a serious imbalance in the number of border crossing points in the EU. This imbalance is due to differences between EU countries both in terms of their geographic features, and their capacities and resources.
For more information, see:
EU customs code (European Commission).

Summary 73:
Distribution system operators and energy storage: energy efficiency and energy efficiency (recast of Directive 2012/27/EU)
Distribution System Operators and Energy Storage: Energy efficiency and Energy Efficiency
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/943 on energy efficiency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the efficiency of the EU’s electricity market by:
ensuring the efficient operation of the distribution system and the efficient use of energy resources;
encouraging the use of distributed energy resources such as energy storage;
improving the transparency of the market for energy efficiency.
It recasts and repeals the original electricity directive from 2012.
KEY POINTS
Electricity distribution system operators (DSOs) must:
operate their networks in a non-discriminatory and transparent manner;
ensure that they have the necessary resources to efficiently operate their networks and to avoid costly network expansions;
provide services such as switching, switching, demand response, energy storage and other services.
They must also provide consumers with information on their bills and billing information free of charge.
The directive sets out minimum requirements for the transmission system operator and the energy storage operator.
Transmission system operators must be able to:
use energy resources, such as renewable energy sources and storage facilities, to operate and manage their networks;
use services from distributed energy sources such as demand response;
have the capacity to switch, switch, manage and operate energy storage.
Providers of energy storage must have the capacity necessary to operate their systems.
providers of electricity must be authorised by the national regulatory authorities.
Regulation (EU countries must set up a single point of contact for consumers.
EU countries may also appoint a supplier of last resort.
In addition, EU countries must ensure that:
the transmission system operators are:
cooperating in the transmission market with producers;
operating with producers, including those offering demand response through aggregation, to participate alongside producers in all electricity markets;
participating in energy markets in all EU countries.
National authorities must ensure:
that:
transmission system users have access to the system user authorisation procedure;
the system user who has been refused access can make use of a dispute settlement procedure.
Authorisation procedures must be fair and transparent. They must not lead to administrative burdens that are disproportionate to the size and potential impact of the producers.
If a transmission system user is refused access, it must be informed of the reasons for the refusal and the dispute settlement procedures available to it.
An independent compliance officer must be appointed by the European Commission. The Commission may appoint a compliance officer to carry out inspections, including unannounced inspections, at the premises of the regional coordination centres and to issue joint binding decisions on the regional coordinating centres.
A national authority must ensure the following:
all transmission system users receive all their billing information;
all final customers receive all billing information, including:
billings and billing details;
billing information for electricity services, including switching and switching services;
billing information for energy storage, including billing information for demand response.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The original directive was adopted in 2012 and had to become law in the EU countries by 2012. It was amended in 2013.
For more information, see:
Energy efficiency (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Distribution system operator: the person or body responsible for the operation of a distribution system.
* Energy storage operator: a company or body that manages, operates, manages or operates energy storage facilities.

Summary 74:
European Partnerships for the development of world-class supercomputing systems (EU’s high-performance computing and quantum computing services) (EU-BDVA)
European Partnersions for the Development of World-Class Supercomputing and Quantum Computing Services (EU) (DAIRO)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 establishing a European Partnerships Joint Undertaking
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
The regulation establishes a European Partnership (EPSJ) for the European Union (EU).
The purpose of this regulation is to establish a European partnership (EPJ) between the EU, EU countries and non-EU countries to develop and operate the European High Performance Computing (HPC) and Quantum Computers (QC) service and data infrastructure.
The EPSJ is funded by Horizon Europe, the European Research and Innovation Fund (ERIF), the Connecting Europe Facility (CEF) and the European Investment Bank (EIB). It can pool resources from the EU and EU countries’ contributions paid to Horizon Europe and from the Digital Europe Programme or the connecting Europe Facility and the private sector. It can launch research and innovation programmes for developing European technologies and their subsequent integration in world-leading supercomputers.
KEY POINTS
The EPJ consists of:
a governing board composed of representatives of the Union and of the participating countries;
the Executive Director of the EPJ;
members’ financial contributions to the operational costs;
a scientific and technological advisory board;
groups of national experts and trainees.
It is governed by an Industrial and Scientific Advisory Board and an Infrastructure Advisory Group.
the Governing Board decides on the allocation of access time for the users of commercial services, taking into account the outcome of the monitoring referred to in Article 17(11) of the Statutes.
EU financial contributions
The EU financial contribution is used for capability building across the whole EU, including the acquisition, upgrade and operation of High Performance Computers, quantum computers or quantum simulators, the federation of the high-performing computing and QC services and the widening of its use, and development of advanced skills and training.
Non-EU contributions are not subject to State aid rules.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Partnership is part of Horizon Europe.
For more information, see:
‘EU-BQC’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* High performance computing: a system that is capable of processing, analysing, processing and analysing high-quality data in a very fast and efficient way. It consists of a number of components, such as a processor, a memory, a processor and a memory subsystem. It is the most advanced computing system in the world. It has the capability to process, analyse, analyse and analyse data in very high-speed and efficient ways. It also has the ability to process and analyse the results of scientific and technical research.
Quantum computing: the use of a high-powered supercomputer for the generation, processing, analysis and analysis of data. It enables the development, analysis, simulation and simulation of high-precision applications in the field of information and communication technologies. It allows for the creation of a global network of high performance computing services and data networks.

Summary 75:
EU 2020 climate action plan: carbon capture and storage (CCS)
EU 2020 strategy for climate change
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1119 on the EU’s climate-neutrality objective
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU meets its long-term climate change goal of limiting warming to 1.5 °C by 2020.
It sets out the objectives and measures to achieve this goal.
KEY POINTS
The EU is committed to stepping up efforts to tackle climate change and to delivering on the implementation of the Paris Agreement adopted under the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (the ‘Paris Agreement’). The EU has adopted a climate action strategy for the period 2020-2040. This strategy aims to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and to reduce the likelihood of extreme weather events and of reaching tipping points.
To achieve this, the EU must:
reduce its greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions to below 2 % by 2020;
ensure that its economy is carbon-neutral by 2030;
take action to reduce its GHG emissions by 20 % by 2050.
The regulation sets out:
the EU 2020 climate target, which is based on the greenhouse gas emission reduction target set out in the Paris agreement;
the target for the EU 2040 greenhouse gas budget, which includes the emission reduction of 0.5 % of GHG by 2020 and the emission reductions of 1 % by 2040;
carbon removals (carbon capture, storage and transportation (CSPs) by 2030.
EU countries must also commit to reducing their GHGCO2 emissions by at least 1 % of their total GHGDPs by 2020, and by 1 % in 2030. This is without prejudice to the review of the relevant EU legislation in order to enable the EU to achieve the target.
In addition, the regulation also sets out a framework for the certification of carbon removal and verification of carbon accounting, while ensuring that there are no negative impacts on the environment, in particular biodiversity, on public health or on social or economic objectives.
A Commission Advisory Board is composed of 15 senior scientific experts covering a broad range of relevant disciplines. It advises the Commission and the European Parliament and the Council on the formulation of the EU climate strategy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It applies from 11 March 2020. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 11 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission adopted the EU 2020 Climate Action Plan in December 2018. It also provided initial guidance on its implementation.
For more information, see:
‘Climate action plan for a cleaner and more competitive Europe’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EC) No 401/2009 of 11 December 2009 on the achievement of the climate objective of the European Union (Paris Agreement) (OJ L 283, 11.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, 2018/842 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 76:
Third Programme for the Union’s action in the field of health (2014-2020)
Third programme for the EU's action on the health sector
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2018/1046 on the third programme in the area of health
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the framework for the third European Union (EU) health programme.
It aims to support actions that foster the production, procurement and management of crisis-relevant products within the EU to mitigate the risk of shortages, while ensuring complementarity with other Union instruments.
KEY POINTS
The programme is funded by the general budget of the EU. It covers the period 2014-2020.
EU countries and participating countries have designated national focal points to assist the Commission in the promotion of the programme, where relevant, in the dissemination of its results and the information available on its impact in the Member States and countries.
The Commission adopts delegated acts to implement the programme. The Commission may adopt delegated acts in the light of the specific circumstances of a programme with substantial implications.
Eligible actions
Actions covered by the programme are those that:
contribute to the prevention and control of the spread of communicable diseases;
promote the prevention, control and prevention of epidemics;
improve preparedness and response planning for health emergencies;
provide technical assistance and training for health professionals;
ensure the exchange of information on health-related products and services;
facilitate the exchange and exchange of best practice;
support the development of information and best practice in the development and dissemination of best practices;
foster the production and procurement of health-relevant goods and services.
Procurement in health emergency situations
The European Commission is responsible for the procurement of the products covered by this programme and the management of the emergency situation.
In addition, the programme provides financial support for actions that promote:
the production of products with a high level of Union added value;
the exchange of knowledge and experience;
improving the quality of health information and training;
contributions to research and development;
enhancing the capacity of EU countries to respond to health emergencies.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The third programme is part of the European Union's general budget.
For more information, see:
‘Health’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Emergency situation: a situation where there is a risk of a shortage of a specific product or service.
