Summary 78:
European Institute of Innovation and Technology (EIT) 2021-2027 strategic plan for the seven-year period 2021 to 2027 (SIA 2021-2020)
European Institute OF INNOVATION AND TECHNOLOGY (Eit) 2021 - 2027 strategy for the seventh-year of its existence
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/695 establishing the strategy and the priorities for the European institute of innovation and technology
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE STRATEGIC AGENDA?
The decision sets out the priority fields and the strategy for research and innovation (R&I) activities conducted by the European Institute for Innovation & Technology (the EIT).
The strategy, which is based on a multiannual grant agreement, aims to establish the EIT’s key actions and to assess its impact on the social, economic and environmental impact, its outreach activities and its capacity to generate best innovation added-value.
KEY POINTS
The EIT is funded by a budget of €2.8 billion (96 % of the total EIT budget) which is to be distributed to the European Innovation Innovation Centre (KICs). The KICs are to develop and implement revenue-creating strategies in order to maintain their innovation ecosystem and knowledge triangle activities beyond the period covered by the grant agreements. The EIT will also provide financial support for the research and development activities of the KIC.
The KIC on WMM will establish the strongest possible synergies with relevant EU policy initiatives as well as with relevant UN initiatives and the SDGs, in particular SDGs such as SDGs and the Sustainable Cities and Communities, in the area of clean water and sustainable cities and communities, and with relevant international initiatives such as the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development (SDGs) and SDG 13 ‘Life below Water’.
A total of €1.5 billion will be allocated to the RIS, which will support research, innovation and technological development (R & T) activities in the areas of education, training and entrepreneurship. The RIS will be based on sound methodological evidence with eight capacity development areas: Leadership and Governance; Digital Transformation; Organisational Capacity; Entrepreneurial Teaching and Learning; Preparing and Supporting Entrepreneurs; Knowledge Exchange; Internationalisation; and Measuring Impact.
In addition, a maximum of 3 % will be dedicated to a pilot higher education initiative of three years.
Funding for the RIS will come from the European Research and Innovation Fund (ERIF) and the European Investment Bank (EIB) and will be divided equally between the RIS and EIT.
Support for cross-KIC activities will be provided by the EIB, the EIF and the EU countries’ governments.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The European Innovation Centre is one of the European Union's (EU's) seven innovation institutes. It is based in Brussels and is responsible for the development and implementation of the EU's innovation ecosystem.

Summary 79:
Protection of certain aromatised wine products — rules for their presentation, presentation, labelling and labelling (Regulation (EU) No 1169/2011) (OJ L 10, 12.2.2011, pp. 1-26)
Protection and labels of certain aromatic wine products
Protected wine products: rules for the presentation, description, presentation and protection of geographical indications of certain products
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules applicable to the definition, description and presentation of aromatisation products*.
It aims to ensure that these products comply with the EU’s general rules on the protection of wine products, while taking into account traditional production methods.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
aromatised fortified wine-based drink (a wine which has been added to alcohol to increase its alcoholic strength by more than 7% and which is obtained from white wine under the same conditions as for Sangría/Sangria;
an aromatic wine produced in a more restricted region (e.g. Chardonnay);
wine produced in an area of the Alps or in the Pyrenees.
It does not apply to the presentation or labelling of products which are produced in other EU countries.
Products may not be labelled as ‘aromatised wines’ or ‘wine-based drinks’.
The European Commission may, by means of implementing acts, adopt delegated acts, decide to adopt a geographical indication (GID) for a specific category of product. The GID may also be used for other categories of products.
Existing geographical designations
The Commission may adopt implementing acts to supplement the GID for products which fall within the scope of the regulation. These may include:
the designation ‘Sangría’;
the name of the EU country of production or of a restricted region;
a description of the production processes and methods of analysis for aromatising wine products; and
the information to be provided in the product specification with regard to the link between the geographical area and the final product.
Trans-border applications
EU countries may submit applications for protection to the Commission. The Commission must examine the application for protection in order to verify whether it meets the conditions set out in this regulation. If it considers that the product does not meet the conditions for protection, it may reject the application.
If an application is rejected, the Commission may:
take all necessary measures related to the protection;
make the decision on protection or rejection available to the public;
checks and verification to be carried out by the EU countries, including testing.

Summary 80:
Conservation of European Union (EU) fish stocks
Conservation and conservation measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1380/2013 on the conservation and conservation of EU fishing stocks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s fishing stocks for the period 2014-2020.
It aims to ensure that the conservation of fish stocks in EU waters is maintained, taking into account scientific, technical and economic advice, including, where relevant, reports drawn up by the Scientific, Technical and Economic Committee for Fisheries (STECF) and other advisory bodies, as well as any advice received from advisory councils.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the following vessels:
Union fishing vessels;
third-country vessels in Union waters;
non-EU fishing vessels in non-EU waters.
Maximum fishing quotas are set out in Annex II to the regulation.
EU countries must not exceed 9 % of the quota.
Fishing, farming and fattening capacity limitations
The European Commission must ensure that:
fisheries are not overfished;
fishing and farming capacities are not exceeded;
there are no limits on the number of fish caught;
the catch-and-release practice and the bag limits are maintained.
The Commission must take all necessary measures to prevent overfishing.
In addition, the Commission must:
ensure that fishing vessels comply with the conservation measures laid down in the regulation;
take the necessary scientific and technical advice;
not exceed the quota;
monitor and report to the Commission on the implementation of the measures taken;
make recommendations to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the European Commission on conservation and sustainable fishing.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 May 2014.
BACKGROUND
   Â 
The EU fisheries system is based on the principle of sustainable fishing and conservation. It is designed to ensure the long-term sustainability of the EU fishing sector.
For more information, see:
EU fishing stocks (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Fishing stocks: fish stocks are defined as the total number of species caught in a given to each EU country.

Summary 81:
EU macro-financial assistance to Ukraine
EU macro–financial assistance for Ukraine
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2018/852 on the European Union’s economic and financial assistance for the stabilisation of Ukraine
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the EU to provide financial assistance to the Ukrainian authorities in order to help the country stabilise its economy.
KEY POINTS
The decision authorises a financial assistance programme of up to €2.8 billion for the period 2018-2022.
The assistance is provided under a Memorandum of Understanding (MOU) with the International Monetary Fund (IMF).
The European Commission is responsible for managing the assistance. It is assisted by a committee composed of the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EBRD).
Under the terms of the MOU, the assistance is to be provided in two equal instalments: €1.5 billion for 2018 and €2 billion for 2019. The first instalment is due to be released in November 2018. The second is due in June 2022.
In order to ensure uniform conditions for the implementation of the assistance, the European Commission and the Council of the EU have conferred powers on the Commission to:
decide on the disbursement of the financial assistance, subject to the fulfilment of certain conditions;
ensure that the economic policy and financial conditions are met;
implement the programme;
monitor the progress made by the authorities;
provide the European institutions with the relevant documents;
take decisions on the release of the loans.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DECISION APPLY?

Summary 82:
Fishing of swordfish in the Mediterranean: EU control system for ensuring compliance with the rules of the common fisheries policy (2018-2022)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — EU control measures for the conservation of Mediterranean swordfish
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU-wide rules on the conservation and management of the swordfish (Swordfish) caught and landed in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that swordfish fishing vessels comply with the EU’s rules on fishing and catch;
take swordfish within their annual fishing quotas and inform the European Commission of the number and type of fish caught, retained on board and landed;
report to the ICCAT Secretariat the number, type and quantity of fish they have caught, landed and landed.
Each year, EU countries must transmit a list of designated ports to the Commission. The Commission must send that information to the Secretariat by 30 April, 30 July and 30 October of each year and by 30 January of the following year.
By-catch
Each EU country must inform the Commission when transmitting its annual fishing plan in accordance with Article 9 of the EU rules on by-catch. This information must include:
the number of fish it has caught;
the length (LJFL) and live weight (live weight) of each swordfish caught; and the date and port of landing or transhipment;
details of the fishing vessel and the number of vessels involved;
fishing authorisations issued;
records of the total number of fishing vessels registered in the EU.
Inspectors must be able to carry out their duties safely and securely.
Vessels must be accompanied by a model identity card. The card must contain the vessel's name and Union fleet register number (CFR) as defined in Annex I to Commission Implementing Regulation (EC) 2017/218 (see summary).
The Commission must transmit the information on vessels to the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT) by 30 June each year. It must also send the information to ICCAT by 30 July, 30 October and 30 January the following the end of the calendar year and send it to the secretariat.
Monitoring and reporting
The European Commission must collect and report data on:
fisheries and catches;
by-catch;
catch data, including length, live weight and number of live fish landed; and
recreational fishing gear.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation applies from 15 February 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a party to the Convention on International Law of the Sea (CITES) and is responsible for managing its fisheries.
For more information, see:
Swordfish (European Commission).

* By-catch: the catch of fish that is by-caught in the context of a fishing plan.
* Swordfish: swordfish that is caught by a fishing vessel for commercial purposes.

Summary 83:
Multiannual financial framework (MFF) for the period 2014-2020 (EU-28) (European Commission)
Multiiannual Financial framework for the years 2014-2020 (European Union)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 28/2014 on the multiannual budgetary framework
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s financial framework, known as the ‘MFF’.
It aims to provide the EU with a consistent and predictable financial framework for a period of 7 years starting on 1 January 2014.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The MFF covers the period from 2014 to 2020. It covers:
the general budget of the EU and Euratom;
the European Social Fund (ESF), the European Investment Bank (ESB) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERASMUS);
the EU budget;
budgetary arrangements for the EU countries;
other special instruments.
The European Commission is responsible for drawing up and implementing the MFF. The European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions have the power to adopt the budget.
Categories of expenditure
The EU budget covers the following categories of expenditure:
general budget:
spending on the general budget, including expenditure resulting from any relevant decision taken unanimously by the Council after consulting the European Parliament and the Council;
revenue from the European External Action Service (Euratom);
all expenditure and revenue of the European Union (EU);
spend on the European social security system (ESSESF);
other expenditure, such as:
revenues from the ESF;
recurring expenditure from the EEA;
administrative expenditure (e.g. social security and pensions);
reciprocal expenditure (spending of EU countries’ contributions to the EU budget).
The budget also includes:
investment appropriations (i.e. payments made by EU countries to the European Commission for the implementation of EU policies and programmes;
contributions made by the Commission to EU countries for the financing of their own budgets;
spontaneous expenditure (including payments from EU countries);
contribution payments (including contributions from the Commission);
administration payments (in the form of grants, loans and grants to the Commission).
Special instruments
The Commission may use special instruments such as the Emergency Aid Reserve (EASR), which is designed to provide financial support to countries in the event of serious financial difficulties. It may be mobilised only in relation to an amending or annual budget. The EASR may also be used for specific policy objectives relating to youth employment, research, ERASMUs in particular for apprenticeships, small and medium-sized enterprises, and Small and Medium-sized Enterprises.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 3 July 2014. It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1927/2006.

Summary 84:
European Maritime, Fisheries and Aquaculture Fund (EMFAF)
European Maritime and Fisheries Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 — EMFAF
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s financial rules for the implementation of the European Maritime Fund for the period from 1 January 2021 to 31 December 2027.
KEY POINTS
The European Maritime Facility for the Faroes, Madeira, Guadeloupe, French Guiana, Martinique, Mayotte, Réunion, Saint-Martin and Saint-Pierre-et-Miquelon (EMF) is a multiannual EU financial instrument. It aims to support the sustainable development of the Faroe Islands, the Azores and Madeira and the Canary Islands in the fishing sector.
The EMF is managed by the European Commission. It is funded by the EU budget.
It is managed and administered by the Commission.
Funding
Each EU country must allocate, within its EU financial support set out in Annex V, at least:
EUR 102 000 000 for the islands located in the outermost regions of the Atlantic Ocean;
EU financial support of at least €82 000 for Madeira; and
EU funding of €131 000 for:
the Canary Islands;
the Azores.
EU countries must ensure that:
fisheries are not overfished;
fishing activities are sustainable;
there is no overfishing.
Fisheries in the Mediterranean Sea of Azores are sustainable.
There are specific eligibility rules for investments in fishing vessels, such as:
increase in the gross tonnage of a fishing vessel to improve safety, working conditions or energy efficiency;
increased in the number of fishing vessels in the fleet;
improvement in the environmental, economic, social and employment aspects of the fishing fleet.
In addition, the EMF must:
ensure that vessels are equipped with the necessary equipment and equipment to meet the requirements for sustainable fishing;
avoid and reduce, as far as possible, unwanted catches and minimising the negative impact of fishing activities on the marine ecosystem.
Eligible investments are:
investments in the marine environment;
investment in marine research and development;
research and development of marine technologies;
reciprocal access to fishing vessels.
Financial management
The Commission may adopt implementing acts to define the cases of non-compliance with the payment deadline. The Commission may also adopt delegated acts to deal with the non-fulfilment of payment deadlines. The EMF has a budget of EUR 6 108 000 000 in current prices.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force
1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
The EU has adopted a common fisheries policy, known as the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP), which aims to restore and maintain populations of harvested species above levels which can produce sustainable yield (MSY) and to minimise the negative impacts of fishing on marine ecosystems.
For more information, see:

KEY TERMS
* Sustainable yield: the amount of MSY that a fishery can produce in a given year.
* Fishing vessel: a vessel designed and built to carry out commercial fishing activities.

Summary 85:
Convention on the conservation and management of straddling fish stocks and highly migratory fish stocks (Obligations Regulation)
Convention of the United Nations Convention on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish Stocks and Highly Migratory Fish Stock
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1005/2008 establishing a Community system to prevent, deter and eliminate illegal, unreported and unregulated fishing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the conservation, management and control of species of fish stocks covered by the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES) and the Agreement for the Implementation of that Convention relating to the Conservation, Management and Regulation relating to:
Conservation and management measures for species covered by CITES
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels flying the flag of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The convention covers:
fishing in the Pacific Ocean between the 50°S parallel from its intersection with meridian 150°W until the intersection with Meridian 130°W;
fisheries in the Indian Ocean from 1 January to 31 December of the previous year.
EU countries must report to the Commission the trade data collected by their authorities each year for bigeye tuna and hammerhead sharks caught and released;
the number and status (dead or alive) of silky sharks, hammerhead shark and bigeye sharks caught, frozen and released.
Vessels and purse seine vessels flying their flag that were actively fishing in the convention area must record the number of silkies and hammerheads caught and the year before the regulation came into force.
The European Commission must submit a report every 5 years to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of the convention.
Fishing vessels must:
ensure that all reasonable steps are taken to ensure its safe release;
report the incident to the EU country concerned, including the number and details of how and why the encirclement happened, where it occurred, steps taken to guarantee safe release, and an assessment of the life status of the animal(s) on release (including whether any were released alive but subsequently died).
Observers on EU fishing vessels must record:
number and status* of the silky shark caught and fished;
number of hammerheads and silkies caught, fished and frozen;
weight of the fish caught;
total number and weight of the fished fish.
In addition, EU countries must notify the International Association for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources (IATTC) of the number, weight and status of all the fish they have caught, caught or fished in the Convention area. The Commission must forward that information to the IATTC Secretariat by 30 June.
Monitoring of fishing vessels
The Commission must collect data on:
total catches and fattened fish caught by vessels flying its flag;
captures of fished bigeye fish;
trafficking in live sea turtles;
unfished sea turtles caught by purse seines;
recovered sea turtles that have been illegally caught and frozen.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
The Convention of 10 December 1982 on the Law of the Sea (OJ L 286, 29.10.1982, pp. 1-5)

Summary 86:
Delegated and implementing acts — Common Understanding between the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission on Delegated Delegations (2016-2020 Common Understanding)
European Parliament, Council and Commission common understanding on delegated and implementing* acts
European Union (EU) Member States have agreed on a common understanding establishing a common set of tools for better law-making.
SUMMARY OF:
Common Understanding on delegated acts and on the related standard clauses
Decision 2016/852/EU on the adoption of delegated acts
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE AGREEMENT?
The decision and the agreement aim to improve the efficiency of the EU’s legislative and implementing processes.
The agreement establishes a common approach to the drafting, adoption and evaluation of EU legislation. It aims to ensure that EU legislation is more efficient, effective, relevance, coherence and value added.
KEY POINTS
Scope and general principles
The three institutions agree to:
ensure consistency and coherence in organising their work to evaluate the performance of Union legislation;
encourage the participation of the public and stakeholder in the drafting and implementation of EU law;
promote the exchange of information between the Commission and the Member States’ experts on EU legislation and its impact assessments;
take account of the views expressed by the EU countries at each stage of the legislative process;
cooperate in the preparation of draft delegated acts.
Delegislative procedure
The Commission adopts delegated acts in line-by-line. It adopts a delegated acts on the basis of a proposal from the Commission. It then consults the Member State concerned. The Commission then adopts the delegated acts with the support of the national authorities.
Implementing acts
The delegated acts adopted by the Commission must be accompanied by a report on the implementation of the delegated act. The report must include a summary of the measures taken and the results of public consultations and feedback on the draft act. It must also include a description of the conditions under which the Commission may adopt a delegated act and the reasons for the use of the urgency procedure (i.e. the need to avoid undue delays in that process). The Commission may also adopt delegated acts without the consent of the Council or the Parliament.
Public consultation and feedback
Before adopting a proposal, the Commission will conduct public consultations in an open and transparent way, ensuring that the modalities and time-limits of those public consultations allow for the widest possible participation. This will include public internet-based consultations.
National authorities must be consulted in a timely manner on each draft delegated act prepared by the commission services. They must also be consulted on the conclusions of the Commission services’ evaluations of existing legislation and policy.
Transparency and coordination
The European Commission must ensure that national authorities’ information and communication systems are in place to ensure the transparency and coordination of the work carried out by delegated acts, as well as the coordination of information and communications.
Empowerment
A delegated act may be bundled if there is a substantive link between two or more empowerments contained in a single legislative act, and unless the legislative act provides otherwise, it must be bundled.
If the Commission considers that a legislative act does not meet the criteria for being bundled, it may not adopt it.
In this context, it is important to note that the Commission is empowered to adopt delegated powers in accordance with Article 155(2) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and that the powers conferred on the Commission are subject to the same conditions as those applicable to the other institutions.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Decision and agreement entered into force on 13 April 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures aimed at improving the efficiency, effectiveness, relevance and value-added of its legislation. These include:
the European Commission work programme and interinstitutional programming;
the implementation of its work programme.
For more information, see:
Better lawmaking (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Decision (EC) No 852/2016/EU of 13.4.2016 concerning the adoption, on behalf of the Union, of a common agreement between the Council, the European Commission, and the European Atomic Energy Community (OJ L 347, 13.8.2016, pp. 1-8)
Common Agreement between the Parliament, of the one part, the EU Member States and the Council of 13 March 2016 on the adopting, adopting, amending or amending of delegated or implementing acts in Union law.
Successive amendments to Decision 2016/884/EU have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiatives for the implementation of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) and the EU’s common foreign and security policy (CFSP)
Citizens' initiatives for implementing the Treaty of Lisbon
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2011 on the submission of proposals for a legal act of the Union for the purpose of implementing the Treaties similar to the right conferred on the European Parliament under Article 225 of the TFEU and on the Council under Article 241 of the TFEU.
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for citizens’ organisations to submit proposals to the European Commission for the adoption of legal acts to implement the treaties of the EU.
It aims to improve the transparency of the legal acts adopted by EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Organisers must:
be citizens;
be of the age to be entitled to vote in elections to the EU Parliament and the Council;
have at least seven members who are residents of at least 7 different EU countries;
form a citizens' committee consisting of representatives from at least three EU countries and at least one representative from each EU country.
The Commission must establish a point of contact which provides information and assistance.
EU countries must ensure that the organisers collect statements of support within a specific time limit. They must also:
use the online collection system to collect the statements;
ensure that the statements are signed by at least 750 people;
verify the validity of the statements.
use one of the personal identification numbers/personal identification document numbers, as specified in the regulation, as set out in the annex to the regulation.
Organisations may also submit a draft legal act to the Commission. The Commission must inform the other EU countries of any draft legal acts it receives.
Registration of a proposed citizens' initiative
The organisers must register their initiative in the national register of the competent authority of the country in which the initiative is registered. The number of signatories must correspond to the number of Members of the Parliament elected in each EU State, multiplied by 750. The organisers must also provide the competent authorities with a certificate of support for the initiative. The competent authorities must issue the certificate to the organisers within 1 month of receiving the statement of support form.
If the organisers fail to comply with this requirement, they may be subject to a decision of revocation. The decision may be taken by the Commission, which has the power to:
revoke the certificate;
suspend the registration of the initiative;
decide to withdraw the initiative from the register.
In addition, the Commission may decide to suspend the registration if the organisers do not comply with the following conditions:
the organisers have not collected the required number of signatures;
the initiative has not been submitted for a specific period of time;
it has not yet been adopted;
there is no public information campaign.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to improve transparency in the EU, including:
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU on the publication of the list of EU countries which require the provision of a personal identification number or personal identification document number in their national register;
Commission Regulation (EC) No 852/2010 on the registration and verification of citizens' initiatives.
For more information, see:
‘Citizen's initiatives’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 88:
Pesticides: EU statistics
Pesticide statistics: placing on the market and use
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1367/2006 — EU statistics on pesticides
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common framework for the systematic production of EU-wide statistics on the placing on market and the use of those pesticides which are plant protection products, as defined in Article 2(a)(i) of Directive 2003/4/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 28 January 2003 on public access to environmental information (see summary).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
pesticides placed on the European Union (EU) market;
plant growth regulators;
molluscicides;
herbicides based on derivatives of urea.
It does not apply to pesticides based on dinitroaniline derivatives.
The statistics cover substances listed in Annex III contained in pesticides placed on market in each EU country.
EU countries must compile statistics for each selected crop within a 5-year reference period. The first reference period is the second calendar year following 30 December 2009.
Data collection, transmission and processing
Each EU country must send the European Commission a report on the implementation of the regulation every 5 years. The report must include:
the number of pesticides used and the quantities of substances used;
the total number of substances in each category of pesticide;
a description of the pesticides used in each type of plant growth regulators.
In addition, the Commission must submit a report to the EU Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the results of the statistical collection and processing carried out under the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
  
It has applied since 31 December 2006.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to reduce the risks associated with pesticide use, including:
rules on the labelling of pesticides;
measures to improve the information available to the public on the use and risks of pesticides.
For more information, see:
European Union statistics on pesticide use (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Pesticide: a pesticide used to protect plants against pests.
* Plant growth regulator: an EU country’s plant growth regulator, which is responsible for monitoring the plant growth of a given crop and monitoring the effects of pesticides on the plant.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 89:
Plant protection products — EU rules on active substances and their biosafety and bioaccumulation (POPs)
Plant Protection Products — European rules on Active Substances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the placing on the market of plant protection products containing active substances
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the approval, labelling and labelling of substances used as active substances for the protection of plants. These substances are referred to as ‘plant protection products’.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
active substances* intended for use as plant protection*;
safeners* and synergists*** for plant protection;
adjuvants* for the use of these substances.
It does not apply to substances that are not used as plants.
The European Commission must assess whether an active substance, safener or synergist fulfils the following criteria:
the presence of a biocentration factor greater than 0.5 mg/kg;
the absence of toxicity;
evidence that the substance has been present in the environment for more than 5 years.
If a substance fulfils all three of the criteria, it is a POP. The Commission may review the approval of a basic substance at any time.
EU countries must ensure that:
a product has been approved for use on their territory for at least 5 years;
an active substance has not been used as a plant protection product;
it has been used in more than one EU country;
a combination of a POP and a synergist has been tested and approved.
An active substance or a combination of active substances is considered to be a POP if it meets all 3 of the above criteria.
A product may not be placed on the EU market if it:
has not been tested or tested in a country other than that in which it is authorised;
has been used only for a limited period of time;
does not comply with the requirements of the European Union (EU) law on the labelling, packaging and product information;
is likely to endanger human health or the environment;
contains a substance that is likely to present a risk to human health and the environment.
Approval procedure
The competent authority of an EU country must first establish whether the approval criteria set out in points 3.2 to 3.7 of Annex II are satisfied. The competent authority must then assess whether the product meets the other 3 criteria. If it does not, the product must be withdrawn from the market or withdrawn from use.
In addition, the competent authority may authorise a product to be used only where it meets the following conditions:
it meets the safety criteria;
there is no risk to the environment or to the health or safety of human health;
its use is not likely to harm the environment, the environment and the health of animals or plants; and
it does not pose a threat to the public.
Where a product does not fulfil all 3 criteria, the European Commission may decide to withdraw its authorisation.
Advertising
The product must bear the following information on its packaging and packaging:
its composition and composition of the active substance;
how it is used in the plant protection process;
where the product is used as an adjuvant, the presence or absence of the substance in the water.
Labelling
The products must be clearly labelled and clearly distinguishable from other products on sale in the EU. The product must also bear the information ‘This product is not for human consumption’, ‘It is not intended for human use’ and ‘it is not suitable for use in any other EU country. This information must be accompanied by a description of the product’s purpose and the risks it presents to the general public. It must also contain the product information and information on the safety of the products.
Manufacturers must also ensure that the information they provide to the competent authorities and the public is clearly legible.
Products must be labelled in a language easily understood by the end-users and that they are clearly identifiable as such. They must also indicate that they have been tested, tested and certified in accordance with the relevant EU legislation.
National authorities must monitor the safety and effectiveness of the approved products and ensure that they comply with all the requirements laid down in the regulation.
They must also monitor the use and use of adjuvants and other substances that have not been approved, as well as the monitoring and control requirements for the products that have been approved. The European Commission is responsible for monitoring and controls. It may adopt harmonised methods to ensure that these methods are effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
Authorisations
The Commission must issue a decision on the granting of an authorisation to a product. The authorisation is valid for a period not exceeding 15 years. This decision may be reviewed at any

Administrative cooperation in the field of information exchange in the EU: Information Management Instrument (IMI)
Administrative Cooperation in the Field of Information Exchange in the European Union (IME)
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) No 565/2013 on the establishment of the information management instrument (IMI)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the administrative cooperation tool for the exchange of information between EU countries’ competent authorities and the European Commission.
It aims to facilitate administrative cooperation between the competent authorities of the EU countries and between the Commission and other EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules on data security and the rights of data subjects. It also lays down the responsibilities of the European Data Protection Supervisor (EDP) and the national supervisory authorities of EU countries to ensure the protection of personal data.
The European Commission is responsible for administering IMI. It is assisted by a committee of coordinators.
Implementation of the regulation is governed by a series of implementing acts. These include:
An implementing act to determine which provisions of EU acts are subject to a pilot project and to set out the basic technical functionality and procedural arrangements required to implement the relevant administrative cooperation provisions;
An Implementing Act to determine the modalities for each pilot project.
A Commission Implementing Decision to set up the European Monitoring Centre for the Exchange of Information (EMC).
The Commission adopts a report every 3 years on the results of the pilot projects.
In addition, every 5 years, the Commission reports to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions on the implementation of the IMI Regulation.

Summary 91:
Fishing and aquaculture in the Mediterranean and Black Sea: common dolphinfish (GFCM Agreement)
Fishery and aquatic activities in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 652/2011 on the implementation of the GFCM agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the management of fishing, hunting and fishing activities of European Union fishing vessels and nationals of EU countries in the Black Sea and the Mediterranean.
It aims to ensure the sustainable and rational management of living marine resources in these waters.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
all commercial fishing and aquaulture activities conducted by EU fishing vessels, nationals of the EU and nationals from non-EU countries, in the waters covered by the agreement;
all fishing and transshipment activities conducted in the area of the agreement.
EU countries must:
report to the European Commission by 15 January of each year the number of vessels involved in the fishery, as well as the total landings and transhipments of common and pelagic fish carried out in the previous year by the fishing vessels flying their flag in each Geographical Sub-Area of the Agreement area as set out in Annex I.
Vessels flying the flag of an EU country must provide the Commission with:
the vessel’s EU Fleet Register number and its external marking;
the period during which fishing and/or transhipment is authorised and
the fishing gears used.
The Commission may adopt implementing acts as regards detailed rules on the format and transmission of the report on such fishing activities.
Reporting of statistical matrices
Each year, EU countries must send the Commission a report on the number and number of fishing vessels involved and the total number of landings or transshipments carried out. The Commission must transmit the report to the Executive Secretary of the General Council of the Mediterranean Fisheries Commission.
Fishing gear
The minimum mesh size for nets used for trawling activities exploiting demersal stocks in the Baltic Sea must be 40 millimetres (mm).
The maximum mesh size of pelagic trawls used for the exploitation of the pelagic fishery must be no more than 24 metres in length.
In the Mediterranean mixed fisheries, the selectivity of some fishing gears cannot be permitted to go beyond a certain level.
Monitoring of fishing activities
EU fishing vessels must submit their fishing logbook and reports to port inspectors, including those transmitted by a vessel monitoring system (VMS), as appropriate.
Port inspectors must examine the fishing logbooks and reports submitted, including the data transmitted by the VMS, as appropriate, and check that the vessel is complying with the rules.
If a vessel is found to be in breach of the rules, it must be subject to a penalty of up to 10 days’ imprisonment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 19 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
The European Commission has adopted implementing acts implementing Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 752/2010 on the GGCM Agreement. The regulation establishes a common framework for multilateral cooperation to promote the development, conservation, rational management and best utilisation of marine resources.
For more information, see:
The EU fishing fleet (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Common and Pelagic Fishery: a fishery in which an individual or group of individuals or groups of individuals and groups of persons are involved in fishing and hunting for common or pelagic fishes.
* Pelagic trawl: a type of trawl used to capture and transport fish in a pelagic sea.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom), Euratom of 13 December 2011 on the implementing act of the European Parliament and of the Council of 13 January 2012 on the interpretation and application of the regulation relating to the application of Article 15(2) of the Treaty on European Union on the Functioning of the Common Fisheries Policy (OJ L 136, 19.1.2012, pp. 1-8)

Summary 92:
Uniform patent protection within the internal market: European patent with unitary effect (EU-level regulation) (European Patent Office Regulation)
United Kingdom (UK) (United Kingdom) (UPC Regulation) (OJ L 76, 22.3.2014, pp. 53-58)
Uniform European patent protection: European Patent with unitARY effect (UCPR)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the creation of unitary patent protection in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that patents granted by the European Patent Office (EPO) have the same level of protection throughout the EU.
It sets out the conditions under which a patent can be licensed in the EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
patents granted in accordance with Article 145 of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU) (see summary);
powers conferred on the patent proprietor by the EPO;
the right to prevent any third party from committing acts against which the patent provides protection.
EU countries must:
ensure that the patent is licensed in respect of the whole or part of the territories of the participating EU countries;
apply the same licensing conditions as those applicable to patents granted in other EU countries, including:
a single annual renewal fee for a European patent;
a ban on the use of patents for non-commercial purposes.
The European Commission must regularly submit to the European Parliament and the Council reports on the functioning of the regulation, with particular emphasis on compliance with the renewal fees.
In addition, the Commission must evaluate the operation of the licensing system and make appropriate proposals to improve the licensing of patents.
A European patent granted by a patent-possessor may be granted in more than one EU country at the same time. It must be licensed for a period of 10 years.
If a patent is granted in one country, it is licensed for 10 years in another EU country.
Licensing of a patent
A patent can only be licensed if it is:
uniform in nature;
obliged by the patent to be applied for in the territory of the EU country in which it was granted; or
unlimited in scope.
Where a patent has been granted in a non-EU country, the licensing conditions are:
the patent must be granted to a person or a group of persons who have the right to use the patent in that country;
it must not be infringed by a third party.
An EU country may refuse to grant a license if the patent infringes one of the following conditions:
it does not infringe the rights conferred on it by the EPC;
there is no infringement of the patent’s validity;
an infringement is likely to have a significant impact on the economic, social or cultural life of the person or group of people whose patent is infringed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures in the area of patent protection. These include:
Decision 2011/167/EU authorising enhanced cooperation between Belgium, Bulgaria, the Czech Republic, Denmark, Germany, Estonia, Ireland, Greece, France, Cyprus, Latvia, Lithuania, Luxembourg, Hungary, Malta, the Netherlands, Austria, Poland, Portugal, Romania, Slovenia, Slovakia, Finland, Sweden and the United Kingdom in the field of patent-related cooperation;
Decisions 2011/166/EU and 2011/168/EU establishing a European Patent Pool.
For more information, see:
European Patent Pool (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Unitary patent: a patent which has the same legal effect in all EU countries as a patent granted in any other EU country, except for Denmark.
* Patent pool: a pool of patents which are granted in different EU countries and which are collectively known as the ‘EU-wide patent pool’.
ACT
Council Regulation (EEC) No 529/2013 of 21 December 2013 on the establishment of a European Product Patent with Unitary Effect in the Community and repealing Regulation (EC) no 553/2009 of the EC.

Summary 94:
Public procurement — enforcement of the EU’s rights under international trade agreements (GPA) and WTO rules on public procurement (Public Procurement Regulation (EU) No 182/2011)
Public procurement: enforcement of EU rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 181/2011 on the exercise of the Union's rights in the area of public procurement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for enforcing the EU's rights under the General Agreement on Trade in Arms and Trade in Services (GATT) and other international agreements on the procurement of goods and services.
It aims to ensure that the EU is able to enforce its rights when a trade partner fails to respect its commitments under the GATT or other agreements on government procurement.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following sectors:
trade in arms and trade in services:
the origin of a good or a service is determined on the basis of the origin of the natural or legal person providing it.
The European Commission must submit a list of appropriate contracting authorities or entities or categories of contracting authorities and categories of entities that may be involved in the implementation of possible commercial policy measures to be adopted by the EU in this area.
EU countries must ensure that:
goods and services originating in a non-EU country are not supplied by persons who have a direct and effective link with the economy of the country in which they are established;
they are not provided by persons with a direct or indirect links with the EU countries;
the EU countries that are not EU countries are not involved in public procurement;
there are no customs duties or quotas on goods or services originating from EU countries.
Public authorities and entities must apply normal precautions and exercise due diligence when assessing information and guarantees provided by tenderers as regards the origin.
In addition, EU countries must take the necessary measures to ensure the maintenance of a substantially equivalent level of concessions, as laid down in the relevant international agreements, in order to safeguard their economic interests.
Exemptions from the regulation
The Commission may suspend, modify or repeal the regulation if it considers that a contracting authority or entity has not complied with its obligations under the agreement. The suspension, modification or repeal of the regulation must be decided by a committee composed of representatives of EU countries and chaired by the Commission.
Implementing powers
The implementing powers of this regulation are conferred on the Commission, which must:
ensure uniform conditions for its implementation;
review the regulation, including possible measures in the sector of intellectual property rights and additional measures concerning services, no later than 3 years after the first instance of its implementation or 5 years from its date of entry into force, whichever is the earlier.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND

The GATT and the WTO agreements on trade in arms, services and public procurement provide the EU with a legal basis for enforcing its rights under these agreements.
For more information, see:
‘Public procurement’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 95:
Agreement on the stabilisation of the EU market for bananas (2012-2013)
Agreement establishing a stabilisation mechanism for bananas
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2012 on the application of the Agreement on the stability of the Union market for Bananas
Decision 2012/852/EU on the conclusion, on behalf of the European Union, of the agreement on the stabilization of the market for banana imports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE AGREEMENT?
The regulation establishes a system for monitoring imports of bananas in the EU.
The agreement aims to ensure that the EU’s banana market is stable and to prevent any serious injury to EU producers of other products.
It sets out rules for the monitoring and monitoring of imports of the products covered by the agreement.
KEY POINTS
Under the agreement, the Commission is responsible for monitoring the import of bananas. It must cooperate and exchange data on a regular basis with EU countries and the EU industry. It may also initiate investigations and proceedings if it considers that an import is likely to cause or threaten to cause serious injury.
If there is sufficient prima facie evidence to justify the initiation of a proceeding, it must publish a notice as provided for in Article 111(3) of the annex to the agreement in the Official Journal.
Where the Commission considers that the importation of a product in question poses a serious threat to the EU producers’ interests, it may decide to take measures to prevent or restrict its importation. These measures may include:
preventing the export of the product from the EU;
imposing provisional or definitive safeguard measures;
suspending the import;
restricting the supply of a particular type of product;
taking measures to protect the interests of producers;
ensuring that the Commission receives all information it considers necessary to make a determination with regard to the conditions set out in Article 2(1), and, where appropriate, endeavour to verify that information.
EU countries may request information and may exchange views in the Committee or directly with the Commission.
Implementing rules
The Commission must adopt implementing rules for:
monitoring measures,
preparatory measures and
pre-surveillance measures.
Prior surveillance measures are to be taken when:
a product is imported into the EU in such increased quantities, in absolute terms or relative to EU production, and under such conditions as to cause, or threaten, serious injury;
a report is prepared by the Commission on the impact of the imports concerned on the situation of the union market.
Safeguard measures may be taken if:
the import of a specific product is causing, or threatens to cause:
serious injury to Union producers of like or directly competitive products as laid down in Article 104;
the product is being imported in such quantities as to have a significant impact on the market;
there is a risk that the product may be used as a substitute for other products which are not subject to safeguard measures. The Commission may, however, take into account the legitimate interest of natural and legal persons concerned that their business secrets should not be divulged.
Provisional and definitive measures may only be applied if the following conditions are met:
an imported product is:
being imported in quantities that are significantly greater than the level of production;
being of such a nature as to pose a serious risk to the economic, social or cultural interests of EU producers; or
being in such a quantity as to be likely to have an adverse effect on the economic interests of the producers.
In the event that the facts as finally established show that the conditions are not met, the case must be referred to the Association Committee for examination in accordance with Article 116 of the Agreements.
Stabilisation mechanism
The Agreement sets up a system of administrative co-operation between the Commission and EU countries to ensure the proper functioning of the system. It also establishes a non-confidential file, which it must manage and through which all information which is relevant and is not confidential within the meaning of Article 12 of the regulation is disseminated.
This system is to be used only for the purpose for which it was requested.
As soon as the necessary technical framework is in place, it shall ensure password-protected online access to the non-Confidential file.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts implementing the agreement since its entry into force. These include Regulation (EEC) No 2913/92.
For more information, see:
Bananas (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Preparatory safeguard measures: measures taken to prevent, restrict or restrict imports of certain products from non-EU countries.
* Seizure measures: a measure taken when a product is suspected of



Summary 96:
Creditors’ right to a ‘preservation order’ against a debtor’s account or accounts — cross-border cooperation in civil matters in the event of a bankruptcy or winding-up of an insolvent company or other legal entity
Crediting parties’ rights in respect of the preservation of funds in an account or account must be respected in cases where the debtor has not yet obtained a judgment, court settlement or authentic instrument.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the preservation order
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for the enforcement of a court-issued ‘Preservation Order’ in the case of a debtor who has been declared bankrupt or whose bank account is in another EU country.
It aims to improve judicial cooperation between EU countries in cases of disputes between creditor and debtor.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
rights in property arising out of a matrimonial relationship or a relationship deemed by the law applicable to such relationship to have comparable effects to marriage;
wills and succession, including maintenance obligations arising by reason of death;
claims against debtor in relation to whom bankruptcy proceedings, proceedings for the winding up of insolvent companies, judicial arrangements, compositions, or analogous proceedings have been opened;
social security;
arbitration.
A creditor may not submit to several courts at the same time parallel applications for a preservation order against the same debtor aimed at securing the same claim.
The creditor must provide security for an amount sufficient to prevent abuse of the procedure provided for by the creditor and to ensure compensation for any damage suffered by the debtor as a result of the order to the extent that the creditor is liable for such damage pursuant to Article 13 of the regulation. The creditor must also provide proof that the debtor is not liable for the damage caused by the order.
Where the creditor has already obtained a court settlement, authentic instrument or judgment, it must provide the court with a security for a sum sufficient to ensure that the order is enforced. The court may also require the creditor to provide proof for the release of the funds preserved.
In the case where the creditor does not have the necessary security to enforce the order, the court must issue a declaration indicating whether and to what extent funds in the debtor's account and accounts have been preserved and, if so, on which date the order was implemented.
Implementation of the Preservation Order
The bank or other entity responsible for enforcing the Order in the EU country of enforcement must issue the declaration using the declaration form established by means of implementing acts adopted in accordance with the advisory procedure referred to in Article 52(2), indicating whether the debtor had any funds in his/her account or its accounts.
Procedure for the remedies
The Court of Justice of the European Union may issue a judgment or an authentic instrument in a case where:
the creditor has not obtained the judgment or the authentic instrument;
the bank account concerned is located in one EU country; or
the debtor has a bank account in another.
If the court considers that the court has jurisdiction over the case, it may issue an order in the same EU country as an equivalent national order in that country. The Court may also decide to issue a court order in an EU country where the court dealing with the application for the order has its headquarters.
When a court issues an order for the execution of a Preservation Order, it is bound by the same rules as those applicable to a national order. It must be lodged with the court which the debtor communicates to the Commission pursuant to point (d) of Article 50(1).
The Commission may adopt implementing acts establishing and subsequently amending the forms to establish and subsequently amend the forms of the declaration.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of measures relating to judicial cooperation in the area of civil matters. These include:
Delegated Regulation (Euratom) No 696/2012 on the protection of personal data, the right to property and the right of access to a fair trial;
Regulations (EC) No 852/2012 and (EC, EC) No 952/2014 on the enforcement and preservation of bank accounts, and Regulation (EU, EC, EC and EFTA) No 1082/2013 laying down the procedures for the implementation of the Order.
For more information, see:
‘Cross-border judicial cooperation’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Creditor: a person who is a creditor to a debtor and who has a claim against the debtor for the same amount of money.
Bank account: any bank account that is held by a debtor in a country other than the one in which it is located.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
 



Summary 97:
Border surveillance at sea: preventing illegal border crossings and combating cross-border crime (Regulation (EU) No 515/2014)
Border surveillance on sea
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2015/852 — border surveillance at the sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes rules for border surveillance operations at sea.
It aims to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter crossborder criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Euratom).
It covers:
border surveillance in the territorial sea;
interception in the contiguous zone;
detection and reporting of vessels suspected of being engaged in illegal activities at sea, which are outside the scope of the sea operation;
assistance to persons found in distress.
Participating units
Each participating unit must:
have at least one person with basic first aid training;
ensure the protection of human dignity;
respect the fundamental rights and the principle of non-refoulement;
participate fully in the performance of their duties, fully respecting human dignity.
The participating units:
collect and immediately report information about any vessel suspected of engaging in illegal activity at sea to the International Coordination Centre, which transmits that information to the national coordination centre of the EU country concerned;
report information about the situation of persons on board, in particular whether there is an imminent risk to their lives or whether there are persons in urgent need of medical assistance;
have the contact details of those competent national authorities, which must take appropriate follow-up measures.
Interception
A border surveillance operation may take place in a country’s territorial sea or in a contiguous zone. The participating units are responsible for:
detecting a vessel flying a foreign flag or refusing to show its flag;
checking that the vessel is, in reality, of the same nationality as a participating unit;
verifying that the flag is not being flown by a non-EU country.
If a vessel is transiting the contiguous sea, the participating unit is responsible for the interception and must inform the host country of the country in whose territory the interception takes place. The host country must then instruct the participating units accordingly through the international coordination centre.
In the event of a vessel not complying with the rules of the regulation, it must be intercepted by the competent national authority of the participating EU country. If a vessel does not comply with these rules, it is subject to a search and seizure operation.
When an EU country detects a vessel in distress, it informs the other EU countries of the situation and informs them of the location of the vessel and of the number of people on board.
A participating unit:
ensures that the person on board is not a threat to the safety of the persons on the vessel;
provides assistance to persons who are in distress;
is accompanied by a qualified first responder;
has the necessary equipment and training to respond to persons in distress and to provide medical assistance.
An operational plan is drawn up for each participating unit. The operational plan must contain contact details for the relevant national authorities.
Where a vessel transits the contiguous maritime zone, the competent EU country must inform it of the details of the competent authorities of the other participating units.
Coastal EU country:
is responsible for initiating an interception;
cooperates with the participating border surveillance units of other participating EU countries.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The European Parliament and the Council adopted the regulation on 18 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of implementing acts on border surveillance. These include:
Directive 2011/36/EU on the prevention and control of trafficking in human beings (see summary);
Council Decision 2010/252/EU establishing, as part of the Internal Security Fund, the instrument for financial support for external borders and visa and repealing Decision No 574/2007/EC (OJ L 150, 20.5.2014, pp. 143-148).
The Euratom Agency has been responsible for implementing the regulation.
For more information, see:
EU border surveillance (European External Borders Agency).
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Delegation (EU), Euratom of 18 June 2015 on the implementation of Regulation (EC) No 550/2014 of 18 July 2014 establishing rules for the surveillance of the external borders of the Member States and the Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms of the European Union at sea and repealing Council Decision No 652/2010/EU.
Successive amendments to Council Delegated Regulations (EU and Euratom), as amended by Council Decision 2014/856/EU, have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is



Summary 98:
EU actions and emergency assistance in or in relation to third countries
EU actions, emergency and technological assistance in and relation to non-EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 966/2012 — financial rules for EU actions, EMERGENCY and technical assistance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out detailed rules for the implementation of the EU’s specific rules for actions and assistance to third-country countries in the field of migration and asylum.
It aims to support EU countries’ efforts to cope with the increasing pressure on the external borders of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU actions and measures to help third countries to deal with the influx of migrants and asylum seekers.
EU countries must designate a designated national authority responsible for the management and control of expenditure under the regulation. This authority is responsible for:
monitoring, monitoring, reporting and evaluating the effectiveness of the support provided;
assigning revenue to the designated authority;
contributing to the EU budget.
National programmes
Each year, EU countries must submit a proposal for a national programme to the European Commission. The Commission adopts the national programme and adopts implementing acts in accordance with the implementing acts adopted by the European Parliament and the Council.
Actions supported under the national programmes must be co-financed by public or private sources, must be of a non-profit nature and must not be subject to funding from other sources covered by the Union budget. They may be of the following types:
non-profit-based;
co-financing;
research and development;
public procurement;
exchange of information;
transnational cooperation.
The national programme must be approved by the competent authorities.
Implementation
The Commission and the EU countries ensure that national programmes are implemented according to the rules laid down in the regulation, including through monitoring and reporting and evaluation.
Responsible authorities are responsible for managing and controlling expenditure. They must:
ensure that expenditure is managed and controlled in line with the objectives of the regulation;
identify, monitor and correct any irregularities and recover amounts unduly paid together with any interest on late payments.
In addition, they must ensure that:
the budget of each EU country is clear and transparent;
the national programme is implemented correctly;
there is no conflict of interest between the budget of the country and the European budget;
all expenditure is covered by EU funds.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
 
   
The Regulation entered into force on 1 February 2015.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* External borders: the area of the external border of the Union in which the EU has its external borders.
* Third-country country: a country which is not a member of the Schengen area and which has not applied for admission to the Union.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC) No 659/1999 of 22 March 1999 laying down detailed rules on the application of Article 108 of the Treaty establishing the European Community (OJ L 83, 27.3.1999, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU) No 608/1999 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 99:
European Asylum and Migration Fund
European asylum and migration Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 514/2014 establishing the European Asylum and Migrations Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets up a European asylum and Migrant Fund (EAMF).
It aims to provide financial support to EU countries in the field of immigration and asylum.
KEY POINTS
The EAMF is the EU’s main source of financial support for migration and asylum policy. It is funded by the EU budget.
It is designed to:
support voluntary burden-sharing operations agreed between EU countries and consisting of the transfer of beneficiaries of international protection and of applicants for international protection, from one EU country to another;
support actions focusing on the persons referred to in Article 11 of this regulation;
contribute to the return of those who have been refused entry to the EU or who have not been returned to their countries of origin;
provide support for the implementation of the common EU resettlement priorities.
The European Asylum Support Office (EASO) is responsible for the coordination of the EAF and its implementation.
In addition, the EASO:
is responsible for:
contributing to the development of the European asylum system;
cooperating with Member States to implement the common European Union resettlement priorities;
facilitating the exchange of information and assistance between Member States;
assisting Member States in their national programmes;
ensuring that Member States are able to share information on the number of people who have entered the EU;
reintegration and reintegration of unaccompanied minors in their countries;
coordinating the return measures of persons who have left their country of origin.
Funding
The Fund is funded through the European Union budget. It has a global budget of €2.5 billion.
An additional amount may be allocated to Member States, provided that it is earmarked as such in the programme and that the specific actions listed in Annex II are used to implement specific actions.
A decision to grant the Fund a grant is binding on the European Commission. The Commission has the power to revoke the grant.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
It has applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The purpose of the regulation is to establish the EAMEF.
For more information, see:
‘European Asylum Fund’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Refugee: a person who has been denied entry into the EU and who is a danger to his or her life or to the safety of others.
* Migrant: someone who has not been granted international protection or who is in danger of being denied entry.

Summary 100:
Funding for the fight against transnational crime: EU action to protect people and goods from transnational threats (2014-2020)
Funding to the European Union (EU) fight against cross-border crime
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 662/2014 establishing a multiannual financial instrument for combating transnational organised crime
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the European Fund for Combating Transnational Organised Crime (ETFIC).
It aims to support EU countries’ efforts to combat the transnational threat posed by terrorism, organised crime, drug trafficking, corruption, cybercrime, trafficking in human beings and arms, inter alia, to protect the internal security of the EU.
It establishes the European Instrument for Democracy and Human Rights (EIFH).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The instrument covers:
the objectives, eligible actions and strategic priorities for financial support to be provided under the instrument;
the general framework for the implementation of the eligible actions; and
the resources made available under the Instrument from 1 January 2014 to 31 December 2020 and their distribution.
Eligible actions
The EU countries must:
ensure that their national programmes are consistent with the EU’s priorities and objectives;
support the work carried out by their competent authorities;
cooperate with each other in order to achieve the objectives of the instrument.
The European Commission is responsible for managing the instrument and for its implementation.
the European Parliament and the Council are responsible for authorising the allocation of funds.
Annual appropriations
EU countries must make use of all possible methods of implementation, including, where appropriate, by indirect management, to ensure the timely and efficient delivery of the activities and projects.
Specific objectives
The objective of the Instrument is to:
promote the protection of the lives of vulnerable persons, in particular children and unaccompanied minors;
protect the economic interests of EU countries;
combat transnational criminal activities, such as terrorism and organised crime;
fight against the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction.
Strategic priorities
The objectives and priorities are set out in the instrument's strategic priorities. These include:
protecting the lives and property of the most vulnerable people;
promoting the security and stability of the Union;
defending the rights and fundamental freedoms of EU citizens;
ensuring that EU countries take the necessary measures to prevent, combat and combat transnational crimes.
Resources for eligible actions
Resources must be allocated to the EU countries as follows:
30 % in proportion to the size of their total population;
10 % of their territory;
15 % of passengers and 10 % of cargo processed through their international air and sea ports;
35 % in inverse proportion to their gross domestic product (purchasing power standard per inhabitant).
Emergency assistance must be implemented in accordance with Articles 6 and 7 of the Emergency Assistance Directive (EAD).
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to authorise the European Parliament or the Council to provide financial support for actions that are suspended due to legal proceedings or appeals having suspensive effect.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 21 May 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EIFH was established for a period of 7 years. It was originally intended to be renewed for a further 7 years from 21 May 2015. However, the European Commission decided to extend its period of operation until 31 December 2018.
For more information, see:
Fundamental principles of the European Investment Bank (European Commission).

Summary 101:
Non-sustainable fishing: measures to combat non-EU countries’ non-compliance with the EU fisheries management rules
Non-EU fishing : measures to tackle non-Sustainable fishing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2012 on the prevention, control and enforcement of non-sustainability of fishing activities involving a fishery involving a stock of common interest to that country and to the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out specific measures to be taken against countries that allow, without due regard to existing fishing patterns or the rights, duties and interests of other countries and the EU, fisheries activities that jeopardise the sustainability of that stock, and fails to cooperate with other countries or the EU in its management.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to countries that:
fisheries are not continuously maintained at or above the levels that can produce maximum sustainable yield or, if these levels cannot be estimated, when the stocks are not being maintained within safe biological limits;
are not cooperating in the management of the fishery;
do not cooperate with the European Regional Fisheries Management Organisation (RFMOs) or, where RFMOs have no competence for the stock in question, by means of ad hoc arrangements among the countries having an interest in that fishery.
The European Commission may adopt specific measures in order to encourage countries to contribute to the conservation of the stock. These measures are based on objective criteria and are equitable, cost-effective and compatible with international law, in particular with the Agreement establishing the World Trade Organisation (WTO).
The Commission must adopt implementing acts to ensure that the measures adopted under this regulation are effective and proportionate. These implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 8 of the regulation.
If measures adopted against a country under the regulation are ineffective and that country continues to be considered to be a country allowing sustainable fishing, further measures may be adopted. The Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the measures taken and the reasons for these measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMESSAGE APPLYEAR OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 3 October 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EU’s fisheries policy is based on the principle of sustainable fishing. It aims to ensure the long-term sustainable management of fish stocks.
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Non-EU country: a country that allows or participates in fishing activities that endanger the long term sustainable fishing of a fish stock.
ACT
