Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements between EU countries and non-EU countries (until 9 January 2013)
Bilateral Investment agreements between European Union (EU) countries and third countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2012 on the amendment or conclusion of bilateral investment agreements with third countries after 9 January 2012
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the conditions under which EU countries are empowered to amend or conclude bilateral agreements with non-European Economic Area (EEA) countries.
It aims to ensure consistency with the EU’s investment policy and compatibility with EU law.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to agreements concluded between the EU countries between 1 December 2009 and 9 January 2014. It does not apply to international agreements relating to foreign investment concluded by EU countries before that date.
EU countries are authorised to enter into negotiations with a third country to amend an existing or to conclude a new bilateral investment agreement. These negotiations must include a description of the provisions to be addressed in the negotiations or to be renegotiated, the objectives of the negotiations and any other relevant information.
Authorisation to open formal negotiations is granted on the basis of clearly defined criteria established in the regulation. The Commission may request further information from the EU country concerned.
The Commission may require the country concerned to include or remove from such negotiations and prospective bilateral agreement any clauses where necessary to ensure that the agreement conforms with the Union's investment policy or compatibility with Union law. It may also require the EU law to be amended.
Notification to the Commission
The EU country must notify the Commission of any changes to the status of those agreements. The notification must include relevant documentation and an indication of the terms to be dealt with in negotiations or renegotiated.
If the Commission considers that the information transmitted by the EU state concerned is not sufficient for the purposes of authorising the opening of formal negotiations in accordance with Article 9, it may request additional information. If additional information is needed, the 90-day period runs from the date of receipt of the additional information to the date on which the Commission decides to take a decision.
In the event that the Commission does not grant the authorisation pursuant to paragraph 4, it must inform the EU State concerned and state the reasons therefor.
Amendments to existing agreements
An amendment or a new investment agreement may be amended or concluded only if it:
is compatible with EU rules on foreign investment;
does not conflict with the common commercial policy of the EU;
is in line with the general principles of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union;
contains clauses that are compatible with the European Investment Bank (EIB).
Authorisations may be refused if they do not comply with the conditions laid down in Article 9 of the regulation or if they are incompatible with the rules of the EIB.
Reviews
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 3 years on the number of amendments or new agreements it has granted. The report must include an overview of the authorisations requested and granted under Chapter III as well as a summary of the need for the continued application of that chapter.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 9 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
  The regulation applies from 1 December 2013.
For more information, see:
Investment agreements (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Bilateral investment agreement: an investment agreement between an EU country and a third party.
* Foreign investment: an international agreement between a country and another country on the terms and conditions of which the EU has exclusive competence to negotiate and conclude.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еганска гранда барка (OJ L 283, 9.12.2012, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2012 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 103:
Tariff preferences for the least-developed countries (Everything but Arms)
Tariffs preferences for:
the most developed countries (GSP+);
the least-developed countries (EBA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2012 — tariff preferences for developing countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a tariff preferences scheme designed to help developing countries have greater development, trade and financial needs.
It sets out the rules for the tariff preferences to be granted under the scheme.
KEY POINTS
The regulation provides for the following tariff preferences:
a general arrangement;
a special incentive arrangement for sustainable development and good governance;
an EBA special arrangement.
The tariff preferences are based on the Combined Nomenclature (CNC) codes. The CNC codes are used to identify products originating in developing countries. The European Commission monitors the imports of products falling under these codes in close cooperation with Member States.
Beneficiaries of these tariff preferences must:
be developing countries;
have a high level of economic and social development;
be in a position to participate in the EU’s common commercial policy.
In addition, the European Commission may adopt delegated acts to adopt tariff preferences under the EBA scheme. These may include:
suspension of the common customs duties on all products originating from an EBA beneficiary country;
the suspension of the Common Customs Tariff duties on certain goods originating in a beneficiary country, except those in Chapter 93; and
the extension of the period of temporary withdrawal of the customs duty on certain products.
Member States are responsible for monitoring the implementation of this regulation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission adopted the regulation on behalf of the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
Tariff Preferences for Developing Countries (European Commission).

Combined Nomenchature: a set of codes used to classify goods originating from developing countries in order to identify them.

Summary 104:
EU rules on the export of firearms, their parts and components and ammunition (UN Firearms Protocol)
EU rules for the export and import of firearms and their parts
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2012 on the EU’s export controls on the manufacture, marking, import, export and transit of firearms
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacturing of and trafficking in firearms.
It sets out rules to prevent and combat the illicit trade in firearms and related parts and ammunition.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the manufacture, import and export of arms and ammunition;
the import and transshipment of firearms; and
the export of ammunition and parts and essential components.
EU countries must:
ensure that:
they have a system of export authorisation and control for firearms;
they apply for authorisation for multiple shipments, transit measures and temporary exports for lawful purposes.
They must also ensure that they:
have a system for identifying and deactivating illegal firearms and parts;
have an export control system in place for ammunition and components;
provide the competent authorities with information on the persons involved in the illegal trade;
establish a firearms export control and export control centre.
A Firearms Exports Coordination Group (the ‘Coordination Group’, chaired by a representative of the Commission, is set up. It is responsible for:
coordinating and coordinating the export control procedures and controls;
monitoring the implementation of export control measures;
ensuring that the export controls are in place;
exchanging information on suspected persons and entities involved in suspicious activities.
The Commission must publish a list of those authorities that have been identified as being involved in suspected suspicious activities;
publishing information on those authorities on a yearly basis in the C series of the Official Journal of the European Union.
Exports must be authorised by the competent customs authority of the EU country in which the firearms are to be exported.
If a firearms, its parts or essential components or ammunition are located in one or more EU countries other than the one where the export application is made, that country must indicate on that application that it is located in that country as well as the country where it is intended for export.
When leaving the customs territory of an EU country other than that of their residence, hunters and sport shooters must produce a European Firearms Pass as provided for in Articles 1 and 12 of Directive 91/477/EEC.
In addition, the regulation requires:
all persons who have the right to export firearms must have a valid travel document issued by the customs authority in the EU;
all individuals who have a right to import firearms must be able to carry out their lawful activities in accordance with the customs legislation of that EU country;
any person who has the right of establishment in a non-EU country must carry out his or her lawful activities under the same conditions as nationals of that country.
Each EU country must ensure that it:
has a system to identify and deactivate illegal firearms*;
implements measures to prevent the illicit export of these firearms and components, and to detect and neutralise their illicit trafficking;
has procedures in place to control the movements of firearms in and out of its territory.
Imports must comply with the rules laid down in Regulation (EC) No 450/2008 on the modernisation of the Community Customs Code (Modernised Customs Code) (see summary).
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 3 July 2012.
BACKGROUND
The UN Firearms Protocol entered into force on 3 July 2005.
For more information, see:
‘Unilateral export controls’ on the European Commission's website.

  барсканка гранда нарка (OJ L 183, 3.7.2012, pp. 1-8)

Summary 105:
EU border control: situational awareness and reaction capability
EU border controls: awareness and response capability
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 establishing a European Border and Coast Guard Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) border control agency.
It aims to improve situational awareness, reaction and situational awareness at the external borders of the EU countries of the Union (‘external borders’) for the purpose of detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to the surveillance of external land and sea borders, including the monitoring, detection, identification, tracking, prevention and interception of unauthorised border crossings.
EU countries may exchange information and cooperate with one or several neighbouring non-EU countries.
National coordination centres are responsible for sharing with each other, directly and in near-real-time, the situational picture of neighbouring external border sections relating to:
incidents and other significant events;
tactical risk analysis reports as contained in the events layer;
a common pre-frontier intelligence picture;
common application of surveillance tools.
The European Union Border Guard Agency (EUROSUR) is responsible for providing the national coordination centres and the national border control authorities of EU countries with surveillance information on the EU’s external borders on a regular, reliable and cost-efficient basis.
a communication network, a European situational picture and a common intelligence picture.
A common surveillance tool is used for the common application of the common surveillance tools and the common operational layer.
All incidents and significant events are shared with the Agency.
Safeguards of personal data
The Agency must:
ensure the protection of persons in need of international protection;
cooperate with other EU countries in the field of border surveillance;
provide the competent national authorities with the information they need to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration.
In addition, the Agency may request support from the Agency subject to the conditions for initiating joint operations or rapid interventions, as laid down in Regulation (ECBAC) No 2007/2004.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 20 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve its border control capability, including:
establishing a European border control Agency (e.g. Europol);
introducing a common situational picture for EU countries’ external borders;
developing a common information exchange and cooperation mechanism.
For more information, see:
Border control agency (European Commission).

the situation on the external border;

Summary 106:
Eurodac system for the identification of criminal suspects (2014-2020)
EuroDac system to identify criminal suspects
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 604/2013 on the operation of the Eurodac fingerprint database
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the operation and management of the European Union’s (EU's) fingerprint database, EurodAC.
It repeals and repeals Regulations (EC) No 2725/2000 and 2008/615/JHA.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies from 20 July 2015. It applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and the United Kingdom (1). It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries’ designated authorities for law enforcement purposes
The European Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms (EAPF) is responsible for the operational management of EuroDac. It is assisted by the national authorities of the EU countries and the European Police Agency (EPSA).
The EAPF:
is responsible for:
supervision;
security;
coordinating relations between EU countries;
the coordination of relations between the EU country and the provider;
operating the Central System;
provides technical support to the EPPF;
is the competent authority for the EuroDAC system.
National Access Points
Each EU country has a single national access point.
The national access points are responsible for processing and storing the fingerprint data.
Each country must:
have a single National Access Point;
ensure that the data transmitted to the central system are kept for a maximum of 24 hours;
keep a record of the searches carried out and of the results of those searches.
In addition, each EU country must also:
keep records of the access points for the purpose of processing and storage of the data;
send a report to the European Parliament and the Council every 2 years on the activities of the system;
cooperate with the European Commission and the Agency for Fundamental Rights and Freedoms on the implementation of the regulation;
make a report every 3 years on its activities.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
From 20 July 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
The Eurodacc system was set up under Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 552/2004. It was originally set up in 2000.
For more information, see:
‘Fingerprint database’ on the Europol website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) 2015/634 of 20 June 2013 laying down the rules and implementing measures relating to the operation, management and control of the eurodac database (recast) (OJ L 393, 20.7.2015, pp. 1-39)

Summary 107:
Protection of designations of origin and geographical indications
Protecting products from the misuse, imitation and evocation of registered names
Protection for products originating in a specific place, region or, in exceptional cases, a country
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1257/1999 on the protection of designs of origin
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to protect products originating from specific places, regions or countries in the European Union (EU).
It sets out rules on the use of protected designations* of origin* and protected geographical indications (DPIs).
KEY POINTS
Designation of origin: a name which identifies a product originating in, originating in or originating in an EU country.
Geographical indication: an indication of the origin of a product which is essentially or exclusively due to a particular geographical environment with its inherent natural and human factors.
The regulation applies to the following products:
prepared meals,
beer,
chocolate and derived products,
bread, pastry, cakes, confectionery, biscuits and other baker’s wares,
beverages made from plant extracts,
pasta*,pasta,
salt.
It does not apply to:
wine;
traditional specialities guaranteed;
plant varieties and animal breeds;
mountain products.
Exemptions
Certain derogations apply to certain categories of products, such as certain types of chocolate as confectionary products. These derogations should be simplified and clarified.
Restriction on use of trade marks
Trade marks may not be used on goods or services that do not comply with the rules of the regulation. Trade marks registered in breach of the first subparagraph may be invalidated.
Trade mark registration
Trade names must be registered in accordance with the following rules:
the registered name must appear in the same field of vision as the product;
the name of the product must appear on the same packaging as the registered name;
trade marks must be accompanied by a description of the geographical area in which the product originates and the production steps of which all take place in the defined geographical area.
In addition, trade marks may be used for the marketing of products which do not conform with the regulations’ specifications.
EU countries must ensure that the registered names are used only for products that meet the specifications set out in the regulation and that they are not used for products which are not covered by the regulations.
Controls
The European Commission is responsible for setting up a system of national control bodies. It is assisted by the Agricultural Product Quality Policy Committee and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
The Commission may also adopt delegated acts, in line with Article 56 of the European Parliament and of the Council, to ensure the proper functioning of these control bodies and to ensure that their work is carried out effectively.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2000.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to protect product designations. These include:
Directive (EU) 2016/852 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States on the registration of trademarks and trade marks (see summary);
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017/853 on the establishment of a European Commission register of trade mark holders and the harmonization of the rules on trade marks.
For more information, see:
‘Protected designations’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 108:
Safety of oil tankers: EU rules on their design, construction and operation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the safety of oil tanker design and operation (recast)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to improve safety and prevent pollution in maritime transport.
It recasts and repeals Regulation (EC) No 417/2002.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
single-hull oil tanker designs and construction;
category 2 and Category 3 oil tanker construction and age.
EU countries are obliged to ensure that:
the ships listed in Annex I to MARPOL 73/78 comply with the double hull or equivalent design requirements;
the flag State administration of an EU country issues a Statement of Compliance and is involved in the CAS survey procedures.
The ships covered by the regulation may continue to operate subject to compliance with a condition assessment scheme (CAS), adopted on 27 April 2001 by Resolution MEPC 94(46) as amended by resolutions MEPC 99(48) of 11 October 2002 and by resolution MEPC 112(50) of 4 December 2003. The CAS is designed to detect structural weaknesses in ageing oil tanker hulls and should apply to all ships above the age of 15 years.
Exemptions from the regulation
The EU countries may grant exemptions from the obligation to comply with certain provisions of the regulation, such as:
continuing operation under the flag of a non-EU country of a Category 2 or Category 3 tanker;
entry into the ports or offshore terminals under the jurisdiction of a Member State of another Member State;
operating in the port of a third Member State in accordance with Regulation 20.
Compliance with the conditions laid down in the regulation must be carried out by:
flag State administrations of the Member States of the EU countries;
contractors of the ships listed above;
shipowners and operators of the vessels covered by this regulation.
Operators must:
ensure that their vessels comply with all the requirements laid down by the EU’s Maritime Safety Agency (MSAA);
operate their vessels under the authority of the flag State of the vessel concerned;
not engage in any activities that could endanger public health or the environment.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation entered into force on the twentieth day following that of its publication in the Official Journal of the European Union (OJEU).
BACKGROUND
In its communication on a common policy on safe seas, the Commission underlined the need for the EU to support the action in the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) on the reduction of the safety gap between new and existing ships by upgrading and/or phasing out existing ships.
For more information, see:
‘Safe seas’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Single hull: a vessel designed and built for the transport of crude oil by road, rail, air or sea.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2013 of 25 November 2013 on the safe operation of single hull oil tanker requirements and repealing Council Regulation (EEC, EC) No 479/2002 (recasts) (O J L 183, 28.11.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 109:
European System of Financial Supervision
European system of financial supervision
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1093/2010 establishing the European System of Supervision (ESS)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European system of supervisory authorities (ESAs) to ensure the stability of the EU’s financial system.
It sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the ESAs.
KEY POINTS
The ESAs are responsible for:
supervision of the financial system of the European Union (EU);
the prudential supervision of financial products and services;
the supervision of the activities of financial institutions in the EU; and
the oversight of financial intermediaries.
ESAs are governed by a Board of Supervisors, which is composed of representatives of the national authorities. It meets at least 5 times a year and is chaired by the European Commission.
The European Banking Authority (ESB), the European Securities and Markets Authority and the European Banking Supervisory Board (ESSA) are also observers.
In addition, the European Insurance and Reinsurance Stakeholder Group (EIRG) and the Occupational Pensions Stakeholders Group (OPSG) are involved in the establishment of the ESC.
A Joint Committee on Financial Conglomerates is set up to ensure consistency in ESAs’ activities and to reach common positions where appropriate. It may, together with the ESRB, discuss areas of mutual interest and inform each other of the other issues being discussed.
Each year, the board of supervisors, on the basis of a draft statement drawn up by the Executive Director and approved by the Management Board, produces a statement of estimates of revenue and expenditure of the authority for the following financial year. This is made available to the European Parliament, the Council, the Commission, the EIBG and the OIRG.
An Executive Director may participate in meetings of the Board of supervisors without the right to vote. The EIRG and OSPG have the power to adopt regulatory technical standards in the absence of an ESAs draft.
To ensure that the ESC is not subject to undue political pressure, it has adopted a work programme. The work programme must be adopted without prejudice to the annual budgetary procedure and must be made public.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The ESC was set up following the recommendation of the de Larosière Report in 2010. The report represented the reforms the experts considered were needed and on which work had to begin immediately.
For more information, see:
European financial supervisory bodies (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Supervisory authority: a body with legal personality.
* Financial intermediaries: a financial institution that provides services to consumers and businesses.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
  Еганска граба баркова   Загода
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1109/2010 of 27 December 2010 establishing the Member States’ Supervisory Authorities (OJ L 136, 1.1.2011, pp. 1–8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 1109 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement of workers and their employment: EU countries’s common rules on freedom of movement and equality of treatment of workers (Codified Regulation (EU) No 652/2011)
Freedom of mobility of workers: EU country’S common rules for freedom of mobility and equality between workers and employers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 608/2011 on the freedom to move within the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions under which workers from one EU country have the right to work in another EU country without any discrimination.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 552/2009.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all workers wishing to work or pursue an activity in the territory of an EU country, irrespective of their nationality.
Any national of a EU country may exchange their applications for and offers of employment, and conclude and perform contracts of employment in accordance with the provisions in force laid down by law, regulation or administrative action.
Workers wishing to move should also be regularly informed of living and working conditions. The specialist services of each EU country must forward this information to the appropriate employment services and agencies as soon as possible.
The Advisory Committee is responsible for assisting the Commission in the examination of any questions arising from the application of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU and measures taken in pursuance thereof, in matters concerning the freedom of moving workers and employment. It meets at least twice a year. The Commission publishes a list of services for information, and any amendment thereto, in the Official Journal of the Union.
Equal treatment for workers
Equality of treatment is guaranteed for all workers in the EU, including:
the right to take up available employment with the same priority as nationals of that country;
equality of treatment with regard to working conditions;
access to education and training;
the possibility to participate in the management of bodies governed by public law and from holding an office.
Employment and equal treatment
Employers must treat all workers with equal treatment when they employ them.
In addition, employers must ensure that:
all workers have the same access to education, training and professional qualifications;
all employees have equal access to health and safety;
employers have equal treatment with respect to the conditions of working time;
any worker who is unemployed has the same rights as any other worker.
Non-EU nationals may not be excluded from taking part in public administration or from holding public office. However, they may be subject to certain conditions, such as:
being excluded from the exercise of their voting rights;
being subject to a ban on the participation of non-EU citizens in public life;
not being able to vote in national elections; and
being denied the right of residence or residence.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  *   * * 
For more information, see:
‘Freedom to move’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Employment: any contract of employment concluded between an employer and a worker for the purpose of providing him or her with a service or service provision.

Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Summary 111:
Protocol on the protection of the ozone layer
Protocol against the depletion of ozone-depleting substances
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on the implementation of the Montreal Protocol on the Protection of the Ozone Layer
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the Protocol to the Convention on Biological Diversity (Ozone Layer) is fully implemented.
It repeals and repeals Regulation (Euratom) No 852/2008.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules on the production, import, export, placing on the market and use of certain substances. It also sets out the conditions for the placing on market and the use of these substances for essential laboratory and analytical uses, as well as processes in which these substances may be used as process agents.
In addition, it sets out a list of the substances that are prohibited for use in the manufacture, maintenance or servicing of products and equipment, including refilling, and in other processes, which contain controlled substances or new substances.
This regulation applies to:
any natural or legal person which:
produces, recovers, recycles, reclaims, uses or destroys such substances;
imports them;
exports them to a non-EU country;
places them on the European Union (EU) market;
operates refrigeration, air conditioning or heat pump equipment, or fire protection systems, that contain them.
Any undertaking that uses, refurbishing, refilling or servicing a product or equipment containing a controlled substance or new substance must inform the European Commission of its use.
The Commission may adopt measures to ensure compliance with the regulation’s rules. These measures must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
EU countries must ensure that:
there are no exceptions to the above-mentioned rules;
there is no derogation from the prohibition of the production of controlled substances and new substances in the following areas:
the production of new substances,
the import, import and export of such substances, and
the use of the chemicals used in these processes;
processes in which the substances are used as a process agent are prohibited.
There are exemptions for certain substances, such as:
chlorinated biphenyls,
nitrous oxide,
dioxins,
fluorocarbons,
sulphur dioxide,
methane,
pesticides,
polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs),
chlorine,
pentachloroethane and
nitrosamines.
Exemptions
The following substances are not covered by the regulation:
1.1.2.3.4.5 methyl bromide,

Summary 112:
European Union Agency for the Protection of the Environment (EPA)
European Union agency for the protection of the environment (EPA).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1210/90 — codification of Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1605/2002
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EEC) 1205/90.
It sets out the tasks and responsibilities of the European Union Agency (EPA), the EU’s environmental protection agency.
KEY POINTS
The Agency is responsible for:
the day-to-day administration of the Agency;
the performance of the tasks defined in Articles 12 and 13 of the regulation;
preparing and executing the decisions and programmes adopted by the Management Board; and
the preparation and publication of the reports.
The Executive Director is responsible:
for the proper preparation and execution of the decision-making process and the implementation of the management board's decisions, as well as for all staff matters;
for managing the Agency's budget.
EU countries must keep the Agency informed of the main component elements of their national environment information networks, especially in the priority areas, including any institution which in their judgment could contribute to the work of the agency, taking into account the need to ensure the fullest possible geographical coverage of their territory.
Each year, the Executive Director draws up a draft estimate of the revenue and expenditure for the following financial year. This is based on an estimate drawn up by the Agency on the basis of a draft draft draft. The Executive Director submits this estimate to the European Parliament and the European Council. The European Parliament, at the latter's request, at its request, for the smooth application of the discharge procedure for the financial year in question, as laid down in Articles 146(3) and 147(3).
The Management Board is composed of representatives of EU countries. It is chaired by the European Commission and comprises representatives of the Member States.
Decisions of the Board are adopted by a two-thirds majority of the members of the board, with the support of the Commission and the Council of the EU. The Agency is funded by the EU budget. The budget is in balance.
In addition, the Agency is assisted by the Institute for Reference Materials and Measurements (IRM).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The European Union (EU) Environment Agency is one of the most important bodies in the field of environmental protection. Its main tasks are to:
collect, analyse and disseminate information on the European environment;
coordinate the activities of national environmental information networks;
cooperate with EU countries in the areas of transfrontier, plurinational and global phenomena;
provide technical advice and guidance on the development of national information networks.
For more information, see:
European Environment Agency (European Commission).

Summary 113:
Registration of environmental organisations in the European Union (EU) (EMAS) — rules on environmental assessment and monitoring
Registration of European Union organisations (EAS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 761/2001 on the registration and monitoring of European environmental organisations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules on the EU’s environmental assessment* and monitoring* system, known as EMAS.
It aims to ensure that organisations are able to comply with EU environmental legislation.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
organisations are registered in accordance with the EMAS register;
they comply with the requirements of the regulation;
their environmental review, environmental policy, environmental management system and internal environmental audit and its implementation fulfils the requirements;
the organisation has been accredited or has been granted a licence by a competent body.
Organisations are required to:
ensure that their environmental review and environmental policy and environmental management systems are in line with the legislation;
establish and implement a comprehensive environmental management and monitoring system;
provide information on the environmental aspects of their activities;
implement an environmental management plan.
The regulation sets out a procedure for the accreditation and licensing of organisations. The competent bodies must:
conduct a conformity assessment and an internal audit;
assess the organisations’ compliance with the regulations;
conduct an environmental verification;
certify that the organisation has complied with the regulation.
If an organisation does not comply with these requirements, it must inform the competent bodies and the European Commission of the reasons for its withdrawal from the following categories of organisations:
non-governmental organisations
small and medium-sized organisations
environmental non-profit organisations
public interest organisations
research and development organisations
social partners.
National authorities must take appropriate measures to encourage the participation of small organisations, inter alia by:
facilitating access to information and support funds specially adapted to them;
ensuring that reasonable registration fees encourage their participation;
promoting technical assistance measures.
EMAS is a tool in the context of application and enforcement of EU environmental law. It can be used as a tool for:
monitoring the environmental status of organisations;
collecting and analysing information on environmental issues;
checking that organisations comply with environmental legislation; and
providing information on legal requirements relating to the environment in that EU country.
In addition, the regulation sets up a network of environmental accreditation bodies (NACE Codes) and a licensing body (Licensing Bodies).
The European Commission is assisted by a committee.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It applies from 11 January 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 11 January 2012.
BACKGROUND
The EMAS regulation is part of the framework for the implementation of the European Environment Action Programme (see summary).
For more information, see:
‘EMAS’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work website.
* KEY TERMS
* Environmental assessment: the conformity assessment process carried out by an environmental verifier to demonstrate whether an organisation's environmental review or environmental policy (environmental management system or environmental management) meets the legal requirements of EMAS and whether its implementation meets the requirements.

Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States: 11.1.2011

Summary 114:
Rules on the definition, description, presentation and labelling of spirit drinks (Regulation (EC) No 1576/89) and on the protection of geographical indications of certain spirit drinks [See amending act(s)].
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules applicable to spirit drinks.
It repeals and replaces Regulation (EEC) 1575/89.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks, regardless of their country of origin. It does not apply to:
wholly alcoholic drinks;
alcoholic drinks containing more than 0.1% alcohol;
spirits containing less than 1% alcohol.
Labelling
A spirit drink must bear the sales denomination ‘spirit drink’. It must also bear the name ‘slivovice’ in the same visual field on the front label.
Where there has been addition of alcohol, as defined in Annex I(5), diluted or not, to a spirit drink listed in categories 1 to 14 of Annex II, that spirit drink shall bear the label ‘spirits’ and may also use the name’s ‘S’ or ‘K’ on the back label. However, drinks falling within CN codes 2203, 2204, 2205, 2206 and 2207 may not be considered spirit drinks and must not be labelled as such.
If there is no alcohol in the product category, the label must state that the spirit drink is not alcoholic.
The label must also state:
a spirit drink has been produced using ethyl alcohol of agricultural origin;
the spirit drink contains a geographical indication of its origin.
an alcoholic content of 0.
the product must be accompanied by a description of its flavour and
the name of the country of manufacture.
a product must not contain more than 1.5% alcohol and
must not be flavoured.
In addition, a product may not contain:
sugars or other alcoholic drinks with a maximum methanol content of 5 grams per hectolitre of 100% vol.
grape marc spirit or grape marc marc may be sweetened to a maximum of 20 grams per litre expressed as invert sugar.
Annex III sets out rules for the labelling and presentation of spirits with a specific flavour.
Regulation No 2207 lays down the rules on the registration of spirits and their labelling.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts to modernise and simplify the rules relating to the regulation. These include:
the introduction of a harmonised labelling system for spirit drinks in the European Union (EU);
the harmonisation of labelling rules for alcoholic drinks and their presentation in the EU countries;
rules on the marketing of spirits.
For more information, see:
‘Spirits and their packaging’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Agri-foods: products produced from agricultural products.
* Agricultural products: products derived from the production of agricultural products, such as wine, beer, cider, rum, wine-based spirits, cidery spirits and cidery products, which are not alcoholic and which have a high alcohol content.
** Spirits: drinks with an alcoholic content that is higher than the percentage by volume of pure alcohol it represents in the total pure alcohol content by volume.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 1157/2006 of 12 December 2006 on the definitions, description and presentation, labelling, protection of the geographical indications and the regulation on spirit drinks of the Member States (OJ L 283, 14.12.2006, pp. 1-17)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1179/2006 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 115:
Safeguarding civil aviation
Securing civil aviation: common rules for safeguarding passengers, cargo and mail
Security of passengers, goods and mail: common European Union rules
Safer air transport: common EU rules for the protection of persons and goods
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common rules to protect passengers and goods travelling within the European Union (EU).
It aims to prevent acts of unlawful interference with civil aircraft that jeopardise the security of civil aviation.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
air carriers operating in the EU and on flights of air carriers licensed by it as well as to air carriers flying from non-EU countries;
government personnel who are specially selected and trained, taking into account the required security and safety aspects on board an aircraft.
EU countries must draw up, apply and maintain an airport security programme. This programme must be relevant, objective, non-discriminatory and proportional to the risk that is being addressed.
Each EU country must deploy in-flight security officers on aircraft registered in that EU country and in flights of airlines licensed by that country.
In addition, each EU country may decide whether to deploy security officers in the following areas:
on board aircraft;
on flights of an air carrier flying from an EU country to another EU country;
at airports.
Security officers must be trained and trained in accordance with the requirements of the Chicago Convention on International Civil Aviation and under the terms of that convention.
They must be able to carry out their duties in a manner that is appropriate to the safety of passengers and cargo;
in-related to the security and security aspects of the flight.
Airport security measures must be in place of proportionate and proportionate effect to the size of the aircraft and to the nature, scale or frequency of operations or of other relevant activities.
More stringent measures applied by EU countries may be justified by:
the size or the number of passengers;
the number of persons on board the aircraft; or
the nature or scale of the operations.
National civil aviation security programme
National authorities must ensure that:
a national security programme is drawn up, applied and maintained;
airport security officers are recruited and trained; and
airports are equipped and equipped with the necessary equipment and personnel.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Regulation (EC) No 849/2004 (OJ L 158, 30.6.2004, pp. 1-8)
It has applied since 20 June 2004.
BACKGROUND
   
The United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS) provides the basis for a common interpretation of Annex 17 to the Chicago convention on international civil aviation, which sets out the rules and standards applicable to the carriage of cargo, mail and passengers.
For more information, see:
‘Civil aviation security’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Inflight security officer: a security officer who is specially selected, trained and specially selected to take account of the safety aspects of passenger and cargo transport, and who is responsible for the security, safety and security of aircraft and passengers on board.
Cargo: a consignor who originates cargo or mail for its own account and whose procedures meet common security rules and standard requirements sufficient to allow carriage of goods or mail on any aircraft, but whose procedures do not meet the security requirements.

Council Regulation (EU) No 552/2008 of 20 May 2008 on the adoption, on behalf of the European Community, of common regulations for the safeguarding of persons, goods, and mail within the Union and repealing Council Regulation (EC, No 2320/2002 on the establishment of a European Union Aviation Safety Agency.
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, EC, EC) No 540/2008 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 116:
Restrictive measures against human rights violations and abuses: EU sanctions (2018) (Official Journal L 295 of 23.10.2018)
EU sanctions (18)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (CFSP) 2018/2018 on the freezing of funds and economic resources of individuals, entities and bodies responsible for, providing support to or otherwise involved in serious human rights abuses or abuses
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to impose sanctions on individuals and entities involved in human rights and human rights-related violations or abuses in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
individuals* and entities* responsible for:
human rights violations* and abuses* in the EU;
providing support to and otherwise engaging in such violations;
other actors exercising effective control or authority over a territory;
non-EU actors exercising control or authorities over a country;
state actors exercising effectively control or control over a region;
foreign non-state actors, subject to Article 1(4) of Decision 2020/1999.
EU countries must notify the European Commission and the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (the ‘High Representative’) of their decision-making procedures and the names and contact details of any persons, entities or bodies whose funds or economic resources are to be frozen in accordance with this regulation. The Commission must also notify the EU countries of any changes in the addresses of their websites listed in Annex II of the regulation.
The European Union’s financial sanctions map (Annex I to the list of persons, groups and entities subject to EU financial sanctions and the interactive sanctions map, as set out in the regulation, which is publicly available, must be made available to the public.
Actions must be taken only when there is reason to believe that they would infringe the measures laid down in this regulation, or that they are likely to infringe them. Actions by individuals or entities may not give rise to any liability of any kind on their part if they did not know, and had no reasonable cause to suspect, that their actions would violate the measures set out. The penalties provided for must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
To ensure maximum legal certainty within the EU, EU countries must inform the Commission of their competent authorities, including the contact details, of any frozen funds, economic resources and persons whose names and other relevant data are made public. They must also inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee (EEC) and the Commission.
In addition, the Commission must:
process personal data in order to carry out its tasks under this regulation;
add the contents of the annex to the electronic, consolidated list of people, groups or entities covered by this regulation and to the interactive EU sanctions map;
process information on the impact of the measures provided for in this Regulation such as the value of frozen funds and information on authorisations granted by the competent authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 23 October 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 23 November 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Human rights violations: violations of human rights or abuses committed in the context of a legitimate political, economic or social interest.
* Non-state actor: any person, entity or body that has the power to exercise effective control, authority or authority to exercise such control, exercise or authority in a territory.

Summary 117:
EU naval operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAFOR MED IRINI)
EU naval action in the Caribbean
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 on a European Union military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the EU to launch a military operation to combat human smuggling and trafficking in the eastern Mediterranean.
It sets out the tasks of the EU naval mission in the area of the Mediterranean, known as EUNAVFOR MEDIRINI.
KEY POINTS
The EU’s Political and Security Committee (PSC) exercises political control over the operation, providing it with strategic direction and taking the relevant decisions in accordance with the third paragraph of Article 38 of the Treaty on European Union (TEU).
The PSC is responsible for the political control and strategic direction of the operation.
The operation is based on the European Union Military Mission (EUMC) and the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).
It is designed to contribute to the implementation of UN measures to prevent the illicit export of petroleum from Libya, to capacity building and training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy, and to the disruption of the business model of human trafficking and trafficking networks.
As its core tasks, EUNAFOR Med IRINIs:
contribute to the UN arms embargo on Libya with aerial, satellite and maritime assets;
supports the UN measures against illicit exports of petroleum;
cooperates with the European Border and Coast Guard (EBS) in the Libyan coast guard and navy;
collects and stores personal data concerning persons involved in the carriage of prohibited items;
disrupts the trafficking and smuggling networks;
ensures that vessels are diverted to ports designated for the purpose of preventing human smuggling;
contains EU-led units and personnel in order to fulfil its tasks;
operates under the authority of the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (HR).
Operation Commander
Rear Admiral Fabio AGOSTINI is appointed EU Operation Commander.
EUMC
The European Union Mission in Libya (EUEMSA) is the operational command and control body for the EUNFORCE OF THE OPERATION
The decision authorises EUEMSA to:
operate in the maritime area of interest;
provide operational support to the EUMC and the EBS;
carry out its tasks under the operational control of the PSC.
FROM WHEN DOES THE COVID-19 DECISION APPLY?
The Decision has applied since 31 March 2020.
BACKGROUND
On 19 January 2020, a Conference was held in Berlin on Libya.
For more information, see:

Summary 118:
Fraudulent documents: the exchange of information on authentic and false ones (FADO system)
Formal and non-formal documents
Document fraud can undermine the internal security of the European Union (EU). To combat this threat, the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCG) set up the FADO (Fraud Analysis and Detection Online Online Online System) to exchange information on authenticity and on known methods of falsification between EU countries.
ACT
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 on the establishment of a European Union Agency for Law Enforcement Cooperation (EUBG) and establishing the Facilitation of the Exchange of Information on Authentic Documents and on Known Methods of Falsification between Member States.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The regulation establishes a common EU-wide information exchange system for authentic and falsified documents. It sets up the Facilitating of Access to Documents Online (FadO) system.
It establishes the EBCG’s role as the central point of contact for EU countries’ authorities in the field of document fraud.
KEY POINTS
The European Union agency responsible for the operation of the system, known as the EUBG Agency, is responsible for entering in the system the information it receives. The information it enters into the system consists of:
information on authentic documents, including images, and specimens thereof, and their security features;
information, including photographs, on false documents, whether forged, counterfeit or pseudo documents and their fraud characteristics;
summaries of forgery techniques;
details of the security features of authentic documents;
statistics on detected false documents.
The Agency must take the necessary steps to pseudonymise all elements of personal data which are not necessary in relation to the purposes for which the data are processed in accordance with the principle of data minimisation.
In addition, the Agency must:
ensure the uniformity and quality of the information that it receives;
provide the EU countries with support in the detection of false documents and information on the methods of their falsification;
assist the European Commission in its work.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
The regulations have applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been working towards a single European Union system of identification and verification of authentic and counterfeit documents for over a decade.
For more information, see:
‘Document Identification and Verification Online System’ on the Europol website.

Summary 119:
Proportionality of EU rules on access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions (Directive 2005/36/EC) (European Commission).
Penalties for infringements of the principle of proportionality in the field of the regulation of regulated professions.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS DIRECTIVE DO?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework for regulating the access to and pursuit of a regulated profession.
It requires EU countries to assess the proportionality of their requirements and to communicate the results of that assessment to the European Commission, launching a ‘mutual evaluation process’.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all regulated professions, including those that are regulated indirectly by giving a particular professional body the power to regulate.
EU countries must ensure that the measures they adopt are proportionate to the public interest and that they do not impose disproportionate restrictions on the access of professionals.
The measures must be justified on the grounds of:
the public interest;
the protection of workers’ rights; or
the improvement of the quality of life of consumers.
Where a regulation is justified on grounds of public interest, EU countries must take account of the fact that scientific and technological developments could reduce or increase the asymmetry of information between professionals and consumers. The introduction of additional requirements may be suitable to attain these objectives.
In addition, the regulation must be accompanied by an analysis of the appropriateness and proportional of the measure adopted by that EU country and by specific evidence substantiating its arguments.
Ex ante assessment of new measures and monitoring of new provisions
EU governments must assess the new or amended provisions after their adoption. They must immediately inform the Commission of the measures and their results. The Commission should facilitate that exchange.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Directive 2006/33/EC entered into force on 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
 
   
The regulation of the regulated professions is one of the general principles of EU law. It aims to ensure the proper functioning of the internal market and to avoid disproportionate restrictions.
For more information, see:
‘Regulation of the European Union’ on the Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Regulated profession: any profession which is regulated by an EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  барска гранда варка, г.р.2006, pp. 1-8
Successive amendments to Directive 2006/34/EC have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 120:
Fisheries management plan for the North Sea and Baltic Sea (2016-2021)
Fishers management plan: 2016–2021
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 laying down a multiannual plan for fisheries management in EU waters of the north sea and the Baltic Sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s strategy for the sustainable management of fish stocks in Union waters.
It aims to restore fish stocks, in the shortest time feasible, at least to levels that can produce maximum sustainable yield (MSY) as determined by their biological characteristics.
KEY POINTS
The plan covers:
the management of fishing opportunities;
the landing obligation for all stocks of species to which the landing obligation applies;
management of by-catch stocks; and
the conservation reference points to safeguard the full reproductive capacity of the stocks referred to in the plan.
The European Commission is responsible for drawing up the management plan. It is assisted by the International Council for the Exploration of the Sea (ICES) and the European Fisheries Management Agency (EFMA).
The management plan must:
contain a range of FMSY values for the stocks covered by the plan;
be based on the best available scientific advice;
provide a detailed description of the management measures to be taken to ensure that the plan is implemented;
ensure that fishing opportunities are set according to the best scientific advice available;
establish a total allowable catch (TAC) for a given year.
the plan must be updated every 5 years, taking into account the changes in fishing opportunities and the number of fishing vessels.
set out the management of the fishing opportunities for a certain stock.
Management of the plan
Management measures including, where appropriate, fishing opportunities, must be set taking account of scientific advice and must be consistent with the objectives laid down in Article 3 of the regulation.
In addition, the plan must provide for additional management measures for additional species to be further specified in accordance with Article 18 of that regulation. The plan must also specify details for the implementation of the EU landing obligation.
Where the Council takes into account a significant impact of recreational fisheries in the framework of the fisheries for a particular stock, it should be able to set a TAC for commercial catches which takes account of the volume of recreational catches and/or to adopt other measures restricting recreational fisheries such as bag limits and closure periods.
Fishing opportunities must be adjusted to take account of changes in the fishing conditions and the quantity of fish caught in the fishery.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has agreed to establish a single multiiannual fisheries management plan covering the whole North Sea, the Baltic and the Black Sea.
For more information, see:
North Sea fisheries management (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Maximum sustainable yield: the maximum yield of a fish stock over the long-term compared to its maximum yield. This is calculated using the range of Maximum Sustainable Yields for the period 2004-2020.
* Minimum sustainable yield for a stock: the minimum yield of fish stock that can be expected to yield MSY over a given period of time.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1308/2013 of 21 December 2013 laying down an annual plan for fishery management in European Union waters (North Sea, Baltic Sea and Black Sea) and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 423/2008 (OJ L 348, 20.12.2013, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2016/1380 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Council Decision (EU/EU) 2015/852/EU of 25 November 2015 on the implementation by the European Commission of the multiannually plan for fishing opportunities in European waters of certain stocks of certain fish species and repealing Regulations (EC, EC, EC) No 552/2008 and (EC), No 553/2008 of the European Parliament and of the Council of the Union concerning the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).

Summary 121:
Convention on the Law of the Sea — conservation and management of the marine resources of the South Pacific Fishery Management Organisation (SPFMO Convention Area)
CONVENTION ON THE LAW OF THE SOUTH PACIFIC FISHING MANAGEMENT AND CONTROL MEASURES OF THE SPRFMO CONVENTION AREA
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 — management, conservation and control measures relating to certain fishing methods in the south Pacific fishery management area
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the conservation, conservation, management and control of certain methods of fishing for certain marine resources in the southern Pacific Fisheries Management Organisation.
It aims to ensure that these methods are sustainable and do not lead to overfishing.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fishing for:
jack mackerel;
seabirds;
sharks;
marine mammals;
fishermen’s equipment;
bottom fishing.
EU countries must:
report to the European Commission on the catches of bottom fishing species from the preceding month in accordance with Article 33 of the regulation;
ensure that the Commission receives a report every 15 days on the number of catches of top fishing species.
The Commission must also collect data on:
the number of vessels registered in each EU country;
the total number of fishing vessels registered and registered in the EU;
total number of commercial fishing vessels in each year.
Fishing methods
the types of fishing methods employed and the number and quantities of fish caught.
FROMISSIONS
Each year, EU countries must send a report to the Commission on their catches of the following:
captive fish caught by bottom fishing vessels;
jacks caught by top fishing vessels.
In addition, the Commission must collect:
data on the total number and quantity of commercial vessels registered, registered or registered in an EU country,
number of vessels in the SPRFishing vessel registration system
number and type of fishing vessel registration;
details of the unloading fishing vessel.
If the Commission considers that a fishing vessel is not complying with the regulation, it must inform the European Maritime Safety Agency (ESA) of the reasons for its withdrawal from the regulation.
Control of third country fishing vessels
Third-country fishing vessels are subject to the following control measures:
control of vessel registration, registration number and IHS Fairplay number (if applicable);
the use of large-scale pelagic driftnets and all deepwater gillnets;
use of bird scaring (tori) lines in use;
monitoring of the use of bird bafflers and other bycatch mitigation measures employed;
records of the amount (weight or volume) of remaining marine resources not recorded under point (j), (k) or (l) discarded, split to the lowest known taxon.
Monitoring and reporting of the EU countries’ compliance with Regulation (EU) 2018/852
The European Commission must send the Commission a report on the results of its monitoring and reporting activities every 3 years. The Commission must forward that information to the Secretariat at least 30 days before the changes take effect. It must also forward the information it receives from the Commission to the United Nations Maritime Organization (UNMOM) and the European Fisheries Management Organization (EFMO) at least 3 months before the change takes effect.
Bycatch mitigation and conservation measures must be in place to minimise the impact of bycatch on the marine environment and to prevent the spread of disease, parasites and other threats to marine life. These measures must include:
preventing the spread and spread of diseases, parasites, diseases and bycatch;
collecting and analysing the quantities of marine life that are likely to be affected by bycatch, including bycatch and parasites;
keeping records of the number, type, quantity and types of marine mammals and birds caught;
reporting on the amount of marine resources caught and disposed of;
assessing the impact on the environment and the conservation of the biodiversity of the sea.
Reporting
The EU country must report the following to the EU Commission every 3 months:
categories of fishing species;
catch of bottom-fishing species; and
total catches.
Data reporting
The data must be collected on the following fishing methods:
top fishing;
captives’ equipment and equipment used;
unloading fishing vessels’ number of registered fishing vessels and registration numbers;
ships’ registration numbers and IMO number;
shipments and transshipment times;
quantitative data on the quantity of fishing gear used.

number, type and



Summary 122:
Unaccompanied cash: rules on the disclosure of cash in the EU (Regulation (EC) No 1889/2005 on controls of cash entering or leaving the EU)
Unaccompanied money: controls on the distribution of cash (Regulations (EU) 2016/679, 2016/865 and 2016/965)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 865/2014 on the obligation to disclose unaccompanied cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common EU-wide system of controls on unaccompanied cash.
It aims to prevent the introduction of illicit proceeds into the EU’s economy and the diversion of money to finance illicit activities.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following categories of cash:
currency, bearer-negotiable instruments, commodities used as highly-liquid stores of value and certain types of prepaid cards.
In addition, it applies to:
unaccompanied cash*;
cash used as a highly liquid store of value;
currency with a gold content of at least 90%;
covert cash;
prepaid cards*.
The competent authorities of EU countries have the power to carry out controls on any consignments, receptacles or means of transport which may contain unaccompanied cash, in accordance with the conditions laid down in national law.
If the competent authorities believe that a consignment of cash does not comply with the regulation, they may carry out random checks or spontaneous controls whenever they deem necessary.
A declaration must contain the following information:
the origin, destination, economic provenance and intended use of the cash, as well as possible;
the name and address of the person or entity responsible for the cash; and the date and place of arrival of the consignment.
Ex officio declaration
In the event of failure to comply with these obligations, the competent authority must compose an ex officia declaration for subsequent transmission of the relevant information to other authorities. The declaration must be delivered to the declarant upon request.
EU countries must also:
ensure that the declaration contains the details of the persons or entities involved in the transport of cash, including the name, address, date and date of arrival, and the identity of the individual or entities responsible for it;
establish a common risk management framework for the exchange of information between competent authorities and with the European Commission;
provide the Commission with statistical information on its website on the number and type of unaccompanied cash that has entered or left the EU and the number of persons involved in its transport.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to combat the illicit flow of cash into and out of the EU, including:
Directive 91/308/EEC on controls on cash entering and leaving the European Union (see summary);
Regulation No 89/2005 establishing a common European Union system of cash controls;
Regulations No 45/2001 (9) and No 10/2006 (10) of the European Parliament and of the Council on the control of cash leaving the Community;
Commission Implementing Decision (EU), Euratom and Euratom Implementing Regulation (Euratom) 2018/935 on the sharing of information on the use of cash and on the protection of personal data.
For more information, see:
‘Unaccompanied Cash’ on the Euratom website.

Summary 123:
Energy Union — national energy and climate plans
Energy Union: national energy plans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — energy union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European energy union (EEU).
It aims to ensure that the EU’s energy security, the internal energy market, energy efficiency, decarbonisation and research, innovation and competitiveness are aligned.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the five dimensions of the energy union:
energy security;
internal energy market;
energy efficiency;
decarbonisation; and
research, innovation, competitiveness.
EU countries must:
establish a national energy security and climate action plan for each of these five dimensions;
ensure that the national energy market and energy efficiency strategies are aligned;
develop strategies to support the development of efficient and decarbonised energy infrastructure;
cooperate with signatories to the Paris Agreement and with third-country members of the European Economic Area (EEA) and with non-EU countries.
National plans must be drawn up and updated every 5 years. They must include:
a long-term renovation strategy for the renovation of the national stock of residential and non-residential buildings, both public and private;
measures to stimulate cost-effective deep renovation and policies and actions to target the worst performing segments of the building stock, in accordance with Article 2a of Directive 2010/31/EU;
a description of measures to develop measures to utilise energy efficiency potentials of gas and electricity infrastructure.
In addition to the national plans, EU countries may also envisage cooperation with signatory to the Energy Community agreements and with other countries of the EU.
The European Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of the regulation by 31 December each year. This report must include information on the progress made by EU countries in implementing their national plans and the progress they have made in implementing them.
Each year, the Commission must provide the Commission with data for the preceding calendar year related to compliance with Article 7 of Directive 98/70/EC, as defined in Annex III to this Directive. The Commission must also provide the European Energy Agency (EAA) with a report on the compliance of EU countries with this directive.
Every 5 years, the EAA must present its recommendations to EU countries on how to implement their national energy strategies.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 98/71/EC entered into force on 4 November 2016.
It has applied since 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
The Energy Union covers five dimensions: energy security; the internal market; energy efficiency; decarbonisations; and research and innovation.
For more information, see:
‘Energy Union’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Energy Union: an energy union is defined as a set of policies, measures and actions that aim to improve the efficiency, security and competitiveness of energy systems in the European Union (EU).
* Decarbonisation: the aim of reducing the total amount of carbon dioxide emitted into the air and water.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EEU) No 852/2018 of 4 November 2018 laying down a European Energy Union (Energy Union) for the period 2018-2022 (OJ L 157, 5.11.2018, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, EEA) No852/18 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 124:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2014 on the control of chemicals and their storage in EU countries (recast)
Recast of Regulation (EC, EC, EC) No 1888/2006 on the controls on chemicals and its storage
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the control and management of chemical stockpiles* in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all substances listed in the Convention and in the Protocol as well as substances listed only in the convention.
It does not apply to chemicals listed in Annex II to the Convention on the Registration, Evaluation, Authorisation and Restriction of Chemicals (REACH).
EU countries must:
monitor the use, management and storage of notified stockpiles of chemicals;
ensure that these stockpiles comply with the regulation’s rules;
establish a European Chemicals Agency (ECA) to monitor their use and management;
take appropriate measures to prevent, control and restrict the use of chemicals in their stockpiles.
Exemptions from control measures
Certain substances are excluded from the regulation, such as:
tetrabromodiphenyl ether (TPDD);
chlorinated biphenyls (CPDDs);
cyclic aromatic hydrocarbons;
dioxins;
nitrous oxide.
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring the use and managing the stockpiles and the control measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULCELEBRATION APPLACE?
   
It has applied since 16 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
It repeals Regulation (EU) No 850/2004 (see summary).
It applies from 16 July 2021.
For more information, see:
Chemical control measures (European Commission).

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products: placing on the market and reducing their impact on the environment (SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2019/30/EU on the placing on market and reduction of plastic waste
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce the number of plastic products found on European Union (EU) beaches and fishing gear containing plastic and products made from oxo-degradable plastic.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all plastic products that are found the most on beaches in the EU. It does not apply to fishing gear, food intended for young children, food for infants and food for the environment.
EU countries must:
ban the placing of these products on their market and the reduction measures taken;
monitor the consumption of such products;
ensure that these measures are effective, proportionate and dissuasive;
establish a system of extended producer responsibility* for these products.
The European Commission must carry out an evaluation of the directive by 3 July 2027. It must also report to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the European Commission on the progress made by EU countries in implementing the directive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
From 3 July 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 3.7.2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the world’s largest exporter of plastic. It is also the largest consumer of plastic and the largest plastic producer in the world over. This means that it is responsible for the most of the plastic that ends up on EU beaches.
It is found on the European seas.
This directive aims to tackle the problem of plastic litter by:
restricting the use of plastic on beaches;
reducing the amount of plastic used in fishing gear;
introducing measures to reduce plastic waste.
For more information, see:
‘Plastic on the EU’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Single-use product: a plastic product that is not designed for re-use or cost-effective recycling and which is not intended for reuse or recycling.
** Fishing gear: a fishing gear made from plastic and which contains plastic or is made of plastic or made of other substances that are not designed to be re-used or cost effective.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2019/29/EU of 3 June 2019 on the putting on the Market and the Reduction of Single-Use Plastic Waste (SOLUTIONS) (OJ L 157, 3.6.2019, pp. 1-18)
