Summary 126:
EU trade mark registration and enforcement — rules for the Office for the Registration of EU Trade Marks (OJ L 347, 28.11.2016, pp. 1-8)
EU trade sign registration and enforceability — rules on the registration, enforcement and enforcement of EU trade marks (Joint Regulation (EU) No 182/2011)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 laying down the rules and implementing acts for the registration of the EU trade mark
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on how the European Union Trade Marks Office (OLAF) works.
It aims to ensure that trade marks are protected and enforceable throughout the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for:
the registration of a trade mark;
the enforcement of the trade mark and the enforcement of its enforcement;
rules on the application for a licence to use an EU Trade Mark.
The rules apply to the following types of goods and services:
goods and services for which the applicant has applied:
a trade mark conferred on a person or a business;
an application for an exclusive or non-exclusive licence;
any other goods or services conferred on the applicant.
Application procedure
The applicant must submit an application to the OLAF. The application must be accompanied by a declaration of division. The declaration must contain the following information:
an indication of the goods and/or services concerned;
a description of the name of the person or business concerned.
the name and address of the applicant’s place of business.
If the applicant fails to fulfil the conditions of the application, the applicant must lodge a complaint with the Office of the European Parliament and the Council. The Office then decides whether to accept or reject the complaint. If it rejects the claim, it must inform the applicant of the reasons for its decision. If the applicant is unable to prove its case, it may lodge an appeal before the Board of Appeal of the Office, which decides on the appeal. The appeal may be brought before the Court of Justice of the Union or before the European Court of Auditors.
When an application is rejected, the Office must notify the owner of any deficiencies in the application. If a deficiency is not remedied within a period to be specified by the office, the office may reject the claim. If, however, the deficiency is rectified within a specified period, the application may be renewed.
In the event of a dispute over the validity of an application, a dispute resolution procedure may be followed. The dispute resolution process may include oral proceedings before the Office. The losing party must bear the costs for one opposing party only and, where applicable, one representative only.
Cancellation procedures
The Office may decide to cancel an application if it considers that the application is invalid. The cancellation takes effect from the day following the date on which the existing registration expired. The decision may also be made by the Executive Director of the office. The office may also decide to suspend an application for renewal if the applicant does not comply with the conditions laid down in the regulation or if the application has not been properly investigated.
Examiners
The OLAF has a number of tasks, including:
examiners;
examination of the applications;
cancelling the application;
drawing up the register;
clearing up any errors in the registration;
keeping the register for an indefinite period of time;
checking that the registration has been complied with;
disseminating the results of the examination and the publication of the registration.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU Trade Trademark Office has been in operation since 2004.
For more information, see:
EU Trade Marks — Register (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trade mark: any trade mark that has been registered in the EU and which has been conferred on one or more products or services.
* Exemptions: a trademark which is not used for the purpose of marketing, advertising or promotional purposes.
ACT
Commission Implementing Act (EC) No 184/2011 of 27 December 2011 laying down rules and establishing procedures for the establishment, enforcement, registration, cancellation and enforcement, as well as for the transfer of an EU trademark (codified version) and repealing Council Regulation (Euratom) (Official Journal L 183, 29.12.2011).

Summary 127:
Accessible formats for copyright and related rights
Assistance for persons with disabilities
Accessibility for persons who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 on the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain works and other subject matter
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright or related rights in order to make and disseminate accessible formats for certain works, other protected subject matter and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
books, audio books, e-books, radio broadcasts and radio broadcasts.
It does not apply to films or television programmes.
Distribution, communication and making available of accessible format copies
The distribution, communication or making available to the public of accessible formats in an EU country must be carried out on a non-profit basis by authorised entities established in that EU country.
Authorised entities must ensure that:
the content of the accessible format copy is in a language easily understood by people with disabilities;
the authorisation has been granted by the competent authority of the EU country in which it is to be made;
authorised entities are responsible for ensuring that the content is in an accessible format.
The authorisation must also be granted for:
accessibility of the authorisation;
transmission of the authorised format copy;
exchange of the adapted e-book, audio book or radio broadcast between authorised entities in the EU and in non-EU countries.
Exceptions or limitations
Authorisations may not restrict the accessibility of a work, subject matter or authorisation to persons who:
are blind or visually impaired;
have a disability; or
are unable to read or write.
Import and use
A beneficiary person or an authorised entity established in a EU country may import or otherwise obtain or access and thereafter use, in accordance with the national legislation adopted pursuant to Directive (EU, 2017/564) and Directive 2001/29/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 12 July 2002 concerning the processing of personal data and the protection of privacy in the electronic communications sector (Directive on privacy and electronic communications).
Exemptions and limitations
An authorised entity may only import, communicate or make available to a person who is:
visual impaired or visually blind;
print-disabled;
aroused or partially sighted.
In addition, the authorising authority may not prohibit the import or use of:
a work or subject matter that has been distributed, communicated or made available to beneficiary persons or to authorised entities;
an authorised entity in a third country that is a party to the Marrakesh Treaty;
accessible format copies that have been distributed or communicated to a public or to a private organisation.
Obligations of authorised entities
Authorisation entities must:
ensure that the authorisations granted for the making or dissemination of the accessible format copy are respected;
take the necessary measures to ensure that the accessibility of these copies is not affected by copyright or other related rights;
develop guidelines or best practices on making and disseminating these accessible formats.
EU countries must provide the European Commission with the necessary information for the preparation of an evaluation report.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Accessible format: a format in which a person with a disability is able to read, write or listen to a written or audiovisual message.
* Authorised entity: a company or organisation that has the legal right to make or disseminate a copy of a protected work or a subject matter.
REFERENCES

Deadline for transposition in the Member States

Summary 128:
Coordinated investigation and enforcement of infringements in the EU — EU consumer protection rules
COORDINATED INVESTIGATION AND ENFORCE MECHANISMS
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — coordinated investigation and implementation of the European Union’s consumer protection law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities investigate and enforce infringements that affect consumers.
It sets out rules on coordinated investigations and enforcement.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
cooperate in investigating and enforcing infringements with a European Union dimension;
coordinate their enforcement actions with other EU countries and the European Commission;
ensure that their enforcement priorities are aligned with those set out in the regulation.
The European Commission is responsible for coordinating the enforcement activities of the competent authorities, other public authorities referred to in Article 6 and, if applicable, designated bodies, in relation to infringements covered by this regulation. The Commission is also responsible for the coordination of the enforcement actions of the EU countries.
Each EU country must establish a single liaison office to coordinate the investigation, enforcement and training of its officials involved in the application of the regulation, as well as the development of sector-specific networks of officials. The liaison office must also be responsible for translating the notifications, communications and other documents that it receives from other competent authorities.
Inspections and enforcement actions must be coordinated with the Commission. Where investigations reveal that an infringement might be taking place, EU countries must start the coordinated action by taking the necessary measures. If an EU-wide investigation reveals that such an infringement, they must take the necessary steps to take the appropriate enforcement measures.
If an infringement is taking place with an EU dimension, the EU country concerned must inform the Commission of its decision. The European Commission must then inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the measures taken to tackle the infringement.
When an EU country takes the necessary action to tackle an infringement with a Union dimension, it must notify the Commission and the other countries concerned.
Where an infringement takes place with a global dimension, a coordinated enforcement action must be taken by the Commission with the help of other EU country authorities. The enforcement action should be coordinated by the European Anti-Fraud Office (EAFO).
The Commission must notify each EU country of its enforcement priorities within 2 years of the submission of information on enforcement priorities and inform other countries and EU countries accordingly. In cases involving a substantial change of circumstances or of market conditions during the 2 years after the last submission, the enforcement priorities must be updated.
A database containing information on infringements must be established for the purpose of collecting, classifying and exchanging data on consumer complaints. It must be accessible to all EU countries, the Commission, the EAFO and other designated bodies.
Information may also be communicated to an authority of a non-EU country by a competent authority under a bilateral assistance agreement with that country, provided that the approval of the authority that originally communicated the information has been obtained, and provided that it is in accordance with EU law regarding the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data.
An EU country may also communicate information to a third country if it considers that the information it receives is necessary for its own investigation or enforcement. However, those entities must not have access to that database.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  *   * * 
For more information, see:
EU consumer protection (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  [Official Journal L 165 of 18.6.2018]
Council Regulation (EC) No 1852/2018 of 21 December 2018 on the coordinated investigation of consumer protection infringements (OJ L 165, 18.5.2018, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) No1852/18 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices: European Union rules on safety and health
In vitro diagnostics: EU rules on safe and sustainable regulation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on the safety and quality of medical devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU)-wide regulatory framework for in vitro diagnostic* medical devices.
It aims to ensure that in vitro medical devices* are safe and safe, while supporting innovation.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all medical devices, including:
in vitro diagnostic devices* for the diagnosis and monitoring of human diseases;
diagnostic medical devices for the monitoring and testing of human and animal health;
medical devices used to diagnose, monitor, monitor or monitor the health of animals;
electromagnetic devices* used to monitor the human body’s immune system.
Manufacturers must:
conduct a safety and clinical performance evaluation, including a performance evaluation report;
conduct an ethical review;
ensure that their products comply with the requirements laid down in the regulation.
The European Medicines Agency (EMA) is responsible for ensuring that manufacturers, distributors and economic operators comply with these requirements.
EU countries must ensure that manufacturers and distributors cooperate with the competent authorities on any action taken to eliminate the risks posed by devices which they have made available on the market.
Distributors must cooperate with competent authorities, at their request, on any actions taken to reduce the risks to the health and safety of their customers.
Imporporters must ensure their products are labelled as single-use devices and that they have been sterilised and packaged in a way that minimises the need for sterilisation before use. They must also ensure that their devices are labelled in a language that is easily understood by consumers.
Regulators must ensure the following information is available to the public:
the name or trade name of the device;
the device version or model;
labelled as single use;
packaged sterile;
quantity of devices provided in a package;
required for sterilization before use; and
critical warnings or contra-indications.
Evaluation and conformity assessment
The manufacturer must carry out an assessment of the devices’ safety, clinical and technical performance, including an evaluation report, and submit a report to the competent authority. The report must contain a summary of the results of the evaluation and a description of the products’ design, manufacture, design, testing and testing methods, as well as of the risks associated with their use.
An electronic system for registration of economic operators must be in place to ensure the proper functioning of the electronic system. The regulation sets out the structure, responsibilities, procedures, processes and management resources required to implement the principles and actions necessary to achieve compliance with the provisions of this regulation. It also sets out a list of the economic operators that must be involved in the supply chain.
A European Union database on medical devices must be established. This database must be updated every 5 years to reflect technological developments in the medical devices market. It must also be updated to reflect the technological progress that has taken place since the regulation came into force.
In addition, the regulation sets up the European Union Device Identification and Traceability Database (UDI-DI). This database contains information on:
manufacturers’ products;
distributors’ devices;
devices’ labelling and labelling information;
products’ serial numbers;
procedures for their identification and traceability;
registers and certificates;
authorisations issued to manufacturers;
information on the EU-wide system for the registration and authorisation of manufacturers and their distributors.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
Directive 2009/43/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 16 December 2009 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the regulation on the regulation of the Safety and Quality of In vitro Diagnostic Medical Devices (see summary).
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
In 2015, the European Commission adopted Directive 2009/44/EC establishing a European Medicinal Devices Directive. This Directive establishes a framework for the regulation to be implemented in line with the EU Medicines Directive.
For more information, see:
EU medical devices directive (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* In vitro diagnostic device: a medical device used to detect, monitor and monitor human or animal health.
* Single use: a device that can be used to measure the presence or absence of a specific biological or chemical substance in the blood or urine of a patient.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) No 550/2014 of 21 December 2014 on the Safety, Quality and Labelling of Medical Devices for the Monitoring and Testing of Human and Animal Health (OJ L 347, 28.12.2014, pp. 1-8)




Summary 130:
Electrical appliances and fittings — EU rules on their conformity assessment and conformity assessment (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
Electrical and electronic appliances and fitting — Union rules on its conformity assessment
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2016 on the harmonisation of the rules on the conformity assessment of electrical and electronic products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the harmonising of the EU’s rules on conformity assessment, conformity assessment* and conformity-assessment procedures for the sale and putting into service of electrical appliances* and electronic fittings.
It repeals Directive 2009/142/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
electrical appliances* intended for use in residential, commercial, industrial or institutional use;
electromagnetic appliances intended for the use of heating, cooling, air conditioning or air-conditioning systems;
gas-operated household appliances intended to be placed into service.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
they have carried out a conformity assessment before placing into service any appliances or fittings on the EU market;
they carry out conformity assessment prior to putting them into service;
the conformity assessment is carried out by an authorised representative appointed by the manufacturer.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any non-compliance with the regulation.
The Commission must, by means of implementing acts and, given their special nature, determine whether measures taken by EU countries in respect of non-compliant appliances or fittingings are justified or not.
Imporporporters and importers must inform the Commission of the name and postal address of the importer of the appliance or fitting. The Commission must then notify the manufacturer of any infringements of the regulation and notify the EU countries of any measures taken to deal with such infringements.
In addition, the Commission must:
ensure that the manufacturer, importer, dealer or any other authorised representative of the manufacturer carries out an independent conformity assessment;
conduct an audit to check that the appliance and fitting comply with the legislation;
check that the appliances are compatible with the local gas supply conditions;
carry out market surveillance and control of the products entering the EU.
If the Commission considers that a product does not conform to the regulation, it must take the necessary measures to ensure that it is withdrawn from the market or withdrawn from service. These measures may include criminal penalties for serious infringements and, where appropriate, a ban on the sale of the product.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
Directive 2009/141/EC has applied since 21 April 2018.
Amending act(s)

The EU has also adopted Implementing Decision (EU), which sets out common rules on:
consolidated rules for electrical appliances, electronic equipment, electrical and industrial appliances and electronic components intended for household use (EU-wide directive on the labelling and labelling of appliances, accessories and related products) (EU regulation on the certification of appliances and accessories);
EU regulation (EC, EC, EC and EC) No 652/2008 on the technical harmonisation and standards of conformity assessment procedures for electrical, electronic and industrial products intended for commercial use (EC and EC).

Summary 131:
Cross-border transport of euro cash: rules for cash-in-transit (CIT) companies
Cross-boundary transport of euros: checks for cross-border cash
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009 — rules for the cross-bORDER transport of cash
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the transport by road and by rail of euro-denominated cash in and out of the euro area.
It aims to protect the safety of the public and the economy of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
cross-border deliveries/pick-ups of euros by road between EU countries;
point-to-point transport by rail;
cash transport by air;
custodial transport.
EU countries must ensure that:
the applicant company, its CIT security staff, vehicles, vehicles and security procedures employed in or applied for the purposes of crossing-border traffic comply with this regulation or, where expressly referred to in this regulation, with national law specifically relating to the transport of money;
the transport arrangements used by the applicant company comply with the relevant national rules.
The applicant company must notify the European Commission of the transport arrangements which are applicable in accordance with the regulation. The Commission must publish a corresponding information notice in the Official Journal of the EU country in which the transport is to be carried out.
In addition, EU countries must:
provide the Commission with a list of the types of transport arrangements that are covered by this regulation;
ensure that the CIT vehicle, its security staff and vehicles and the security procedures used for the purpose of transport comply with these arrangements;
identify the persons, companies and bodies responsible for carrying out these transport arrangements, as well as the persons responsible for their security.
CIT transport arrangements must be:
conform with the rules laid down in Articles 8 and 9 of Regulation (EU) 2017/852 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States with regard to the carriage of cash;
comply with the requirements laid down by the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions;
not exceed the number of euro deliveries or pick-ups that a CIT company may carry out in a host EU country during the same day;
cooperate with the national authorities of the other Member State in order to ensure that the cash transported complies with the law of that country;
have the necessary security measures in place to prevent, detect and neutralise any risks to human health and the environment;
be able to take advantage of the cash services of the nearest national central bank (NCB) branch or CIT cash centre, even if it is located in another Member State.
Security measures must be in place at all stages of transport, including:
before, during and after the transport;
before the start of the journey;
after the end of the trip;
in the event of an accident;
during the journey.
There must be at least two security staff per vehicle.
National authorities must ensure the following:
that:
each CIT transport vehicle has at least one security staff;
there are at least 2 security staff in each vehicle;
each security staff has a valid professional weapons licence or authorisation issued by the country where the applicant’s employer is established;
at least 1 security staff is authorised to carry out the transport, and at least 3 security staff must be authorised to operate the vehicle, and there must be a minimum number of security staff.
If a security staff does not have a licence, it must be validated by the competent authority of the country of the host country.
A security staff may be authorised only if he or she has been trained in the relevant professional weapons training in the Member State where the employer has its head office.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission published a communication on the implementation of the regulation in December 2009.
For more information, see:
Euro cash transport (European Commission).
* KEY TERMS
* Cross-borders: a transport route where cash is transported between two or more EU countries by road or rail.
* Euro cash transport: a means of transport in which cash is delivered or picked-up.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (Euratom) No 1108/2009 of 15 December 2009 on the cross border transport of Euro cash (codified version) (OJ L 183, 1.1.2010, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, Euratom) no 1108 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 132:
Foods — mandatory nutrition information (2014-2020)
Foods— mandatory nutrition Information (2014 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 608/2004 on mandatory food information
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the mandatory labelling and labelling of foods in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that the information contained in the nutrition declaration on foods is clearly visible, understandable and understandable.
It also aims to encourage food business operators to provide on a voluntary basis the information that may be omitted from the declaration.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
foods with a maximum weight of more than 100 g;
alcoholic beverages and non-prepacked foods that may not be exempted from the regulation’s requirements;
non-foods that are not intended for human consumption.
The European Commission must submit reports to the European Parliament and the Council every 3 years on the implementation of the regulation.
EU countries must indicate the country of origin or place of provenance of the food they produce or the place of origin for:
meat other than beef;
milk, milk used as an ingredient in dairy products;
unprocessed foods;
processed food;
single ingredient products; and single ingredient products containing more than 50 % of a food.
In addition, food businesses must provide the following information:
the number of grams of protein, carbohydrate, fat, protein, protein and carbohydrate;
the quantity of fat, carbohydrate and protein;
a description of the composition of the ingredients;
an indication of the type of meat and the quantity of protein or carbohydrate.
Food businesses must also provide information on:
total carbohydrate content
the amount of protein in a product
the percentage of carbohydrate in a food
the total number of protein grams and carbohydrate content of a product.
Exemptions
Certain foods may be excluded from the definition of ‘mechanically separated meat’ and ‘milk’.
There are also certain foods which are exempt from the requirement of the declaration, such as:
alcohol,
alcohol and alcoholic beverages
wholly processed foods
alcohol-free foods
whole-grain foods
pre-packaged foods
food with added phytosterols
phytosterol esters, phytostanols or phytosanoids
polyphenols
polyglycerols
glyceryl esters
polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons
polymer esters and polyphenols.
Certain food businesses may also provide the information in Annex V to the regulation on the quantity and composition of vitamins, minerals and other substances.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive (EU) 2018/852 amends Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 652/2004. It repeals Directive 87/250/EEC.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of rules on food labelling. These include:
rules on the labelling, packaging and presentation of foods, including rules on labelling (see summary);
rules for labelling alcoholic beverages, non-alcoholic drinks, foodstuffs, food additives and food additives;
rules to ensure the consistency of labelling on foods.
For more information, see:
‘Food labelling’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Mechanically separated: a product is separated from other products by means of a mechanical separator.

Summary 133:
Issuing of euro coins and collector coins
Issuance and circulation coins and collectors coins
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 974/98 on the introduction of the euro
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the issuing and circulation of euro and cent coins.
It aims to ensure that euro coins are legal tender in all EU countries whose currency is the euro.
KEY POINTS
EU countries may only issue two types of euro coin: circulation coins* and commemorative coins*.
The circulation coins must be issued and put into circulation at face value. They must comply with the denominations and technical specifications laid down by the European Central Bank (ECB).
Collector coins must have the status of legal tender only in the issuing EU country.
The European Commission must conduct an impact assessment on the continued issuance of 1- and 2-cent coins. The Commission must also take all appropriate measures to discourage the use of collector coins as a means of payment.
Each EU country must issue two commemororative coins per year, save where:
commemorative coins are collectively issued by all the EU countries; or
a commemorative coin is issued on the occasion of a temporary vacancy or a provisional occupation of the function of Head of State.
In addition, each EU country may issue up to 2,000,000 euro collector coins, subject to certain conditions:
the number of coins to be issued must be approved by the ECB;
the quantity of coins must not exceed 10 million;
they must be valid for a maximum of 5 years.
collector
collectors must be informed of the volume of coins they are authorised to issue.
the amount of coins authorised for circulation must be submitted to the ECB for approval.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE?
The regulation has applied since 20 June 2012.
BACKGROUND
The euro is the single currency of the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘The euro’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 134:
European Social Fund (ESF) — Youth Employment Initiative (YEI)
European Society for the Improvement of Employment and Inclusion (ESI) – Youth Employment and Integration
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the European Social Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the ESF’s role in the youth employment and inclusion (YEI) programme.
KEY POINTS
The ESF is the main source of funding for social policy in the European Union (EU). Its main objective is to support the social inclusion of young people in the labour market. This is achieved by:
promoting equal opportunities between men and women;
supporting young people not in employment, education or training (NEET), who are unemployed or inactive;
encouraging young people to take part in the economy and society.
The regulation sets out specific rules for the implementation of ESF funding for the YEI, including:
programming arrangements;
participation of EU countries in the programme;
promotion of gender equality;
coordination between EU countries and the European Commission;
monitoring and evaluation of the operational programmes;
transnational cooperation.
Programming arrangements may take one or more of the following forms:
a dedicated operational programme, a dedicated priority axis within an operational programme or a part of one of the priority axes;
a part of a transnational cooperation programme involving at least two EU countries.
the European Commission is involved in the preparation, implementation and monitoring of the programmes.
EU countries may also choose to participate in transnational programmes involving partners from non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1), Ireland (1) and the United States (1).
The European Commission and the EU countries are responsible for monitoring and evaluating the programmes and the results of the operations.
Information and communication measures
The annual implementation reports and the final report as referred to in Article 50(1 and (2) and (3) of the regulation must include additional information on the implementation. The Commission must attend the European Parliament's annual debate on those reports.
In addition, the Commission must:
report annually on the progress made in implementing the programme and on the results achieved;
provide a report on the impact of the programme on employment and inclusiveness;
present a report every 2 years on the effectiveness of the implementation and results achieved.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU’S social policy objectives are set out in the EU budget. The ESF contributes to these objectives by providing financial support to the social partners and the public authorities.
For more information, see:
European social policy (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
 
  * Youth Employment & Inclusion: a programme that aims to improve the quality of life for young people and young people's families. It is designed to support, in particular, young people who are excluded from employment or training, but also young persons not in education, training or employment, who are inactive or unemployed.
* Gender: the number of women and men who are actively involved in society and who have a positive impact on the economy.

Summary 135:
Population and vital events — EU countries’ annual statistics
Statistics and statistics on population
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 — annual demographic statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of national and regional statistics on the EU’s population.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on their population and on vital events during the reference period (2013-2027).
The data cover the following variables:
live births by sex, month of occurrence, live birth-order, age, country of birth, region of residence, nationality and country of citizenship;
deaths by age, sex, year of birth.
The reference period for vital events data must be the calendar year in which the events occurred.
Each EU country must use the same population definition that they use for the data referred to in paragraph 1.
Eurostat must carry out feasibility studies on the use of the definition of ‘usual residence’ for population and for the estimation of the ‘usually resident resident’ population. The results of these studies must be sent to the Economic and Financial Affairs Committee of the European Parliament and of the Council of the EU, which assesses the long-term sustainability and quality of public finances on the basis of population projections produced by Eurostat.
Every 2 years, Eurostat must publish a report on the quality and comparability of the data it receives from EU countries. The first report covers the population and the last report covers vital events. The report must also cover the first reference date and the year ending in 2027.
In order to ensure the comparability, EU countries must ensure that the data they provide to Eurostat are based on the same reference date or period and refer to the same references.
Data and metadata
The Commission must make available to the European Statistical System System (ESSSS) the data and metadata required under this regulation in accordance with the data exchange standards specified by the Commission. The data must cover:
the number of live births and deaths;
the total number of deaths; and
the average number of legal residents of a specific geographical area (i.e. those who have lived in their place of usual residence for a continuous period of at least 12 months before the reference time; or
those who arrived in the EU country with the intention of staying there for at least one year.
National authorities must ensure the quality of data transmitted and the confidentiality of personal data in the context of this regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It applies from 1 January 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to encourage the development of sustainable development. These include:
a sustainable development strategy (Sustainable Development Strategy of the Union, launched by the European Council in Gothenburg in 2001 and renewed in June 2006);
the European Pillar of Social Rights (EPSR), which aims to improve the living conditions of EU citizens;
a European Union budget for the period 2014-2020.
Annual demographic statistics are fundamental for the study and definition of a wide range of policies, with particular regard to social and economic issues.
For more information, see:
‘Annual population statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* ‘Migration’: the number of people who are legally resident in an EU country and who have the right to live in that country for a given period of time. The term ‘migration‘ is used to describe the movement of people from one EU country to another. It is defined in the annex to the regulation as the total population of EU countries over a given reference period. The reference period is defined as the period between 1 December 2013 and 31 December 2027, with the exception of the reference periods for births, deaths and deaths, which are defined in terms of the NUTS classification (see summary).
*‘Vital events: events that have a direct impact on the population of a country or region.’ These refer to deaths or births that occur in the territory of the country or regions in which a person is resident.

Summary 136:
Labelling of non-textile parts of animal origin on the labelling or marking of textile products containing animal fibres and fibres with antistatic effect (Regulation (EC) No 552/2010)
Labelling and marking of non–textile part of animal origins on the market
Labels and marking on the sale and marketing of textile product containing non–animal origin
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2018/852 — labelling and labelling of textile parts of non‐textile origin
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for labelling, marking and marketing textile products with non-animal origin.
It aims to ensure that consumers can make informed choices when buying textile products.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
wool, cotton, polyamide, polyester, elastomultiester, cotton cordage, rope, cordage and ropes used in mountaineering and water sports;
cotton, wool and polyamide;
polyamide and polylactide used for the production of cordages, ropes and cordages.
The textiles covered by the regulation must be clearly labelled on the label or on the packaging.
Textile products must not contain:
non-animal fibres or fibres that are not fibres intended for use in clothing;
fibres intended to be used in the manufacture of clothing, such as wool, cotton or polyamide.
Decorative fibres used in textiles intended for the making of clothing or for the decoration of clothing.
Cotton, cotton and cotton cordages and ropes falling within the scope of the regulation may not be labelled as such.
Non–animal fibre may also be labelled in accordance with the regulation’s Annex II.
In addition, the regulation sets out methods for determining the nitrogen content of jute and certain animal fibre. These methods are based on the composition of the mixture of the fibre and the percentage of animal fibre in the clean dry specimen. The results of these methods are expressed as a percentage of the dry mass of the specimen.
A number of methods are available for the analysis of these results. These include:
methods by determining nitrogen content;
method by determining the quantity of cellulose and cellulose-containing fibres.
Methods by determining cellulose content.
Method by determining gelatin content. This method is based on:
the percentage of gelatinous fibres in the mixture;
the number of fibres per 100 grams of cotton; and
the quantity of elastomeric fibres (e.g. elastomers) per 100 g of cotton.
Rights and responsibilities of the European Commission
The Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts, subject to the conditions laid down in this regulation, in order to implement the delegated acts.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  The regulation is one of a series of legislative acts adopted by the European Parliament and the Council to ensure uniform rules for the marketing of textiles. The other legislative acts are:
Directive 2010/13/EC on textile labelling (see summary);
Regulation 2010/11/EU on the marking of products made from textile fibres, including textile products intended for clothing and accessories;
Council Directive 2010/12/EC establishing a European Union Agency for Textile Labelling and Labelling of Textiles.
For more information, see:
Textiles (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Non-animal fibre: any part of a fibre other than the fibre itself.
* Cellulose: any fibre other than that of the fibre itself. It may consist of cotton, cotton wool or cotton rope.
** Elastomers: fibres other than cotton. They are fibres which have a non-toxic effect. They may be used to make clothing or accessories.

Summary 137:
European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF) — objectives and structure
European Maritime & Fisheries Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 establishing the European Maritime and fisheries Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EMFF’s objectives and its structure.
KEY POINTS
The EMFF aims to:
support sustainable economic growth, employment, innovation and new technologies in the fishery and aquaculture sectors;
encourage innovative approaches to create growth and jobs, in particular by adding value to fishery products and diversifying the local economy towards new economic activities, including those offered by ‘blue growth’ and the broader maritime sectors.
To achieve these objectives, the EU provides financial support for:
fisheries;
aquaculture;
business start–up;
the implementation of the common fisheries policy (CFP);
the monitoring and enforcement of the CFP.
Funding
The EU provides support for the following activities:
increased fishing capacity;
fishing for sustainable and selective fishing;
development of new fishing vessels;
improvement of fishing infrastructure;
enhancing the capacity of existing fishing vessels.
Support
The European Union provides financial assistance for the development of fishing vessels and for the establishment of new ones.
Fisheries
Support may be granted for the construction of a new fishing vessel or for the renovation of existing vessels. It may also be provided for the improvement of the fishing infrastructure of a fishing port.
Aquaculture
EU support may be given for the increase in production and/or modernisation of existing aquacultural enterprises, or for new ones, provided that the development is consistent with the multiannual national strategic plan for the sustainable development of aquaquulture.
Sustainable economic growth
Support can be granted to the following sectors:
seafood production;
seaweed production and processing;
industrial fishing.
In addition, the European Union may provide financial support to the establishment, development and operation of research and development centres.
Business start-up
Support for business start-ups may only contribute to the acquisition of the first fishing vessel and to the training of a professional fisherman. The trainee must be accompanied on board by an experienced professional fisherman of at least 50 years of age.
Eligible operating costs
The following costs are considered to be eligible operating costs:
the salary costs of personnel of national administrations;
operational costs of vessels and equipment;
costs of equipment and spare parts.
EU control, inspection and enforcement systems
EU financial support is available to comply with the implementation of a Union control, inspections and enforcement system.
The Commission may adopt delegated acts to adopt implementing acts in accordance with the advisory procedure set out in Article 127(2) of the regulation.
Monitoring and enforcement
EU funds may be used to monitor and enforce the rules of the Common Fisheries Policy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The CFP has been in operation since 2004 and had to become law in the EU countries by 2004.
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Fishery: any fishery in which there is a high risk of overfishing.
ACT

Summary 138:
Investment products for retail investors — key investor information (KIS) and key information document (KICs)
Investments for retail consumers — key information and disclosure document
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2014 on the disclosure of key information for retail investment products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down common standards for the disclosure and disclosure of information to retail investors of investment products.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
investment products designed to provide specific investment solutions tailored to the needs of retail investors;
transferable securities* for collective investment in transferable securities (UCITS);
other investment products that are combined with insurance coverage or can be complex and difficult to understand.
EU countries must ensure that:
the disclosure of the key information is accurate, fair, clear and not misleading;
it is clearly expressed and written in language and a style that facilitates the understanding of the information, in particular, in language that is clear, succinct and comprehensible.
The disclosure must:
be presented and laid out in a way that is easy to read, using characters of readable size;
focus on key information that retail investors need;
be clearly expressed, written in a language and in a format that is clearly understandable to them.
An explanatory statement must appear directly underneath the title of the disclosure document. This statement must read: ‘This document provides you with key information about this investment product.’
It must be accompanied by:
a description of the product’s features;
a summary of the products’ risks and their risks;
an explanation of how the product is designed to be managed.
a list of the investment products covered by the regulation.
the key information must be provided by the competent authorities of the EU country in which the transaction takes place.
In addition, the regulation sets out rules for the marketing of PRIIPs under an existing passport, if any. The regulation does not apply to products that have not yet been established in the EU and which are subject to a transitional period of 5 years after its entry into force.
It also applies to the sale of products which are not covered by a passport.

KEY TERMS
* Investment product: a product that provides investment solutions designed to meet the needs and objectives of a retail investor.
* Transferable securities: a financial instrument that is intended to be traded on a regulated market.

Summary 139:
Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM) — rules for the Single Supervisory Mechanism and the Single Resolution Fund (SRSM)
Single resolution Mechanism — rules governing the single resolution fund (SSM)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1024/2013 on the establishment of a single resolution mechanism for European banks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a single European resolution mechanism (SMS) for the European Union (EU).
It aims to provide a single, centralised resolution fund for all banks in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The SSM is the EU’s main resolution mechanism. It is designed to ensure that the resolution of failing banks is as simple as possible.
The European Central Bank (ECB) is responsible for supervising the SSM. It has the power to impose fines and periodic penalty payments on any institution or group that fails to comply with its rules. It also has the powers to authorise national authorities to exercise the power of write-down or convert relevant capital instruments.
EU countries are required to guarantee the assets and liabilities of a failing bank or group of banks. This guarantee must be sufficiently remunerated by the entity. It must also be guaranteed by the EU country in which the entity is located.
When providing a guarantee, EU countries must ensure that:
the guarantee is not less than 50% of the total assets of the entity;
the amount of the guarantee does not exceed €1.5 billion;
it is not more than €5 billion.
A single-point-of-entry or multiple-point of-entry resolution is possible, provided that the entity does not have a significant exposure to non-EU countries.
Resolution planning is carried out by the Board of Governors of the European Banking Supervisory System (ESSS). The Board must draw up a resolution plan for each of the participating EU countries. This plan must include:
a description of the assets, liabilities and assets that must be written down or converted into equity;
a list of the entities to be wound up or liquidated;
procedures to be followed when a bank fails to meet its financial obligations;
implementation of the resolution plan.
In addition, the Board must:
ensure that the ECB or national competent authorities inform the board of any measures that they require an institution or a group to take or that they take themselves pursuant to Article 16 of Regulation (EU), Article 27(1) or Article 28 or 29 of Directive 2014/59/EU, or to Article 104 of Directive 2013/36/EU.
It must also inform the European Commission of any decision imposing a fine and/or periodic penalty payment under Article 38 or 39 of the Regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 19 August 2014.
BACKGROUND

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions: assessment and management of aircraft noise at EU airports (Regulation (EU) 2016/852)
Noise - management of airport noise
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on aircraft noise management
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the assessment and control of noise at airports in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure the effective functioning of the EU’s air transport systems and the protection of the environment.
It repeals and repeals Directive 2002/30/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to aircraft such as military aircraft and aircraft undertaking customs, police and fire-fighting operations. It does not apply to:
military aircraft;
police and firefighting operations;
aircraft undertaking customs and police duties;
commercial aircraft.
EU countries must take into account the following factors:
the health and safety of local residents living in the vicinity of the airport;
environmental sustainability, including interdependencies between noise and emissions;
any direct, indirect or catalytic employment and economic effects.
Airport authorities must:
assess the impact of noise on safety, airport capacity and competition;
ensure that noise-related measures are taken to ameliorate the noise impact over the same period;
take due account of the impact on the environment and the economic impact on local residents.
The European Commission must notify the relevant authority if it finds that the process followed for the introduction of a noise restriction does not meet the requirements of the regulation.
noise assessments must be based on objective and measurable criteria common to all EU countries and should build on existing information available, such as information arising from the implementation of Directive 2001/49/EC on environmental noise (see summary).
The Commission must publish the results of these assessments and the measures taken to improve the noise situation at airports.
Aircraft operators and airports must be responsible for the management of the noise they generate at their airports. The Commission must report to the European Parliament and to the Council on the application of this regulation, including the number of aircraft and airports subject to noise restrictions.
In addition, the Commission may, on its own initiative, review the process for introducing an operating restriction within 3 months of the day on which it receives notice under paragraph 1, to review the reasons for their selection.
Where appropriate, EU countries may use cost-benefit analyses where appropriate to determine whether additional measures are necessary to reduce or eliminate the noise.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
 
It applies from 13 June 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 14 June 2021.
BACKGROUND
  
The EU has adopted a number of rules on noise management, including:
Directive 2001/50/EC — the EU Aviation Safety Agency — the rules on airport noise management;
Council Regulation (EC) No 852/2004 — the general rules on air traffic management and on noise assessment;
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/13/EU — the regulation on airport charges.
For more information, see:
‘Airport noise management’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Noise: the soundness of the air in which an aircraft is taking off or landing. It is the noise emitted by the aircraft as a result of the engine, propeller, propellers, brakes, brakes and exhaust systems of the aircraft, as well as from the aircraft itself.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еганска гранка (OJ L 183, 14.7.2016, pp. 1-17)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2016 /852 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  ARTICLE
 			
Council Decision 2014-13/Euratom of 14 June 2014 on the establishment of a European Union Air Traffic Management Agency (ETMA) (Official Journal L 183 of 14.6.2014)
Commission Delegated Regulation 2014/14/EU of 13 June 2014 establishing a European Commission Air Transport Management Agency and repealing Council Regulation (EEC) 2002/29/EC of 25 June 2002 relating to the assessment, management of environmental noise at European airports and repealing Commission Regulation (2002/31/EC).
Communication from the Commission to European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions — Air traffic management at European Union airports (COM(2014) 596 final of 14 May 2014).

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency proceedings: rules for the administration of cross-border proceedings (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Cross-Border insolvencies: rules on the administration and supervision of transnational proceedings (Commission Regulation)
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EC) No 552/2017 — rules on cross-Border Insolvency Proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the application of certain of the EU’s rules governing the cross‑border administration of inter-border judicial proceedings.
It sets out rules on:
the application of the European Union (EU’) law on the insolvent resolution of insolvent companies;
the coordination of proceedings between insolvent parties;
cooperation and communication between the parties involved in the proceedings;
access to information via the system of interconnection.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
cases where a debtor or a group of companies is subject to proceedings in different EU countries;
cases involving more than one debtor;
other proceedings where the debtor is a single person.
EU countries must ensure that:
they appoint a single insolventcy practitioner for several proceedings concerning the same debtor or for different members of a group;
they ensure that this is compatible with the rules applicable to each of the proceedings, in particular with any requirements concerning the qualification and licensing of that practitioner.
They must also ensure that the competent authorities have the necessary resources to carry out their tasks.
the regulation does not apply to proceedings that concern:
insurance undertakings;
credit institutions;
investment firms and other firms, institutions and undertakings to the extent that they are covered by Directive 2001/24/EC;
collective investment undertakings.
In addition, the regulation sets out the procedures for:
adoption of restructuring plans under national law;
procedures for the establishment of a restructuring plan;
open of group coordination proceedings; and
the establishment of secondary proceedings. In addition, EU countries may designate the specific procedure which should be relevant in this context.
The European Commission must present a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the implementation of the regulation by 27 June 2022.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It applies from 27 June 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 26 June 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has set the objective of establishing an area of freedom, security and justice. To achieve this, it has adopted a number of measures, including:
rules on the recognition and recognition of insolvenic practitioners;
rules for the exchange of information and information exchange;
a system of information exchange between EU countries.
For more information, see:
‘Transnational proceedings’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 142:
Limitation of liability for damage caused to passengers in the event of a maritime accident (Athens Convention on Insurance)
Limit of liability in the case of damage caused on board ships of Class A and Class B vessels
Limitations of liability on the liability of ship-owners and insurance companies in cases of passenger death or personal injury (Athhens Convention)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2006 on the limitation of liability under the Athens Convention relating to the carriage of passengers and their luggage by sea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on liability rules for passengers involved in maritime accidents.
It aims to ensure that EU countries take the necessary measures to ensure a high level of compensation for passengers.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all domestic sea-going voyages. It does not apply to:
passenger carriage by sea within a single EU country under Article 4 of Directive 98/18/EC;
carriage by sea between EU countries on board a ship of class A;
the carriage of goods by sea under Article 3 of Directive 1998/8/EC.
EU countries may apply this regulation to all international sea-passenger voyages, including:
sea-going journeys between the EU and non-EU countries;
international voyages;
sea voyages between the European Economic Area (EEA) and the United Kingdom (1) and Iceland, Norway, Liechtenstein, Switzerland, Norway and Iceland;
voyages to and from the United States, Iceland and Norway.
international sea voyages to, from, or within, the EU.
The insurance arrangements required under the regulation must take account of the financial means of the insurance companies. Insurance must be provided for in accordance with the convention’s rules.
Insurance must cover:
death or injury to a passenger caused by any of the risks referred to in paragraph 2.2 of the convention;
damage caused by ionising radiations from or contamination by radioactivity from nuclear fuel or from any nuclear waste or from the combustion of nuclear fuel;
any chemical, biological, bio chemical, or electromagnetic weapon.
Claimants must be entitled to compensation of at least €21 000 for each death or injury caused by the following risks:
the death of a passenger;
injuries caused by:
electromagnetic interference;
nuclear radiation;
radioactivity;
electrical interference.
In addition, the regulation sets out the liability limits for the insurance and other financial security provided for under the convention. The liability limits are set out in the annex to the regulation. Insurance companies are liable only for their part of the total amount of account in respect of each passenger on each distinct occasion, or 340 million units of account overall per ship. The burden of proving fault or neglect lies with the claimant.
Where a passenger dies or is seriously injured, the compensation must not be less than EUR 21 000. If the claimant fails to prove fault or negligence, the claimant must be liable for the full amount of the compensation.
If a passenger is killed or seriously injured on board an EU-registered vessel, the liability must be at least equal to that of the carrier or the performing carrier. The claimant must prove that the negligence was caused by negligence or fault by the negligence of the insurer or the carrier itself.
An EU country may choose to defer application of this regulation until 4 years after the date of its application. It may also decide to apply it until the date on which the insurance or other security is established. If appropriate, the place of business of the insurers or the guarantor must be indicated.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2007. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
The Athens Convention (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еганска градка  барка (OJ L 183, 1.1.2007, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC No 553/2006 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 143:
Framework for the management of freight corridors in the EU (Regulation (EC) No 561/2010)
Framework Regulation (EC), No 551/2010
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules on the management and allocation of railway infrastructure capacity for international freight trains running on freight corridors.
It aims to promote the development of the EU’s internal rail market.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
the establishment of a freight corridor;
the management of the infrastructure capacity on such corridors; and
the levying of charges for the use of rail infrastructure.
Establishment of the freight corridor
The European Union (EU) Member States are responsible for the establishment and management of a rail freight corridor. The establishment of the corridor must be proposed by the Member States concerned. It must be supported financially within the framework of the TEN-T, research and Marco Polo programmes, and other EU policies and funds, such as the European Regional Development Fund or the Cohesion Fund.
The Member States must establish the corridor at the latest 2 years after the decision of the Commission to establish it. The Commission must consult the Committee of the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the establishment or modification of the railway infrastructure located on their territory.
A management board is set up to manage the corridor. It is composed of infrastructure managers from the different Member States. The management board may be an independent legal entity. It may also issue own-initiative opinions. The executive board defines the framework for the allocation of infrastructure capacity.
Management of the corridors is managed by the management board.
An application for infrastructure capacity must be submitted to the European Commission within 60 days of the decision to establish a corridor. This time limit does not exceed 60 days.
If the Commission considers that an application is not satisfactory, it may impose a fee on the applicant. The level of this fee must be appropriate, dissuasive and effective.
One-stop shop for applications
A register must be made freely available to all interested parties. It contains the dates of the requests, names of the applicants, details of documentation supplied and of incidents which have occurred.
Regulatory bodies must be easily accessible to the market players, and able to take decisions independently and efficiently.
When a Member State does not agree on establishing or modifying a rail infrastructure corridor, it must consult one of the other Member States on its territory. If no agreement is reached, it can refer the matter to the regulatory body referred to in Article 20 of the regulation.
Implementation
The Commission must periodically examine the application of this regulation. If it considers that the regulation is not being applied effectively, it will adopt a regulation to amend it.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of implementing acts on railway infrastructure. These include:
Directive 2001/14/EC establishing the European Union Railways Agency (see summary);
Council Directive 91/440/EEC of 29 July 1991 laying down the rules for the development and operation of the Community's railways;
Council Decision 1999/468/EC of 28 June 1999 laying down procedures for the exercise of implementing powers conferred on the Commission, with the exception of the regulatory procedure with scrutiny, which is not applicable.
For more information, see:
Railways (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Freight corridor: a railway infrastructure corridor is defined in the regulation as a railway line which is used exclusively for the transport of international freight.
* Infrastructure management body: the body responsible for managing the infrastructure on such a rail corridor.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  Егранска наганка (OJ L 183, 1.1.2011, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC) no 551(2010) have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 144:
Road transport operators’ professional qualification — common rules for road transport operators
Road transport operator’s professional qualifications
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 — professional qualifications for the occupation of road transport operator
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the professional qualification of road-transport operators in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the quality of the road transport sector and to improve road safety.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all road transport companies operating in the EU. It does not apply to undertakings operating in non-EU countries.
It applies from 4 December 2011.
The rules apply to:
the transport manager or managers designated by the competent authority of the EU country in which the undertaking is based;
the competent authorities of the country in question;
road transport undertakings established in the territory of an EU country.
Examinations and examinations
EU countries must organise examinations to assess the level of professional competence of the transport managers and managers. The examinations must be organised in accordance with Part II of Annex I. They must be held on the first working day of October and published in the Official Journal of the Union (OJ).
The examinations must consist of a written examination and a physical examination. The examination must be carried out by a qualified person with a minimum weighting of marks of 40% and a maximum of 60% of the total number of marks to be given. In addition to the examinations, the competent authorities must also organise training prior to the examination.
the examination must cover the following the completion of the examinations.
If a transport manager is found to be unfit for the job, the undertaking must suspend or withdraw the authorisation to engage in the occupation within the time limits set out in Article 3 of the regulation.
A transport manager must be accompanied by a written declaration on oath or a solemn declaration of fitness. The competent authority must issue a certificate authenticating the declaration.
An undertaking must also provide a certificate of fitness for the position of transport manager. The certificate must be produced within 3 months of the date of issue.
Penalties for infringements of the rules must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. An undertaking must be warned in advance and should have a reasonable period of time to rectify the situation before incurring penalties.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 4 December 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 4 December 2010.
BACKGROUND
Road Transport Regulation (Euratom) (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Road transport operator: a person who:
operates a road transport vehicle;
is responsible for the transport of goods and passengers;
has a role in the management of a transport company.
* Physical examination: the examination is carried out on the basis of a questionnaire and consists of a number of questions. The questionnaire must be completed in a language easily understood by the transport manager and the examination must take place on the premises of the undertaking.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барска гранка   гландра болода

Summary 145:
Recovery of social security benefits for accident at work and occupational disease victims
Recovery and enforcement of Social Security benefits
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 883/2004 on the recovery of benefits for persons who have been injured or ill at work or occupational disease
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that people who have suffered from an accident or an occupational disease are able to recover their benefits.
It sets out rules on how the social security system in the European Union (EU) works.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU citizens who are covered by the following social security schemes:
old-age and survivors’ pensions and invalidity benefits;
cash benefits and sickness benefits in kind provided to the person concerned.
Beneficiaries must be insured in the EU country of their place of residence.
Claims must be submitted to the national social security institution of the country in which they are resident. The institution must immediately arrange for a medical assessment of the person’s incapacity for work and for the certificate to be drawn up.
The applicant party may only make a request for recovery if:
the claim and/or the instrument permitting its enforcement are not contested in its own EU country, except in cases where the second subparagraph of Article 81(2) of the implementing Regulation is applied;
it has applied appropriate recovery procedures available to it on the basis of the instrument referred to in paragraph 1, and the measures taken will not result in the payment in full of the claim;
the period of limitation according to its own legislation has not expired.
If a claim is rejected, the institution must inform the applicant party of the grounds for refusing the request for assistance and the reasons for its refusal.
In the event of a claim being refused, the person may appeal against the rejection. The applicant party must submit a declaration or notification to the competent institution. The declaration must be addressed to the institution.
Where the institution rejects a claim, it must immediately inform the person who made the claim. The person must provide the institution with all the relevant information, such as the name and address of his/her employer, the date of the accident and the duration of the sickness benefit, the amount of the benefit and the reason for rejecting the claim, as well as information on how to appeal against its rejection.
A creditor institution may make a claim for recovery against the institution which initially paid the cash benefits. The creditor institution is responsible for the cost of the claims and the enforcement of its enforcement measures. The claim may be contested in the country where the claim was lodged.
When a creditor institution rejects the claim it must notify the applicant parties of the reasons why. The claimant party must then submit a provisional decision on the provisional decision within 2 months. The decision may be appealed to the European Court of Justice (ECJ) or the Court of Auditors of the EU. The ECJ may also decide to issue a judgment in the case of a dispute between the creditor institution and the claimant parties.
Reimbursement of benefits
The European Commission must ensure that:
beneficiaries are reimbursed for the costs of their actual expenditure;
they are paid on a fixed amount. This fixed amount must be as close as possible to actual expenditure.
Sickness benefits are calculated according to the theoretical amount and the actual amount of benefit in accordance with the basic regulation.
Invalidity benefits and old-age pensions are calculated based on the number of days worked and the average number of weeks worked per year.
Creditors may charge interest on outstanding claims. The amount of interest may be charged by the creditor institutions if the claim is not paid within 18 months of the end of the 18-month period.
EU countries must establish liaison bodies to deal with disputes between social security institutions and to exchange information on administrative procedures.
Evaluation of the regulation
The Commission must assess whether the regulation is being applied correctly. It must also assess whether it is being implemented correctly and whether the rules are being applied proportionately.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2006.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Recovery’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 146:
Statistics on health and health and safety at work: European statistical system (ESS)
Statistics on work and health (ES)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 establishing a European Community statistical system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) statistical system for the collection of statistics on health status and determinants.
It aims to provide comparable data and information, with appropriate indicators, in order to monitor Community actions in the fields of public health, public health and occupational safety and health at work.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
health status and health determinants;
health-related health problems and illnesses;
the causes of morbid events leading directly to death or the circumstances of the accident or violence which produced the fatal injury.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the following:
the number of people who have been diagnosed with a disease or injury at work;
number of deaths caused by work-related illness or accidents;
deaths caused by accidents or violence.
Every 5 years, each EU country must provide Eurostat with a report on the quality of the data transmitted.
The Commission must prepare a report evaluating the findings of the pilot studies and/or cost-benefit analyses, including the effects and implications of national specificities, in cooperation with Member States, in the framework of the collaborative networks and other ESS structures.
Quality assessment
Eurostat must ensure that the data are collected in accordance with the ESAW Phase III specifications on a voluntary basis. The quality assessment is carried out every 5 years.
National authorities must ensure the confidentiality of the information they transmit.
References
The European Commission must publish a reference period, intervals and time limits for data provision. The data must be submitted no later than 18 months after the end of the reference year.
In addition, the Commission must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (ESC) on the results of the studies carried out under this regulation. The report must include:
a description of the types of data to be collected;
a summary of the quality assessment results;
an estimate of the relevance of the statistics to the current and potential needs of users.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been developing a European health monitoring system for several years. It also collects data on those areas through other sources.
For more information, see:
European Health Statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Health status: a person’s health status is defined as the number of years in which he or she has lived or is likely to live.
* Work-related sickness: a health problem or illness which can be caused, worsened or jointly caused by working conditions.
ACT
 
  The European Parliament and the Council of 23 October 2008 establishing a Community Statistical System for the Protection of Health and Safety at Work (OJ L 183, 29.10.2008, pp. 1-8)
