Summary 147:
EU countries’ quarterly data on job vacancies (EMU statistical requirements) (European Employment Strategy) (EESS)
EU countries' quarterly job vacancy (EMEU statistical requirements).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2010 — EU countries' data on quarterly job vacancies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal basis for EU countries to send the European Commission (Eurostat) quarterly statistics on the number of vacancies in their labour market.
It sets out the format and technical specifications for the data that EU countries must send to Eurostat.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
units with fewer than 10 employees;
units in which the employer is taking active steps and is prepared to take further steps to find a suitable candidate from outside the enterprise concerned;
the following activities:
public administration and defence; compulsory social security;
education;
human health and social work activities;
arts, entertainments and recreation;
activities of membership organisations, repair of computers and personal and household goods and other personal service activities.
The data must be broken down by economic activity in accordance with the NACE in force at section level. They must be transmitted in a format and within the deadlines set out in the regulation and within transmission deadlines. The Commission may use other sources, such as administrative data, provided that they are appropriate in terms of quality, in order to monitor and analyse the level and structure of labour demand in the EU countries.
Each EU country must submit a report on the results of feasibility studies it has carried out to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the European Statistical Programme Committee on the quality of the data it transmits to the Commission.
Every 3 years, the Commission publishes a report to the Parliament and the Council on the implementation of the regulation. The report must include:
the number of job vacancies in each EU country,
the sources of all data provided and the deadlines for their transmission;
a description of the types of data to be transmitted.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLACE?
From 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Employment Strategy (ESS) is the cornerstone of the European Union’s (EU) economic and social policy. It sets out a number of objectives for the EU, including:
raising the level of labour supply;
improving the competitiveness of the labour market;
reducing unemployment;
increasing the share of women in the labour force.
For more information, see:
EU employment statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Quarterly job vacancy: a paid post that is newly created, unoccupied, or about to become vacant.
* Economic activity: the total number of hours worked in a given quarter.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 555/2010 of 21 December 2010 establishing a legal framework for the transmission of quarterly data covering job vacancies by Member States (OJ L 178, 28.12.2010, pp. 1-8)

Summary 148:
Hybrid fuel cell vehicles: type-approval requirements for hydrogen containers and components
Hybrid fuels: type of approval and testing
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 — type-approved hydrogen containers for use in motor vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the design, manufacture, testing and installation of hydrogen containers* and components* for the use in hydrogen-powered vehicles.
It aims to ensure that these vehicles comply with the requirements laid down in Directive 2007/46/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
hydrogen containers designed to use liquid hydrogen (i.e. containers that can be filled with liquid hydrogen);
components designed for use compressed (gaseous) hydrogen with a nominal working pressure of over 3,0 MPa (3.5 atmospheres).
It does not apply to hydrogen mixtures.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
the containers used in their vehicles meet the requirements set out in Annex II of the regulation;
the components and systems used in such containers are tested and certified according to the requirements contained in Annex III;
they must be kept for a minimum of 10 years.
The European Commission is assisted by the Technical Committee — Motor Vehicles (TCMV) established by Article 40(1) of the Directive.
Penalties for infringements
The penalties are effective, proportionate and dissuasive. They include:
falsifying test results;
making false declarations during an approval procedure or a procedure leading to a recall;
refusal to provide access to information;
use of defeat devices.
FROMENTRY INTO FORCE
From 24 August 2010, EU countries had to notify the Commission of any subsequent amendment affecting them.

Summary 149:
Access to the internal market in natural gas networks: fair conditions for access to the transmission networks and storage networks (Regulation (EC) No 1775/2005) on conditions for accessing the natural gas transmission networks, storage and liquefied natural gas facilities (ENTSO for Gas Regulation)
Assessment of the implementation of the ENTSO Regulation on access conditions for the access of network users to natural gas distribution systems and storage facilities (ETN-2009) (European Network of Transmission System Operators for Gas).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the setting of fair access conditions to the EU’s internal market for natural gas.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the transmission system operators (TSOs);
the natural gas supply and production undertakings (PSUs);
distribution system operators;
the gas storage and LNG facilities (LNG facilities).
It does not apply to the operators of natural gas transport networks.
Transmission system operators must:
ensure that they offer services on a non-discriminatory basis to all network users;
provide both firm and interruptible third-party access services.
They must also:
make public detailed information regarding the services they offer and the relevant conditions applied, together with the technical information necessary to gain effective network access;
make available to the public the information on their network capacity, flows and maintenance, balancing and availability and usage of gas storage;
transfer of primary capacity rights where notified by system users.
make it publicly available for the information they provide;
allow the Commission to adopt a network code.
The Commission must adopt a draft network code for each transmission system operator within 2 months of receiving a public consultation on its implementation. The Commission must then adopt that code within 6 months.
National regulatory authorities must ensure that national regulatory authorities and other national authorities, including the European Network Safety Agency (ENTSO) for Gas, take into account the views and proposals of all relevant parties during the decision-making process.
In addition, the Commission may adopt measures, in accordance with the principle of subsidiarity, as set out in Article 5 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU), to encourage cooperation at regional level in the field of gas transmission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
The ENTSOs for Gas is the body responsible for the coordination of the regulation. It is responsible for ensuring that the regulation is implemented.
For more information, see:
‘Directive 2003/55/EC on common rules for market in gas’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Transmission system operator: a natural gas system operator that has the right to supply or receive natural gas through its transmission network, storage or LNG facility.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU) No 3/2009 of 21 December 2009 establishing a Regulation on the Access Conditions for the Access of Network Users to the Natural Gas Transmission Networks, Storage and Liquefied Natural Gas Facilities (EnTSO-2009 Regulation) (OJ L 283, 28.12.2009, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 150:
Convention on civil law between EU countries and non-EU countries (Civil Justice Regulation)
Civil law Regulation (Civil Law Regulation) (European Union)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on the conclusion of an agreement with a third country on matters of civil law
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a legal framework for cooperation in civil matters between the European Union (EU) and its Member States.
It aims to ensure that the EU has exclusive competence to conclude agreements with third countries relating to areas of civil justice that fall within the scope of Title IV of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all civil law matters covered by Title IV (recognition and enforcement of judgments in civil and commercial matters). It does not apply to matters of jurisdiction, recognition and enforcement.
In order to provide an adequate legal framework to meet specific needs of a given Member State in its relations with a given country, there may also be a manifest need for the re-negotiation of existing agreements.
The European Commission may propose negotiating guidelines or request the inclusion of particular clauses, as referred to in the second subparagraph of the regulation, in order to improve the legal status of the agreement.
Notification to the Commission
The Member State concerned must notify the Commission of ongoing or already announced negotiations with a view to obtaining authorisation to open formal negotiations. The notification must include, as appropriate, a copy of the existing agreement, the draft agreement or the draft proposal, and any other relevant documentation. The Commission must assess whether the negotiated agreement:
meets the condition set out in the regulation;
fulfils the requirement under Article 5(2) of the annex to the regulation concerning the same specific legal issues;
falls within the competence of the Community;
may refuse to authorise the conclusion if the agreement does not meet the conditions laid down in Article 4(2)(b);
does not comply with the confidentiality requirements.
If the Commission considers that the Member State has not complied with the conditions, it must inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee of the reasons for its refusal.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
   
It entered into force on 1 July 2009.
BACKGROUND
Judicial cooperation between Member States and third countries has traditionally been governed by agreements between the EU and its member countries. However, in 2006, the Court of Justice of the European Communities confirmed that the Community has acquired exclusive competence for concluding an international agreement like the Lugano Convention with non-European Union countries.
For more information, see:
‘Civil law’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 151:
Statistics on migration and asylum
Statistics on immigration and asylum and on international protection
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the collection and analysis of Community Statistics in the field of migration
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the harmonisation of EU-wide statistics on migration, asylum and international protection.
It aims to improve the quality of the EU’s statistical information on asylum and migration.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
provide to the European Commission (Eurostat) statistics on the numbers of:
third-country nationals refused entry to the EU country's territory at the external border;
third countries’ nationals found to be illegally present in the EU State's territory under national laws relating to immigration.
These statistics relate to reference periods of one calendar year and must be supplied to Eurostat within 3 months of the end of the reference year.
The statistics must be based on the following data sources:
records of administrative and judicial actions;
registers relating to administrative actions and administrative actions involving a particular sub-group of persons;
censuses;
sample surveys;
other appropriate sources.
Statistics must be disaggregated by age and sex, and by citizenship of the persons concerned.
In addition, EU countries must report to the Commission on the use and probable effects of estimations or other methods of adapting statistics based on national definitions to comply with the harmonised definitions set out in the regulation.
National definitions may be used for the following negotiations on alternative (national) definitions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLYEARS OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force
It applies from 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
In April 2003, the Commission released a Communication to the Council and the European Parliament, setting out an Action Plan for the collection, analysis and dissemination of Community statistics in the area of migration. This included a number of important changes designed to improve completeness and degree of harmonisation.
For more information, see:
‘Migration and asylum statistics’ on Eurostat's website.

Summary 152:
Statutory audit of public-interest entities: transparency and accountability
Statutory auditor and audit firm transparency and accounting standards
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 on statutory audits of public interest entities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that statutory auditors* and audit firms* carrying out statutory audits* are transparent and accountable for their work.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to statutory audits carried out by:
the statutory auditor or audit firm carrying out the statutory audit of a public interest entity;
the audit firms of systemically important financial institutions.
It does not apply to:
statutory auditors or audit firms carrying out non-audit services;
audit firms that have not been designated by a competent authority.
The statutory auditor and the audit firm must:
ensure that the audit report is in clear and unambiguous language;
report to the competent authorities every 3 years on the quality of the audit work they have carried out;
publish their annual activity reports and annual work programmes;
provide a report on the overall results of the quality assurance system on an annual basis.
They must also:
disclosure in good faith to those authorities of any irregularities referred to in the first subparagraph of the regulation;
notify the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EBA) of any breaches of confidentiality and professional secrecy rules.
In addition, they must ensure that:
they:
have a quality control system in place;
are transparent and at least publish an annual activity report and an annual work programme;
have an annual audit report and a quality assurance report;
cooperate with the EBA and the ECB;
operate and cooperate with the ECB and the other competent authorities.
If an audit firm or statutory auditor fails to comply with the regulation’s requirements, it may be subject to sanctions.
EU countries must ensure:
that the statutory auditor* or the audit firms involved in the audit of an EU public interest body is:
registered with the competent authority;
registered in the EU;
engaged with the European College of Auditors (CEAOB) for at least 5 years;
ensured that they have the necessary documents and information to carry out their tasks;
inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of the results of their engagement with a statutory auditor, audit firm, or other audit firm during the 5 years preceding the engagement.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
 
It has applied since 17 June 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 17 June 2012.
BACKGROUND

Delegated Regulation (EU) 2017/852 on statutory audit;
Directive 2006/43/EC on the establishment of the European Systemically Important Financial Institutions (ESIBs);
Commission Decision 2009/856/EC establishing the European Accounting Standards Board.
For more information, see:
‘Public-interest audit’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 153:
Conservation of marine biological resources and management of fisheries targeting them (Common Fisheries Policy) (CFP) (OJ L 183, 31.12.2013, pp. 1-20)
Conservation and management measures for the conservation of marine biological resources and the management of fishing targeting them.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2013 establishing a common fisheries policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common fishing policy for the European Union (EU) in the Baltic Sea, North Sea, Celtic seas, Bay of Biscay and the Iberian Coast, Mediterranean and Black Sea sea basins.
KEY POINTS
The CFP is the EU’s fisheries policy. It aims to conserve and sustainably exploit marine biodiversity and to ensure the sustainable exploitation of these resources.
The regulation applies to:
fishing within the 12 nautical mile (nm) area of the CFP;
fisheries targeting certain species of marine life (e.g. herring, swordfish, porpoises, mussels, oysters, clams);
the fishing of certain migratory species of fish and crustaceans;
the management of certain fisheries targeting these species.
EU countries must:
ensure that the conservation and sustainable exploitation rates are achieved by 2015;
cooperate with each other in the implementation of their national conservation and management programmes;
take the necessary measures to ensure that fishing activities are sustainable;
promote the sustainable development of aquaculture activities;
coordinate the conservation, management and sustainable use of the marine life in their respective waters.
Consult each other EU countries on the implementation and implementation of the regulation.
Each EU country must submit to the Commission a report on the execution of its national data collection programmes and make it publicly available. The Commission then adopts implementing acts to implement this report.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 March 2013.
BACKGROUND
 
The European Commission has adopted a number of implementing acts implementing the common fishery policy. These include:
Implementing acts for the management and conservation of fisheries;
implementing measures to promote the sustainable utilisation of marine biodiversity;
regional cooperation on conservation measures.
For more information, see:
‘The Common Fisheries Policy’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Common fisheries policy: the EU's fisheries policy, which aims to protect, conserve and sustainable exploit the marine ecosystems of its waters. It covers the fishing of fish, shellfish, crustacean species and migratory fish species, including:
marine mammals;
marine fauna and flora;
seabirds and marine invertebrates;
aquaculture; and
marine invertebrate species and fauna.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1323/2013 of 30 March 2013 laying down a common Fisheries Policy for the period 2013-2015.

Summary 154:
Fishery and aquaculture markets — common organisation of the markets in fishery products (CMO)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2013 on the establishment of fishery producer organisations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common system for the organisation and functioning of the EU’s markets in fisheries.
It aims to ensure fair competition in the market for fishery and aquatic products.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
fishery producers;
aquaculture producer organisations;
the European Commission;
other producers’ organisations.
Member States must ensure that:
the regulation is implemented in accordance with the rules of the Common Fisheries Policy (CFP);
the rules are implemented in line with the principles of fair competition;
there is no discrimination between producer organisations and traders;
market prices are set according to a trigger price, based on trends in production and demand and the convergence of market prices.
Fisheries producer organisations must:
prepare an annual report of their activities under the production and marketing plan and submit it to the competent national authorities for approval;
ensure that their annual report is submitted to the Commission for approval.
The Commission must adopt implementing acts concerning the format and procedure for the notification of the notification provided for in Article 25(1) of the regulation.
Producer organisations may pursue other complementary objectives, such as:
promoting the sustainable development of the fishery sector;
protecting consumers’ interests;
raising awareness of the impact of the common fisheries policy;
encouraging the sustainable management of the fisheries sector.
In addition to the above objectives, the regulation sets out a list of categories of products for which producer organisations are responsible for:
seaweed and shellfish;
fishing products of animal origin intended for human consumption;
marine products intended for export to non-EU countries;
fish products intended to be sold on the European Union market (EU market).
The producer organisation responsible for the marketing of these products must prepare a marketing plan for each category of product for which it is responsible. This plan must include:
a description of the products to be marketed;
an annual report on the production, marketing and sales plan;
a list of products to which the producer organisation is responsible and a description of its annual marketing plan.
A producer organisation must also submit its annual report to the European Commission. The Commission must make all such information publically available.
Implementing acts
Each year, the Commission adopts implementing acts specifying the format, procedure and information to be provided by the producer organisations. The regulation also lays down detailed rules for the implementation of Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 establishing a Community control system for ensuring compliance with the EU rules on official controls on fishery origin.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 29 December 2013. Member States had to incorporate it into national law by 29 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
The CFP is an integral part of the European fisheries policy. It aims to achieve its objectives by:
establishing a common market in fisheries;
improving the conditions for fair competition and the application of social standards.
For more information, see:
The Common Market in Fishery and Aquaculture Products (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Trigger price: the price at which a fishery product is traded on the market.
* CMO: the market price set by a producer organisation to determine the trigger price for a product. It is based on a number of factors, including:
trends in production, demand and trends in prices,
the stabilisation of the market prices;
consumers’ incomes and the producers' incomes;
interests of consumers; and
the interests of consumers.

Summary 155:
Settlement systems — securities settlement systems (SPSS)
Settlement system — security settlement systems
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1095/2010 — rules for the establishment, operation and operation of the securities settlement system (CSD)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for establishing, operating and maintaining securities settlement* systems (CSDs) in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that the EU’s securities market infrastructure (SSS) is safe and secure.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all transferable securities issued after 1 January 2023 and from 1 January 2025. It does not apply to securities issued before that date.
The CSD must:
provide services to issuers and other market infrastructure;
ensure that the relevant authorities in the EU country in which the CSD is based have the necessary authority to authorise it to provide services;
be transparent and accountable to the management body for the day-to-day management of that CSD.
It applies to the following services:
transfers of securities between a CSD and another CSD;
the provision of services to an issuer and another market infrastructure.
A CSD may refuse to provide a service if:
the issuer has not complied with the conditions laid down in the regulation;
it has not been authorised by the competent authority of the country of origin; or
it does not comply with the requirements of the regulation.
the CSD has not provided services in another EU country.
In addition, the regulation sets out specific rules on:
access to other CSDs’ systems;
fees’ rights to participate in the exchange of information between CSDs and other SSDs.
EU countries must ensure that CSDs are:
cooperating with the European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) and the European Banking Authority (EBA);
notifying the competent authorities of other EU countries of any non-compliance with the regulation, and
ensuring that the CSDs comply with its rules;
providing services to a non-EU country and to other market infrastructures.
Providers of services must: provide services to the issuer;
notify the authorities of EU countries if they believe that the services they provide are not being provided in a timely manner;
have the right to refuse services if they consider that they are not providing the services in line with the rules laid down by the regulation or that they do not meet the necessary standards;
allow the issuers to use the services of a designated credit institution to settle their securities.
They must also:
not charge fees for the services provided;
set up link arrangements to enable their respective participants to access other securities settlements systems. These link arrangements are subject to specific requirements to enable the access of their participants to other SSD systems.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 18 June 2010. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 18 June 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of rules on the establishment of securities markets, including:
Directive 2014/65/EU on securities markets (see summary);
Regulations (EC) No 909/2014 of the European Parliament and of the Council on securities settlement and implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2014 on the electronic trading of securities and on the harmonisation of the rules on market infrastructure for the settlement of securities;
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/852/EU of 18 June 2014 establishing a European Securities Trading System (ESTS) and repealing Directive 2014/64/EU (See summary).
For more information, see:
‘Securities settlement systems’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 157:
Sustainable urban development: European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) support for urban development and sustainable tourism, culture and natural heritage (SUT)
SUTS
SutS
The European regional development fund supports the sustainable urban development of regions in the European Union (EU).
ACT
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 October 2013 establishing the European Regional development fund (EDF) and repealing Regulation (EC) No 1080/2006.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The ERDF is the main instrument of the EU’s economic and social policy. It provides financial support to regions in order to reduce disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
Scope of support from the ERDF
The main objective of this regulation is to provide financial support for the development of urban areas in the outermost regions. The ERDF supports the urban areas of the most deprived regions and the least developed regions, in particular those which have suffered from severe or permanent social or economic handicaps.
The aim is to contribute to:
the development of sustainable urban areas;
the conversion of declining industrial regions;
sustainable tourism and culture, including the conversion of industrial regions into a tourism-based economy;
economic diversification of the urban economy.
the creation of jobs and growth of sustainable tourism.
In order to achieve this objective, the European territorial features (e.g. sustainable tourism and cultural heritage) must be part of the territorial strategy for specific areas, including:
urban development;
urban renewal and urban renewal;
environmental protection;
transport services and start-up aid for transport services;
operations linked to storage constraints, the excessive size and maintenance of production tools, and the lack of human capital in the local market.
Specific provisions on the treatment of particular territorial features
The regulation sets out specific provisions on:
Sustainable tourism
The managing authority of an urban authority must:
designate a territorial strategy to be implemented by the urban authorities;
define the scope of tasks, to be undertaken by urban authorities, concerning the management of integrated actions for sustainable development. The managing authority must formally record its decision in writing.
Urban development
In addition, the managing authority may:
assess the economic, social and environmental impact of urban development programmes;
establish a programme-specific result indicators, which relate to investment priorities, based on the latest available data and targets set for 2023;
set up a monitoring system to monitor the implementation of the programme;
ensure that programmes are implemented in line with the programme objectives;
draw up an annual report on the progress made by the implementing authorities.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
This regulation entered into force on 31 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU‘s economic policy is based on a number of principles:
timely and equitable development, which is the aim of reducing disparities between regions’ development levels;
promoting sustainable growth and jobs;
creating an environment that is conducive to sustainable development, with a view to reducing social and economic disparities;
supporting the sustainable development of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs).
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable Urban Development’ on the European Commission's website.

Entry into force

Official Journal

Summary 158:
European territorial cooperation goal for the 2014-2020 programming period (ERDF-2020)
Eastern Europe: European territorial cooperation goals for the 2018-2023 period (EU-2020 programme)
European Territorial cooperation goal (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (Euratom) No 552/2013 on the European Regional Development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s European Territorial Cooperation goal (ETC) for the EU-2020.
It aims to contribute to reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reducing the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is to be paid to rural areas, areas affected by industrial transition, and regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
The ERDF is the main instrument for the implementation of the ETC. It is funded by the European Development Fund (EDF), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the Cohesion Fund (Cohesion Fund).
The ETC is designed to:
contribute to the development of regions with low levels of economic and social development;
reduce disparities between regions;
improve the quality of life in the most deprived regions; and
improve regional integration.
The regulation sets out specific investment priorities for certain interregional cooperation programmes to reflect their specific activities.
In order to achieve its ETC goal, the ERDF allocates up to 2.75 % of the global resources available for budgetary commitment from the European Union (EU), the ESF and Cohesion funds, and up to 5% of the total amount allocated for cross-Border and transnational cooperation.
Cooperation programmes must be designed and implemented in line with the objectives set out in Regulation (EC) No 1303/2013 (see summary).
In addition, specific or additional investment priorities must be set out to reflect the specific activities of the participating regions. These priorities are:
transnational cooperation;
cross-border cooperation, including the additional allocation provided for under Article 4(2) of Regulation (EU, ESF, Cohesion and Development Fund Regulation (EEC) 2018/2023;
interregional and regional cooperation, with a total allocation of up to €500 million;
technical assistance (e.g. research and development);
research and development (R&D) for rural areas;
economic development (research and innovation);
education (education, training and training);
employment (employment and training).
A managing authority is designated by the participating EU countries to carry out verifications on the whole programme area. The managing authority must also be responsible for the audit of the programme.
Where an EGTC is designated, verifications must be carried out by or under the responsibility of the managing authority at least for those EU countries and non-EU countries from which there are members participating in the EGTC.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014 and had to become law in the EU countries by 22.4.2014.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘European regional development goal’ on European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Regional development goal: the aim of which is to reduce disparities between regional development levels and to improve the level of economic, social and cultural development in the least developed regions of the EU. This goal is achieved by:
increasing the number of regions covered by regional cooperation programmes;
improving the quality and efficiency of regional cooperation activities;
encouraging regional integration;
promoting the integration of rural areas into the labour market;
enhancing regional integration in the economy;
providing technical assistance for regional cooperation. The aim of this goal is to provide technical assistance to regions with a high level of technical expertise in the fields of information and communication technology (ICT) and information society (ICT).
* Cross-Border cooperation: the goal of which aims to promote cooperation between EU countries’ regions in order to promote the exchange of information, communication and information technologies (ICTs) and to develop the knowledge and skills of the regions concerned.
Interregional or regional cooperation: cooperation between regions with an overall geographical area of more than 1 million km2.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  Еганска народна гранда барка (OJ L 183, 29.6.2013, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (e) No 550/2013 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 160:
Online dispute resolution for consumer disputes (ODR) — EU rules on online dispute resolution (until 9 January 2016)
Online dispute Resolution for consumers (OLR)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 524/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 21 May 2013 establishing a European Union framework for resolving disputes arising from online transactions between consumers and traders
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common EU framework for the online resolution of disputes between traders and consumers over online transactions.
It aims to improve the resolution of online consumer disputes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all disputes arising out-of-court from online purchases. It does not apply to disputes that are initiated by a trader against a consumer.
EU countries must:
ensure that traders have a single point of contact for consumers seeking to resolve online transactions;
provide consumers with a single online complaint form to resolve disputes;
establish a dispute resolution platform for online transactions that have arisen from a trader’s sale of goods or services.
The ODR platform should take the form of an interactive website offering a single entry point of entry to traders seeking to settle disputes out of court. It should be easily accessible for consumers.
ADR entities* must provide a complaint form for consumers to lodge a dispute with a trader. The complaint form must contain:
the name and contact details of the trader;
the number and postal address of the consumer;
a description of the dispute;
an electronic translation function to allow the parties to have the information exchanged through the platform and to resolve the dispute translated, where appropriate.
Each ADR entity must provide at least two ODR advisors to help consumers resolve their disputes. The ODR contact points must also provide their email address so that consumers have a first point of communication with traders.
In addition, EU countries must encourage consumer associations and business associations to provide an electronic link to the website of the platform.
Disputes should be resolved by:
a dispute resolution agent (ADR entity);
a consumer association or business association;
one or more ODR experts.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 9 July 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 9 July 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve consumer protection, including:
Directive 2008/52/EC on the protection of consumers’ rights in the internal market (see summary);
Delegated Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 182/2011 laying down the rules and general principles concerning mechanisms for control by EU countries of the Commission‘s exercise of implementing powers.
For more information, see:
‘Directive on online consumer redress’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 164:
EU rules on the service of judicial and extrajudicial documents in civil and commercial matters (Civil and Commercial Matters Regulation (EC) No 1348/2000)
EU rules governing the service in the Member States of judicial or extrajudicial document in civil or commercial matters
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, No 1349/2008) on the provision of services for the transmission and delivery of judicial documents
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) system for the service and transmission of judicial, extrajudicial and commercial documents.
It sets out rules for the transposition, transmission and service of these documents in EU countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
jurisdiction of the sending and receiving agencies;
transmission of documents;
receipt of documents by receiving agencies.
Transmission and service
A receiving agency must send a receipt to the transmitting agency by the swiftest possible means of transmission using the standard form set out in Annex I.
The transmitting agency must serve the document or have it served, either in accordance with the law of the Member State addressed or by a particular method requested by the sending agency, unless that method is incompatible with the national law of that Member State.
When a document is transmitted from one EU country to another, it must be served by the receiving agency within 7 days of receipt.
If a document has been translated into another EU country’s national law, the sending or receiving agency has the right to refuse to serve it, unless the translation is incompatible.
the sending agency has a right of refusal.
In this case, the sender must inform the addressee of the reasons for its refusal. The sending agency must then inform the sender of the reason for the refusal and the date of service of the document accompanied by the translation.
EU countries are free to use consular or diplomatic channels to forward judicial documents, for the purpose of service, to those agencies which are designated pursuant to Articles 2 or 3 of the regulation. The designation may be renewed every 5 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLACE?
   
It has applied since 13 November 2008.
BACKGROUND
The European Judicial Network in Civil and Commercial matters (EJNICM) is a network of EU countries’ judicial and administrative authorities that facilitates the exchange of information on judicial and other judicial documents between them.
For more information, see:
European Judicial Network (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Judicial document: any judicial or other legal document that has been transmitted from a Member State to another for service there.
* Extrajudicial document: a document in which a person has been convicted of a criminal offence and has been sentenced to a prison term.

Summary 165:
Advance therapy medicinal products — EU rules on their marketing
Advancement in biomedicine — EU laws on their use
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1394/2007 on advanced therapies for human use
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down EU-wide rules on the marketing of advanced therapies* for the treatment of diseases and dysfunctions of the human body.
It applies to any of the following medicinal products:
a gene therapy medicinal product as defined in Part IV of Annex I to Directive 2001/83/EC (see summary);
a somatic cell therapy (SCT) medicinal product;
a tissue engineered product.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
advanced therapy medicines* for use in human beings;
somatic cell therapy medicines;
tissue engineered products;
combined advanced therapy medicines*.
It does not apply to products based on viable cells or tissues.
Authorisation procedure
The European Medicines Agency (EMA) must issue a marketing authorisation for an advanced therapy medicine before it can be marketed in the EU. This authorisation is valid throughout the EU and must be renewed at least every 5 years.
The committee for advanced therapies (Committee for Advanced Therapies) is responsible for the evaluation and monitoring of the products and for their marketing. It is composed of representatives of the European pharmaceutical companies, the European Commission and the European Society for Medicines Manufacturers’ Association (ESMA). It is chaired by a Commission representative. The Commission is assisted by a representative from each EU country. The committee’s remuneration is based on the number of authorised medicinal products it receives from the EU countries.
Risk management
The Commission must ensure that the products covered by the regulation comply with the essential requirements laid down in Annex I of Directive 93/42/EEC. It must also take into account the risks associated with the use of these products.
Advertising and promotion
Products covered by this regulation must:
be accompanied by a marketing information sheet;
have been approved by the Commission;
not contain any substances, such as cellular products*, bio-molecules, bio-materials, bio‐materials or chemical substances, scaffolds or matrices.
They must be accompanied by:
scientific advice;
clinical and clinical data;
pre-authorisation follow-up of efficacy and adverse reactions, and risk management;
marketing information sheet.
EU countries must inform the Commission of the number and type of medicinal products they have authorised and the names of the persons responsible for marketing them.
In addition, the Commission must be consulted by the committee on the evaluation of any other medicinal product which requires specific expertise falling within its area of competence.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Advanced therapy medicines (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Genetically engineered medicinal product: a medicinal product is defined as a product based on a gene, somatic or tissue engineered cell or tissue.
* Cell therapy: a medicine based on cells, tissues or other biological material that has been genetically modified to have a therapeutic effect.
Somatic cell: a medical device which forms part of a combined advanced therapy.
Tissue engineered product: any medical device designed to be used to treat a disease or disorder. It may also contain additional substances.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барганска гранка (OJ L 324, 10.12.2007, pp. 121-122)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, No 1393/2007 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 166:
EU type-approval system for motor vehicles and their trailers (Euro 6)
EU type–approval systems for motor vehicle and its trailers
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2003 — type–approved motor vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the type–approval* and type–conformity* of motor vehicles, their trailers and components.
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has a uniform and harmonised system for the approval and conformity assessment of vehicles.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
vehicles with a mass greater than 75 kg;
trailers weighing less than 100 kg.
Manufacturers’ obligations
The European Commission must:
ensure that vehicles approved under the regulation comply with the following requirements:
the vehicle meets the requirements of the EU’s harmonised fuel consumption and carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions standards;
have been tested and certified in conformity with the relevant EU technical specifications;
notify the competent national authority of the conformity assessment results;
make available to the public, independent operators and authorised dealers and repairers the technical specifications and information required for the fitting of parts or equipment on vehicles;
provide the competent authority of each EU country with information on the type approval and type conformity assessment procedure.
EU countries must ensure that manufacturers comply with these requirements.
National authorities must take all necessary measures to prevent, detect and report any infringements of the regulation. These include:
recall of vehicles that fail to conform with the regulation;
suspension of a vehicle if it is found to be in danger of failing to meet its conformity assessment requirements;
restrictions on the sale or installation of pollution control devices intended to be fitted on vehicles that are not of a type in respect of which a type approval has been granted in compliance with this regulation and its implementing measures;
the withdrawal of type approval if a vehicle is found not to comply with its type conformity assessments.
The competent authority may also impose penalties for infringements. The types of infringements are:
making false declarations during the approval procedures or procedures leading to a recall;
falsifying test results for type approval or in-service conformity;
withholding data or technical specifications which could lead to recall or withdrawal;
use of defeat devices;
refusal to provide access to information.
Implementing measures
The implementing regulation sets out a number of implementing measures, including:
a standardised method of measuring fuel consumption, carbon dioxide emissions and tailpipe emissions of vehicles, which must be used to determine the type of vehicle to be approved for the Euro 6 stage;
a system of conformity assessment for vehicles of the same type, based on a set of technical specifications, for each stage of the certification procedure;
rules on the number of types of vehicles to be authorised to be sold and registered in the EU;
measures to be taken against air pollution by emissions from motor vehicles (e.g. a ban on the placing on sale or putting into service of new vehicles that emit more than 10 tonnes of nitrogen oxide (NOx) and sulphur dioxide (SO2);
rules to prevent the placing into service or putting on the market of vehicles of different types that emit less than 5 tonnes of NOx;
requirements to be met for the installation of exhaust systems and exhaust systems.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 2 January 2003. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a comprehensive EU type approval system, established by Council Directive 70/156/EEC of 6 February 1970 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to type approval of vehicles and trailers.
For more information, see:
Types of vehicles (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* CO2 emissions: the total number of tonnes of CO2 emitted from the exhaust system of a motor vehicle.
* Fuel consumption: the average fuel consumption per 100 kilometres of driving time.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member State
Official Journal
Commission Implementing Decision 2005/55/EC

Summary 167:
EU list of authorised substances for use in foodstuffs
EU list on substances used in and on foods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1334/2008 — common procedure for the authorisation of substances in food for human consumption
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a common EU list of substances that may be used in or on foods.
KEY POINTS
The European Union (EU) regulates the use of a wide range of substances, including food additives, food enzymes and food flavourings, in the production and handling of food. These substances must be authorised by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
The EU’s food safety authorities must:
ensure that the substances on the EU list are not used in food;
assess the safety of the substances before they are placed on the market or used in foods;
identify the risks associated with the presence of a substance on the list.
The procedure for authorising a substance must be transparent and public information-free.
Applicants must provide the European Commission with:
the name and address of the applicant;
a clear description of the substance;
the justification for its use;
information that is relevant to the assessment of the safety;
an analysis method;
where applicable, the analysis method(s);
information concerning risk management.
If the Commission considers that a substance poses a risk to human health or the environment, it must inform the applicant and the reasons for not authorising it.
An application must be sent to the Commission within 9 months of receiving the application. If the Commission receives a valid application, it may decide to extend the period of application for a further 9 months. In such cases, it informs the applicant, the EU countries and the European Parliament, Council and Commission of the extension.
In addition, the Commission must consult the EFSA and the Member States’ organisations operating in the fields covered by its mandate. The Commission must also consult the European Medicines Agency (EMSA) if it considers that the application does not comply with the conditions laid down in each sectoral food law.
When a substance is added to the Community list, it is subject to the same conditions, specifications and restrictions as those applicable to the substances already on it. This means that it is not subject to any additional conditions or restrictions.
EU countries may, where appropriate, amend the non-essential elements of the EU law relating to the addition of a substances to the list and for adding, removing or changing conditions, specification or restrictions associated with its presence on the community list, in accordance with the regulatory procedure with scrutiny referred to in Article 14(4).
Where the Commission requests additional information from applicants on matters relating to risk management, it will determine, together with the applicant*, a period within which that information can be provided.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 20 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The common procedure to authorise substances is the first step in the process of establishing a Community list of controlled substances. It is designed to ensure the transparency and public access to information on the substances covered by the regulation.
For more information, see:
‘The EU list’ on the European Agency for Food Safety and Consumers’ website.
REFERENCES

Summary 168:
EU rules on accreditation, market surveillance, CE marking and controls of products from non-EU countries and the EU’s external trade in medicines
European rules on accredited products and the CE marking
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 339/93 — general principles of the European conformity assessment, accreditation and market surveillance regime for products with the European Community (CE) marking
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the general principles and procedures for the European Union (EU) Member States to apply to the European CE marking.
It aims to ensure that products covered by the regulation are safe and can be sold freely throughout the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the following products:
medical devices;
medical products for human and veterinary use;
motor vehicles;
personal protective equipment;
electrical products;
telecommunications equipment.
Accreditation
Member States must ensure that:
the CE marking is affixed to a product by a body recognised by the European Commission;
the body responsible for the accreditation has the necessary expertise and expertise to carry out its tasks;
accreditation certificates issued before 1 January 2010 remain valid until the date of their expiry, but no later than 31 December 2014.
The Commission may recognise more than one body at a time.
National accreditation bodies are subject to peer evaluation organised by the body recognised under Article 14 of the regulation. They must:
ensure that the products they accredit comply with the regulation;
establish, implement and maintain procedures for monitoring the performance and competence of the personnel involved;
monitor accidents and harm to health which are suspected to have been caused by those products; and
verify that corrective action has been taken;
follow up complaints or reports on issues relating to risks arising in connection with products subject to EU harmonisation legislation;
provide the Commission with information on any corrective action taken.
In addition, the regulation sets out procedures for:
monitoring the safety of products entering the EU market;
assessing conformity assessment results;
conducting market surveillance activities;
ensuring that the Commission has access to the information it needs to conduct market surveillance.
Activities eligible for Community financing include:
research and development programmes;
market surveillance activities.
Organisations that receive Community financing are required to:
conduct market surveillance;
identify, monitor and report to the Commission, the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee any risks to public health and the environment that may arise from the use of the CE mark;
carry out market surveillance and market assessment activities in line with the requirements of EU policies and legislation.
Authorisation of accreditation
The European Commission may authorise a national accreditation body to issue a CE marking if it considers that a product meets the requirements laid down in the regulation and that it complies with its obligations. The body must consult relevant stakeholders. The Commission may also authorise the Commission to issue CE marking certificates to bodies that have been accredited by the Member States.
Consultation procedures
The Member States must establish procedures to ensure the correct implementation of the rules governing the EU CE marking, and to take appropriate action in the event of improper use of it.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The CE marking has been the subject of a number of legislative acts, including:
Decision No 768/2008/EC on a common framework for the marketing of products (Official Journal C 120 of 16.5.2008);
Regulations (EC, EC, EC and EFTA) No 1/2009/EC and (EC), EC, EFTA and EC) No 2/2009 on the harmonisation of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions of Member States relating to the conformity assessment and the market surveillance of products.
For more information, see:
European conformity assessment (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* CE marking: a product with the EU conformity assessment or market surveillance marking. It is used to indicate that the product meets all the essential safety requirements of the EU legislation on the safety and health of human and animal life.
* Electrical equipment: a device that can be used to transmit electrical signals.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 338/93 of 27 October 1993 laying down general principles on the European system of conformity assessments, accredited products for the Community (OJ L 127, 28.10.1993, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC)(1) No 336/93 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 169:
EU-Iceland agreement on the arrest warrant and the surrender of non-EU nationals
EU–Iceland Agreement on the Arrest Warrant and the Surrender of Non-EU Nationals
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the European Union (EU) and its Member States and the Kingdom of Norway and the Republic of Iceland on the European arrest warrant for the purpose of prosecution or the execution of a custodial sentence or detention order
Decision on the signature, on behalf of the EU, and provisional application of the Agreement between the EU and the EU countries and Norway and Iceland
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve, in accordance with the provisions of this agreement, the surrender for prosecution or execution of sentences between, on the one hand, the Member States of the European Community (now the EU) and the European Economic Area (EEA).
The decision concludes the agreement with the European Court of Justice (ECJ).
KEY POINTS
The EU and Iceland agree to:
ensure that:
the arrest warrant is executed within the time limits set out in the agreement;
the requested person is surrendered as soon as possible on a date agreed between the authorities concerned.
The executing judicial authority of the issuing country decides whether the person is to be surrendered.
If the executing country considers that the person has not complied with the conditions of the agreement, it may refuse to execute the warrant.
In the event of multiple requests, the executing the warrant, it must notify the country of transit.
the executing country of the reasons for its decision. The country of origin must notify its decision by the same procedure.
When a person is in transit, the country in which he or she is staying must inform the country concerned of the reason for the decision.
EU countries must ensure that the requested person has the right to legal counsel and to be informed of the consequences of their decision. In addition, the requesting country must guarantee the person’s right to have access to a lawyer in the case of detention.
Where a person has been convicted of a criminal offence, they must be given the opportunity to appeal against the decision to surrender or to be handed over to another EU country.
An arrest warrant must be translated into the official language or one of the official languages of the executing state. The executing country must send a translation of the warrant to the national authorities of the other EU countries. The issuing country must then transmit it to the judicial authorities of those countries. If it is not possible to call on the services of the SIS, the issuing judicial authority may call on Interpol to transmit an arrest warrant. If an unscheduled landing occurs, the EU country must provide the authority designated pursuant to paragraph 2 with the information provided for in paragraph 1.
A person who has been arrested must be informed by the issuing state of the country to which he/she is being transferred. The person requested must be surrendered within 30 days of being handed over. If the execution is delayed, the execution must be carried out within 15 days of receiving the notification. The execution must take place in the country where the person was arrested.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The Agreement entered into force on 1 June 2006.

Summary 170:
Consolidated rules on the carriage of passengers and luggage by rail (Regulation (EC) No 552/2007)
Consolidating rules on carriage of passenger and luggage
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) No 550/2007 — carriage charges for international carriage by rail
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down uniform rules for the carriage charges and carriage conditions for passengers and baggage by rail in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that passengers and their luggage are not disadvantaged when travelling by rail.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
the carriage charge levied by the railway companies for international passenger and baggage carriage;
the general conditions of carriage for carriage; and
the passenger’s right of action in the event of death or personal injury.
The carriage charge is calculated according to the number of passengers. It must not exceed 8 000 units of account. The carriage charge may be waived or stipulated in certain circumstances, such as:
travel time;
availability of tickets, through tickets and reservations;
access to the ticket office;
refunds and compensation for delays;
service quality standards;
customer satisfaction surveys;
disputes between passengers and railway companies.
EU countries must ensure that the carriage charge and the carriage conditions are correctly calculated and applied.
Carriage charges are calculated in accordance with the EU legislation on carriage charges. The regulation does not apply to international passenger carriage.
Liability
The carrier is liable for:
delays;
traffic congestion;
delay of 60 to 119 minutes;
cancellation, late running of trains or missed connections;
missed connections and cancellations;
unavailability of the ticket price for a delay of 120 minutes or more.
In addition, the regulation sets out the liability of the carrier in the case of:
disclosure of lost or stolen luggage and vehicles;
deliberate failure to keep to the timetable;
failure to provide passengers with sufficient information;
non-availability of services for disabled persons and persons with reduced mobility.
Exemptions
The European Commission must report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee every 5 years on the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a high level of consumer protection in the field of transport and the regulation is part of a package of measures designed to improve the quality of transport services in the EU.
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Carriage charge: a charge charged by the rail company for the international carriage of a passenger or a passenger's luggage. It is calculated on the basis of the total number of kilometres covered by the carriage. It does not include:
ticket price;
distance travelled;
number of passengers;
time of departure;
duration of journey;
total number of hours of travel.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Decision 2007/548/EC of 26 September 2007 concerning carriage charges, carriage conditions and carriage obligations of the rail companies (OJ L 183, 1.1.2008, pp. 1-7)

Statistics on Education and Longevity
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the production of statistics on education, lifelong learning and the participation of adults in lifelong learning
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU-wide rules for the collection and analysis of data on the European Union’s (EU), European Economic Area (EEA) and Member States’ (EU-SILC) education and training systems.
It aims to contribute to the implementation of the Lisbon strategy and to establish comprehensive lifelong learning strategies.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to statistics on:
students’ enrolment, including the characteristics of the students;
entrants;
graduates and graduations;
education expenditure;

class sizes.

participation;
participants’ participation;
experience and learning;
social and economic benefits of education.
The European Commission is assisted by the Statistical Programme Committee and the European Statistical System Committee (ESC).

National authorities must take account of the capacities of the EU countries’ statistical capacities.

Summary 172:
Statistics on energy consumption in the European Union (EU) (2008-2020) (Energy Statistics Regulation)
Energy statistics in the EU (2008 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on energy statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and transmission of EU-wide energy statistics.
It aims to improve the quality and comparability of the EU’s energy statistics and to ensure that they are comparable.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
energy supply and consumption;
energy demand;
transmission of energy statistics to the European Commission and the European Parliament and the Council;
the energy statistics collected by the European Statistical Programme Committee.
EU countries must submit their national energy statistics every 3 years to the Commission. These statistics cover:
the total energy consumption of households and the total consumption of energy-related products and services;
total consumption of electricity and electricity-related services.
The Commission transmits the energy statistics on a quarterly, monthly and short-term basis to the EU countries. The statistics are transmitted with the following frequencies:
annual;
monthly; and
short-term monthly.
Reporting
The energy statistics must be collected in the form of an annual report and a monthly report. They must include:
total energy consumption (total consumption and total consumption);
energy consumption (energy-related product and service consumption); and
energy production (energy production and services).
energy statistics must include the total energy consumed by households and households (energy consumption and services);
total electricity consumption (electricity, gas, oil, aviation, heating, heating and cooling, transport, etc.).
The European Commission must transmit the data to the national statistical authorities in each EU country by means of a standardised transmission system. The transmission system is based on the transmission of data by telephone, fax or electronic means. It is designed to allow the Commission to collect the information it needs for the purposes of its work.
In addition, the Commission may, on the request of an EU country, request exemptions or derogations from the regulation for those parts of the national statistics for which the collection would lead to an excessive burden on respondents.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
It entered into force on 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
The Energy Statistics Regulation (Euratom) is one of a series of legislative acts designed to improve and harmonise the quality of EU energy statistics, in order to improve comparability between EU countries’ energy consumption and their energy consumption.
For more information, see:
EU energy statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Energy consumption: the total amount of energy used by households, households and businesses.
* Electricity consumption: total consumption, households’ total consumption and business activities.
Electricity production: total electricity consumption, including: electricity generated, electricity supplied, electricity generated and electricity consumed, electricity consumed by businesses, electricity produced and consumed, and electricity generated by households.
Transportation: total number of kilometres of road, rail, inland waterway, inland sea, inland air and inland waterways.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 173:
Passengers’ rights on the road — EU rules on the carriage of passengers by bus and coach
Passengers' rights onthe road — European rules on passenger transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 2006/2004 — the rights of passengers travelling by bus or coach
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that passengers who travel by public transport in the European Union (EU) enjoy the same level of protection as other passengers.
It sets out the rights and obligations of the passenger in relation to the transport contract.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all passengers, irrespective of their age, disability, age or any other factor. It does not apply to:
people with reduced mobility;
passengers who are not covered by Directive 90/314/EEC (see summary);
travelers who do not have the right to travel on a package tour.
Passenger rights
EU countries must:
ensure that:
all passengers are entitled to a minimum level of passenger protection, including:
reasonable funeral expenses, including reasonable funeral expenses;
personal injury, including loss of or damage to luggage due to accidents arising out of the use of the bus;
compensation for death or personal injury;
adequate accommodation, accommodation, food, clothes and transport;
accessibility and information for passengers;
free of charge for disabled persons and persons who are in wheelchairs and other mobility equipment or assistive devices.
Carriers and terminal managing bodies must provide assistance free of charge to disabled passengers and persons with a reduced mobility, at least to the extent specified in the regulation’s annexes.
National enforcement bodies are responsible for ensuring that passengers are provided with the necessary assistance.
The European Commission must report to the European Parliament and the Council by 2 March 2016 on the operation and effects of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 2 March 2006. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 April 2007.
BACKGROUND

For more information, see:
‘Passenger protection’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Passenger: a person who is the weaker party in the transport transport contract, who is not entitled to the full benefits of the contract, such as:
the right to a regular service;
traveler: any person who has a disability or reduced mobility.

Statistics of aquaculture
Statistics of aquatic organisms
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 852/2008 on the submission of EU fisheries statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the collection and compilation of EU-wide fisheries statistics.
It aims to contribute to the development and management of the EU’s fisheries sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope

fisheries, including aquacultural production;
aquaculture, including production from hatcheries and nurseries offered for sale;
capture-based and production of molluscs and aquatic plants;
fishing areas for statistical purposes.
EU countries must submit to the European Commission, the European Statistical System Office (ESO) and the Standing Committee for Agricultural Statistics the following data:
total production by volume in terms of fish and aquatic species;
total annual production by weight of aquatic plants.
The data must be collected in the form of surveys, surveys and surveys. The Commission must submit the data to the ESO and the standing committee for agricultural statistics in accordance with Regulation (EU) No 1198/2006 on the European Fisheries Fund (see summary).
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation. It is assisted by the European Agricultural Statistics Committee (EASC).
It must submit a methodological report every 3 years on the results of the data collected. The report must include:
the methods used for collecting, compiling and compiling the data;
a description of the sources for each type of data collected;
the number of surveys and the methods used to collect the data.
Annex I and II of the regulation specify the types of data to be collected, including the methods of sampling, sampling and surveys;
annex III specifies the methods to be used to gather the data for each category of data, including:
participation of individuals in surveys and interviews;
methods of collecting, collecting and compiling data; and
the format for the methodological report.

BACKGROUND

EU fisheries statistics (European Commission).

* Fishing areas: fishing areas in which fish are caught and processed.

Summary 175:
Convention Area for the Conservation and Management of Fishery Resources in the Mediterranean (Cotonou Convention)
Convention area for the conservation and management of fishery resources
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on the implementation of the Convention Area Regulation on the conservation, management and sustainable management of fisheries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a European Union (EU) framework for multilateral cooperation on the management and conservation of fisheries within the Mediterranean Sea Convention Area.
It aims to promote cooperation between EU countries and non-EU countries in the area defined by the Convention.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
fishing vessels that are authorised to fish in the convention area;
non-concerning parties that are not fishing in that area.
EU countries must:
ensure that non-conforming parties to the convention do not take part in transshipments and joint fishing operations;
assign inspectors to the scheme to carry out surveillance and inspection (NEAFC inspectors);
encourage cooperation between non-contributing parties to ensure that their vessels comply with the regulation’s rules.
The regulations set out the following measures:
the number of inspections to be carried out;
the frequency of inspections based on fleet size, taking into account the time spent by fishing vessels in the region;
reporting on the quantities held on board when entering the area and when transhipment and transshipment;
all catches taken inside the area; and
the quantity of fish on board.
stowage and transhipments.
Monitoring and inspection
EU fishing vessels must send a sighting report to the flag state of the vessel concerned, to the Commission or the body designated by it and to the NEAFC Secretary. The report must include the following information:
number of fishing vessels registered in the EU;
number and quantity of catches taken;
quantities of fishing gear and equipment on board;
transshipments;
total number of vessels involved in tranships;
exceptions to the regulation.
Non-contacting parties must report on their fishing activities to the European Commission. The data contained in such reports must be accessible to the commission on request.
Inspections
The European Commission must carry out inspections to ensure the proper functioning of the scheme. It must transmit a copy of each sighting report for every vessel without delay, by electronic transmission in the form set out in accordance with Article 50(2) of the regulation, to its flag state, the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA), the European Fisheries Management Agency (EFSA) and the European Union Agency for Fisheries and Aquaculture (EFMA).
The Commission must also send a report on the number of vessel sighting and inspection reports it receives every year to the EU countries’ fisheries management authorities.
If a vessel is found to have infringed the rules, it must inform the EU country in which it is registered and inform the Commission of the reasons for the infringement. The Commission must then inform the European Economic and Social Committee (EASC) and, where appropriate, the Council of the EU.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 May 2009.
BACKGROUND
The Convention on the Conservation, Management and Sustainable Management of Fishes (CCMSA) is a multilateral agreement between the European Community and its Member States. It aims to ensure sustainable and rational management of fishing resources in the marine environment.
For more information, see:
Fishery conservation: Convention Area (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Non-concontinating party: a party that is not fishing within the convention.
* Transshipment operation: the unloading of all or any fishery products on board a fishing vessel onto another fishing vessel.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барска: Regulation (EC, No 1223/2009 of the European Parliament and of the Council establishing a framework for the implementation, on the basis of Article 1(1) of Article 2(3) of Council Regulation (EEC, Euratom) No 652/2009, of the Agreement on the Convention of the Parties to the Convention on Conservation and sustainable Management of Fisheries (OJ L 127, 29.5.2009, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euram) No 2012/2009 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
